Received

    First published on 17th June 2019AbstractIn recent years, nanocrystals of metal sulfide materials have attracted scientific research interest for renewable energy applications due to the abundant choice of materials with easily tunable electronic, optical, physical and chemical properties. Metal sulfides are semiconducting compounds where sulfur is an anion associated with a metal cation; and the metal ions may be in mono-, bi- or multi-form. The diverse range of available metal sulfide materials offers a unique platform to construct a large number of potential materials that demonstrate exotic chemical, physical and electronic phenomena and novel functional properties and applications. To fully exploit the potential of these fascinating materials, scalable methods for the preparation of low-cost metal sulfides, heterostructures, and hybrids of high quality must be developed. This comprehensive review indicates approaches for the controlled fabrication of metal sulfides and subsequently delivers an overview of recent progress in tuning the chemical, physical, optical and nano- and micro-structural properties of metal sulfide nanocrystals using a range of material fabrication methods. For hydrogen energy production, three major approaches are discussed in detail: electrocatalytic hydrogen generation, powder photocatalytic hydrogen generation and photoelectrochemical water splitting. A variety of strategies such as structural tuning, composition control, doping, hybrid structures, heterostructures, defect control, temperature effects and porosity effects on metal sulfide nanocrystals are discussed and how they are exploited to enhance performance and develop future energy materials. From this literature survey, energy conversion currently relies on a limited range of metal sulfides and their composites, and several metal sulfides are immature in terms of their dissolution, photocorrosion and long-term durability in electrolytes during water splitting. Future research directions for innovative metal sulfides should be closely allied to energy and environmental issues, along with their advanced characterization, and developing new classes of metal sulfide materials with well-defined fabrication methods.

Dr Sundaram Chandrasekaran received his BSc (2004) and MSc degrees (2007) from Madurai Kamaraj University, Tamilnadu, India. He obtained his PhD degree in 2013 from Gandhigram Rural University, India. In 2013, he joined Korea University, South Korea as a Research Professor and later he worked as a Post-doctoral researcher at University of Ulsan, Ulsan, South Korea from 2014 to 2018. Since 2018 until now, he is working as a High Talent Post-Doctoral Research fellow at Shenzhen University, Shenzhen, China. His research interests focus on the synthesis and applications of nanostructured materials, graphene, and other high performance 2D materials, and on the development of new energy materials for electrocatalytic applications such as HER, ORR/OER, photocatalytic, photoelectrochemical water splitting and energy storage.

After a postdoctoral training in Prof. Peixin Zhang's group, she was appointed as an assistant professor in 2018. Her research focuses on solid electrolyte and porous materials for energy conversion and storage.

He obtained a Bachelor's degree from South China University of Technology, a Master's degree from Zhejiang University, and a PhD degree in Materials Science from the University of Manchester where he continued postdoctoral research in carbonaceous materials with Prof. Robert Young. He then established his own research group in Shenzhen University in 2015. His main research interests are electrochemical energy conversion and storage including photocatalysis, electrocatalysis and batteries, particularly focusing on pyrolytic carbons and metal oxides.

Peixin Zhang is a professor in Chemistry at Shenzhen University, China. His research areas focus on energy storage and conversion including batteries, electrocatalytic and photocatalytic hydrogen evolution and solar cells, through nano-structured transition metal oxides and compounds. He has published over 300 papers and owns 20 patents.
1. Introduction
As a result of its potentially cost-effective approach and ecofriendly nature, the utilization of solar energy for splitting of water into hydrogen (H2) and oxygen (O2) using nanostructured electrocatalytic and/or photocatalytic materials has attracted research interest. To date, numerous types of catalytic and/or photocatalytic semiconducting materials have been examined; this includes metal oxides,1–6 metal nitrides and oxy-nitrides,7–10 metal sulfides,11–13 alkali metal base,7,14 Z-scheme systems,15–19 and organic (carbon, graphene etc.) materials.20–23 Among them, metal oxides, metal sulfides and their composite forms have received more attention. Notably, many metal oxides exhibit a wide band gap, since their valence band comprises a deep 2p oxygen orbital, and hole carriers have a comparatively heavy effective mass owing to the localized nature of the oxygen 2p state.24–26 Hence, metal sulfides have received attention over the past decades due to their suitable electronic band gap, band position, exposed active sites and their promising catalytic and photocatalytic activity. In contrast to metal oxides, metal sulfides often have a shallow valence band24,27,28 and due to their small effective mass, they exhibit a robust quantum size effect.29–31 In addition, due to their availability in a variety of shapes, sizes, crystalline forms, chemical compositions and their excellent photon (light) response, metal sulfide materials have a range of potential novel properties which can contribute to harvesting the complete solar light spectrum. To create such a high performance photocatalytic system, photocatalytic materials with an ability to harvest visible-light and electrons and/or holes with excellent mobilities for promoting effective charge separation are necessary.32–35 To date, a variety of metal sulfides and their nano-scale composites have shown a substantial enhancement in electrical conductivity, surface morphology, long-term durability, and often a metallic luster, which are beneficial for efficient energy conversion and storage applications.36–43
There are a number of excellent reviews on metal sulfide materials;44–52 however they have an emphasis on either one or a limited number of metal sulfide systems for energy conversion and storage applications. This review will focus on exploiting hydrogen energy evolution using these materials via three novel energy conversion systems, namely (i) electrochemical, (ii) photocatalytic (iii) photoelectrochemical (PEC) applications and beyond are summarized; as outlined in Scheme 1. The intention of this comprehensive review is not to simply summarise the hydrogen energy production mechanisms, as these have been covered in detail elsewhere; for instance see ref. 44–52. This review is the first to deliver an in-depth overview and knowledge on the variety of synthetic strategies for forming metal sulfides and their structural, chemical, and physical property modification. It will also describe how energy harvesting metal sulfide materials and strategies has been utlized to achieve effective hydrogen generation. Finally, the critical challenges that need to be addressed in future research activities are discussed, with an aim to initiate new efforts in this emerging research area.
 Scheme 1  Utilization of metal sulfides for renewable energy applications. 
2. Chemical structures of metal sulfides
Sulfide is the simplest inorganic sulfur anion with the chemical formula S2−, or a compound containing one or more S2− ions. Metal sulfides are compounds in which the sulfur anion is combined with a metal or semi-metal cation or cations to form MxSy giving the mono-metal sulfides of MS, M2S, M3S4 and MS2 stoichiometries.53 In a similar approach, the formation of bi-metal sulfides leads to A1−xBxSy, where x and y are integers.4,54 To date, several hundreds of metal sulfide materials have been recognized and they are presented in Tables 1 and 2. Many of the metal sulfides are structurally simple and exhibit high symmetry. In addition, they have excellent physical and chemical properties, which are coupled with a high specific gravity and low hardness. Table 3 highlights the sulfur source materials used to prepare metal sulfide materials. Importantly, the metal and sulfur atomic arrangements in metal sulfides are combinations of a close-packed system, engaged by ionic size and charge,55–57 where notable points are: (i) the most symmetrical and important structural type is the simple sodium chloride (NaCl) structure, where each ion occupies a position within an octahedron containing six oppositely charged nearest neighbors (e.g. PbS; galena). If the crystal structure contains two sulfide ions in each of the octahedral positions in the NaCl structure, the structure is a high-symmetric pyrite, (ii) the next structural type is sphalerite (ZnS), where each metal ion is surrounded by six oppositely charged ions arranged tetrahedrally, and (iii) the third important structural type is the fluorite form, where the metal cation is surrounded by eight anions; every anion, in turn, is surrounded by four metal cations (e.g. CaF2). Conversely, if the atomic positions are reversed, and the metal cation is surrounded by four anions and each anion is surrounded by eight metal cations, the anti-fluorite structure is formed (mostly alkali metal sulfides; Li2S, Na2S, K2S and Rb2S etc.).

Typically, as shown in Fig. 1a, the bonding of the transition metal sulfides is covalent, which increases the semiconducting properties, but some have metallic properties.58–61 The covalent effect of sulfur allows sulfur–sulfur (S–S) bonds and the assimilation of S2 pairs in some metal sulfides, such as pyrite. A number of sulfides, including MoS2, WS2 and CuS, also have layered structures. Moreover, a number of exceptional metal sulfide varieties such as CdIn2S4,62 LiTi2S463 and CuCr2S464 have the cubic spinel structure. The common formula for the spinel group is AB2S4, where A denotes a divalent metal ion such as Mg, Fe, Ni, Mn and/or Zn. Typically, cations with lesser radii favor the occupation of A sites, habitually, the quad valent (also called as tetravalent) lead ion can also occupy this site. Generally, the tetrahedral (A-site) interstices are smaller than the octahedral (B-site) interstices.54 B denotes trivalent metal ions such as Al, Fe, Cr and/or Mn, Ti. Sulfur (S) usually adopts a 2− oxidation state and to sustain valence symmetry, notably, the A cation can be in the 2+ or 4+ oxidation state and the consistent B cation can be in 3+ or 2+ oxidation state.4 These structural possibilities can be stated as A2+B23+X42− and A4+B22+X42−.
 Fig. 1  (a) Calculated relative covalent contribution to the metal–sulfur bond strength for each transition metal (inspired from ref. 61) and (b) comparative energy band diagram of metal oxides and metal sulfides, whereas CB is conduction band and VB is valence band. Schematic of (c) hydrogen evolution reaction (HER), whereas, GC is glassy carbon and (d) photocatalytic water splitting mechanism, whereas, Eg is the band gap. 
3. Comparison and classification of metal sulfides
To date, several metal sulfides and metal sulfide nanocomposite materials have been reported for energy conversion and storage. As shown in Table 1, several hundred sulfide materials can act as efficient electrocatalysts and photocatalysts for hydrogen production. In brief, most of the sulfide photocatalysts are comprised of d10 configured metal cations since the metal sulfide catalysts conduction bands are composed of d and sp orbitals, while the valence bands consist of S 3p orbitals, which are much more negative than O 2p orbitals, as shown in Fig. 1b. This offers sufficient negative conduction band positions to reduce H2O to hydrogen and oxygen. In addition, the narrow band gap provides a good response to the complete solar spectrum compared to oxide materials.24,65–68 As shown in Fig. 1(c and d), this reaction produces H2 at room temperature but does not consume fossil fuels and does not emit CO2.
3.1 Comparison of metal sulfides to metal oxides
Metal sulfides differ from metal oxides in their chemical composition, and the metal sulfides are attractive catalytic and photocatalyst materials for electrocatalytic hydrogen generation, PEC water splitting, environmental remediation, batteries, and sensors.12,24,28,69–73 Metal sulfide nanostructures possess exceptional qualities including high photosensitivity, large specific capacities, low redox potential, and a long lifetime compared to metal oxides. Furthermore, due to the high theoretical volume expansion calculated using traditional conversion reaction formula, several metal sulfides, such as SnS2 and MoS2,37,74–78 have a layered structure which can help alleviate strain induced by a volume expansion. Metal sulfides provide high selectivity and sensitivity to specific classes of molecules at a working temperature that is lower than classical metal oxides such as TiO2,79 Fe2O3,80 WO381 and CuO.82,83 An additional feature of metal sulfides, which differentiates them from metal oxides, is their lower melting points. Therefore, the focus on metal sulfide materials, where sulfur atoms replace oxygen, can provide a route to sensitive, selective, and stable photo-sensing materials for energy conversion applications. However, metal-oxide materials continue to retain a prominent role due to their high sensitivity, selectivity, low-cost, small dimensions and simple integration.
3.2 Classification of nanostructured materials
Recently, several hundreds of nanostructured materials have been prepared by a variety of routes; and there is a need for their structural classification.84,85 Initially, Gleiter86 proposed the classification of nanostructured materials and Skorokhod85 subsequently elucidated these structures. However, Gleiter and Skorokhod's schemes were not fully considered since 0D, 1D, 2D, and 3D structures such as fullerenes, nanotubes, and nanoflowers were not taken into consideration. Later, a modified structural catalog for nanomaterials was proposed by Pokropivny and Skorokhod,87 in which 0D, 1D, 2D and 3D structures were incorporated, and their schemes are shown in Fig. 2.
 Fig. 2  Structural classification of nanomaterials, based on ref. 87. 
4. Synthesis of nanostructured metal sulfides
The large-scale synthesis of metal sulfides and their derivatives with well-defined shape, size, layered structure, and defect states is essential, which is the initial step towards achieving high-performance materials. To date, numerous methods have been developed to prepare high-quality metal sulfides, including mono-metal sulfides, bi-metal sulfides, multi-metal sulfides, doped and heterostructures. Therefore, a strong correlation between the nanostructures and their functional properties has encouraged research for controlled synthesis of innovative energy materials with tailored nanostructures. The existing synthesis methodologies of metal sulfides include physical and chemical methods. However, for easy classification we have separated them into two techniques based on ‘top-down’ and ‘bottom-up’ fabrication methods, see Fig. 3, on the basis of common manufacturing methods.88–92
 Fig. 3  Schematic of commonly used synthesis methods. 
4.1 Top-down approaches
Typically, a top-down approach refers to slicing or successive reduction of a bulk material to achieve nano-sized particles.

4.1.1 Sputtering. 
The fabrication of large areas of metal sulfide in a uniform thin film is the ultimate aim for commercial level device manufacture. Sputtering is a rapid physical vapor deposition technique for the fabrication of thin films that consist of nanoparticles. For the preparation of polycrystalline monolayer metal sulfide nanostructures, magnetron sputtering deposition has proven to be better than chemical vapour deposition (CVD) due to its ease of fabrication, low-cost, rapid high production and scalability.93–97 During synthesis, argon (Ar) ions are ionized by collisions with electrons which initially bombard the target materials. Then, the sputtering particles from the target will deposit on the substrate to form a thin film. Recently, transferable and continuous centimeter sized polycrystalline WS2 monolayer films were fabricated98 on the surface of a quartz substrate using WS2 precursor film and sulfur powder, as shown in Fig. 4. As a result, a WS2 thin film with a thickness of ∼25.2 nm exhibited an amorphous nature and after post-annealing its crystallinity increased with diffraction peaks observed at 14.31°, 29.02° and 43.74°, which were indexed to the (002), (004) and (006) crystal planes of hexagonal WS2, as shown in Fig. 4. This confirmed the fine layer stacking along the crystallographic c-axis. In addition, monolayer or few-layer MoS2 thin films have been prepared at high temperatures on a variety of substrates using magnetron sputtering.99 Mo atoms react with vaporized S atoms to form a MoS2 thin film. Meanwhile, the MoS2 film grown on sapphire exhibited narrower and stronger Raman peaks compared to that on SiO2, and was reported to be of improved crystallinity. In addition, to obtain Cu3BiS3 thin films, a metal precursor of Cu–Bi was sputtered onto fused silica substrates (50 mm × 50 mm) in a hydrogen sulfide (H2S) environment.100 The thin films of Cu3BiS3 were highly dependent on the ratio of copper (Cu), bismuth (Bi) and H2S. Several other metalsulfide thin films, including CuInS2,101,102 PbS,103 In2S3,104 and FeS2105,106 have also been formed.
 Fig. 4  (a) Illustration of the annealing method for WS2 films (b) three-dimensional atomic structure diagram of monolayer WS2 (c) XRD pattern of WS2 films on the quartz substrate before and after annealing (d) photograph of quartz and WS2 on quartz before and after annealing and (e) optical image of WS2 film on SiO2/Si substrate. Reproduced from ref. 98 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2016. 


4.1.2 Electrospinning. 
Electrospinning is known as an effective approach for fabrication of nanofibers using a high voltage and a fluid flow jet that solidifies into nanofibers on the substrate. The fabricated nanofibers often achieves a large surface area to volume ratio and high porosity, which provides flexibility in terms of surface functionalities, superior mechanical performance and self-standing structures with various levels of doping, which can facilitate their application as electrodes in energy conversion and storage.38,107,108 To date, a variety of fiber materials such as polymers,109,110 carbon materials,111–113 metal oxides,114–116 metal sulfides117–119 and their composites120–123 have been successfully prepared. The morphology and structures of nanofibers are controlled by varying the metal salt precursors and the ratio of chemical salts. With this approach, Zn0.76Co0.24S nanoclusters with N-/S-doped carbon overlayer embedded in the interconnected 1D carbon fibers were prepared by Yin et al.124via electrospinning combined with metal organic framework template, as shown in Fig. 5(a–g). Polyacrylonitrile (PAN) encapsulated Zn0.76Co0.24S@C encapsulated (Zn0.76Co0.24S@C@PAN) nanofibers was prepared using PAN, N,N-dimethylformamide (DMF) and Zn0.76Co0.24S@C. The prepared 1D conductive nanofibers provided an excellent stable electrode structure, and an efficient transport route for electrons, as well as rapid ion diffusion. Recently, Zhong et al.125 found that 3D carbon nanofiber/tin(IV) sulfide (CNF@SnS2) core/sheath fibers can be used as a membrane for photocatalytic reactions. Initially, PAN nanofiber membranes were prepared by electrospinning using precursors of PAN and DMF. The PAN membranes were then carbonized at 500 °C for 2 h and (CNF@SnS2) core/sheath fiber membranes with different SnS2 loadings were fabricated, as shown in Fig. 5(h–n). Similarly, as shown in Fig. 5(o–s), Jagadale et al.126 introduced a combined method of electrospinning and electrodeposition to fabricate CNF@CoNi2S4 porous electrodes with a highly conducting nature.
 Fig. 5  (a–g) Electrospinning preparation process and microscopic images of nanofibers of Zn0.76Co0.24S@C@CNF. Reproduced from ref. 124 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018. (h) Schematic of the fabrication of CNF@SnS2 and SEM images of concentration varied fibers (i and j) CNF@SnS2-1, (k and l) CNF@SnS2-2, and (m and n) CNF@SnS2-3 membranes, whereas SnCl4·5H2O amount was 145, 290, and 580 mg respectively. Reproduced from ref. 125 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2016. (o) CNF@CoNi2S4 electrode fabrication process and (p–s) their corresponding microscopic images. Reproduced from ref. 126 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2018. 


4.1.3 Lithography. 
Lithography is a versatile strategy toward the fabrication of nanostructured metal sulfides.127–129 The technique has been used to prepare the metal sulfide structures with precision over large areas on a sub-10 nm scale without the need for etching, lift-off or film transfer stages.130 Saifullah et al.131 used electron beam nanolithography with a single source molecular resist to pattern arbitrarily sized and shaped functional ZnS nanostructures, as shown in Fig. 6. In order to enhance the catalytic active sites, Roxlo et al.132 prepared surface defected MoS2 by lithography. As a result, the highly exposed edge surfaces in these layered MoS2 single crystals provided an enhanced catalytic activity. Typically, nanostructured metal sulfide layers have been fabricated by lithographic patterning of self-assembled monolayers or functional surface molecule layers on the substrate through selective film growth or metal sulfide deposition via molecular printing or electron-beam lithography (EBL) techniques.133–135 These approaches are useful to manufacture patterned metal sulfide nanostructures in a small area within a reasonable time scale.
 Fig. 6  Scanning electron microscopic images of (a and b) 8 nm and (c and d) 6 nm ZnS lines about 200 and 50 nm square grids, respectively; (e and f) 10 nm ZnS dots with pitches of 25 and 22 nm, respectively. Reproduced from ref. 131 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2017. 


4.1.4 Ball milling. 
In this process, powder particles are exposed to severe mechanical deformation through collisions with stainless steel vessels and balls and become continually deformed, cold welded, and fractured, so that solid-state reactions and/or mechano-chemical reactions in powder blends can be produced. This low-cost and easy scaleable mechano-chemical route136 has been used for fabricating uniform 5 nm sized CuS quantum dots (QDs), as shown in Fig. 7(a and b). The CuS QDs exhibited a high surface area with the existence of dislocations and planar defects such as twins and stacking faults,136 which are beneficial for electrocatalytic and photocatalytic performance. In addition, several nickel sulfide powders of Ni3S2, Ni7S6, NixS6, and Ni3S4 have been prepared using NiS powder by ball milling.137 As shown in Fig. 7(c–e), a highly active electrocatalyst of MoS2 was prepared by Ambrosi et al.138 using MoS2 flakes. The ball-milling process enhanced the electrochemical and electrocatalytic performance of MoS2. In addition, CuCrS2 and NiCr2S4 were also synthesized by mechanical alloying using ball milling technique, as shown in Fig. 7(f and g), and materials were sintered and heat treated to enhance electrical conductivity.139
 Fig. 7  (a) Schematic of (a) synthesis and (b) XRD pattern of CuS QDs photocatalysts by ball milling method. Reproduced from ref. 136 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2016. (c) Schematic of the ball-milling process fabricating a range of small MoS2 flakes of different sizes and thickness starting from a large natural MoS2 grain and the electron micrograph for natural MoS2 starting material (d) and after the milling process (e). Reproduced from ref. 138 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2015. Crystal structure of (f) NiCr2S4 and (g) CuCrS2 with Ni and Cu atoms (open circles) partially filling octahedral and tetrahedral holes, respectively, between edge-sharing CrS6 octahedra (blue). Reproduced from ref. 139 with permission from Springer, copyright 2014. 


4.1.5 Exfoliation. 
Several methods of preparing 2D nanosheets of metal sulfides have been prepared for metal sulfides. This technique is similar to graphene exfoliation, simple, low-cost and provides the potential to fabricate high quality 2D metal sulfide materials.
4.1.5.1 Mechanical exfoliation. 
Using this method, MoS2 flakes can be produced on a substrate simply using adhesive tape (‘sticky’ tape). Typically the bulk materials of MoS2 or WS2 were used as the starting material, where parts are peeled off with adhesive tape and pushed into the substrate surface.27,140–143 On release of the tape, a quantity of metal sulfide stays with the substrate, rather than the tape, due to the van der Waals force to the substrate surface. Repeating the process can produce flakes of WS2 or MoS2 with random sizes, shapes and number of layers.144,145 Similarly, Mo1−xWxS2 nanosheets have been obtained on a silica substrate by mechanical exfoliation,146 where a single crystal of Mo1−xWxS2 was used as a starting material. The disadvantage of this method is that the as-exfoliated metal sulfide flakes have to be transferred to a new substrate for further use; it is also a low yield process.
4.1.5.2 Liquid phase exfoliation. 
Liquid phase exfoliation starts from a bulk metal sulfides materials and can manufacture flakes with different shapes, sizes and number of layers.147–150 The yield is considerably higher compared to mechanical exfoliatration, but is typically of lower quality. There are two ways to exfoliate metal sulfides in solution. The first is exfoliation by a mechanical approach such as shearing, sonication, grinding, stirring, and bubbling.147,150–154 Yang et al.155 used starting materials of cadmium acetate, zinc acetate, thioacetamide, selenium, indium chloride and sodium molybdate dehydrate to prepare ZnIn2S4 layers, ZnIn2S4/MoSe2, CdIn2S4, In2S3 layers and hetero-layered CdIn2S4/MoSe2 and In2S3/MoSe2 metal sulfides for hydrogen generation applications. These layers were obtained by a facile low-temperature refluxing method followed by a modest exfoliation, as shown in Fig. 8(a–e), where the precursors of Zn(CH3COO)2·2H2O, InCl3 and thioacetamide were used to produce ZnIn2S4 layers. Initially this precursor solution was prepared in distilled water, which was followed by heating to 95 °C for 5 h. All other materials were prepared by a similar method. For the formation of ZnIn2S4/MoSe2 heterostructures, the negatively charged ZnIn2S4 layers and positively charged MoSe2 promoted surface charge for self-assembly that was driven by strong electrostatic attraction during synthesis. In this fabrication method, some chemical reactions may produce metal sulfide flakes.156–158 For example, surfactants (e.g. chitosan, sodium cholate) may be used in the reaction solution to prevent recombination of exfoliated flakes. In some cases, electrolysis was also employed to generate bubbles to incorporate into the metal sulfide interfaces and exfoliate them. In addition, nanocomposites of MoS2–polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP)159 were formed by sonication of MoS2 powder in ethanol with PVP. Compared to tape-assisted mechanical exfoliation, the final quantity of the samples was improved using this method.
 Fig. 8  (a) Exfoliation of clean and freestanding single-unit-cell ZnIn2S4 layers in surfactant/intercalator-free medium (b) scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image of bulk ZnIn2S4. Scale bar, 2 μm (c) transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image of single-unit-cell ZnIn2S4 layers. Scale bar, 50 nm (d) atomic force microscopy (AFM) image and (e) corresponding height images of single-unit-cell ZnIn2S4 layers. Scale bar, 200 nm. The inset in b is picture of Tyndall effect of the ZnIn2S4 suspension; insets in c are the resultant selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern and high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image of single-unit-cell ZnIn2S4 layers. Reproduced from ref. 155 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2017. (f) The way of producing monolayers of metal sulfides using lithihilation and (g) exfoliation of nanosheets from 2H to 1T MoS2. Reproduced from ref. 158 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2017. 
A second route is ionic intercalation, where lithium ions are typically used to intercalate between the metal sulfide layers and enlarge the interlayer spacing,160 as shown in Fig. 8(f and g). As an example, Matte et al.161 introduced an ion intercalation method to produce monolayer MoS2 and WS2. Initially, under a nitrogen-filled glove box, MoS2 and/or WS2 powder was mixed with n-butyllithium and hexane, and the precursor was then maintained at 373 K for 72 h. Then, the intercalated samples were sonicated using deionized water under vigorous agitation. It was reported that a violent reaction between the lithium ion intercalated between the layers and water could produce a large amount of hydrogen to expand the layers, and subsequently mono- or few-layer metal sulfides were formed. However, the ion intercalation method is difficult to control and achieve a high yield for single-layered materials.

4.2 Bottom-up strategies
Generally, the bottom-up method refers to the build-up of a nanomaterial atom by atom, molecule by molecule or cluster by cluster. They are now described below.

4.2.1 Chemical vapor deposition (CVD). 
Among the various synthesis techniques, CVD is well suited to existing semiconductor technologies. Normally, this system forms a thin film on a substrate, where chemical reactions are involved in the thin film growth mechanism. For large area MoS2 fabrication, most current research begins with solid state precursors of molybdenum, such as molybdenum trioxide (MoO3) or metal of Mo.162 During this fabrication method, if the precursors of Mo were not vaporized during the process the technique is also called a sulfurization technique where H2S or vaporized S is the sulfur source. It was reported that by using MoCl5 precursors it is easy to achieve monolayers of MoS2 of large area.144,163 Characteristic chemical reactions taking place during the CVD process are: MoO3 + 2H2S + H2 = MoS2 + 3H2O(1) 8MoO3 + 24H2S = 8MoS2 + 24H2O + S8(2) 16MoO3 + 7S8 = 16MoS2 + 24SO2(3)
The usual growth temperatures for CVD are ∼700–1000 °C, and the addition of a metal catalyst, such as Au, is encouraged to attain high quality films.164,165 Recently, the temperature was lowered to 150–300 °C using a plasma-enhanced CVD (PECVD) technique, thereby making it possible to deposit on plastic and flexible substrates.166,167
Metal–organic CVD (MOCVD) is a special case, where organometallic precursors are used and modern semiconductor industries are preparing single crystal epitaxial films by this method. Recently, this method has been used in the fabrication of MoS2 thin films.93,165,168 Using MOCVD, both MoS2 and WS2 have been fabricated on 4 inch silicon wafers,93 where precursors of molybdenum hexacarbonyl (MHC), tungsten hexacarbonyl (THC), and diethyl sulfide (DES) were used. The addition of H2 gas enhanced the decomposition of diethyl sulfide, and to produce monolayer metal sulfides of only three atoms thick, as shown in Fig. 9. This MOCVD approach for the synthesis of 2D semiconductors undoubtedly opens a new pathway towards creating future robust nano-scale devices based on these materials. The greatest benefit of this method is that precursors of ultrapure gaseous are injected into the reactor chamber to deposit a thin layer of atoms onto the substrate (i.e. atomic layer levels). In addition, if a liquid or solid precursor is used for synthesis, it is vaporized in the MOCVD chamber which improves the quality of films and allows control of the final product. It is well known that Mo1−xWxS2 is a promising 2D ternary system and its monolayer behavior strongly depend on the atomic ratio of Mo/W.169,170 However, at 500 °C MoO3 is partially reduced by sulfur vapor to form volatile MoO3−x, thus MoS2 can be formed at temperatures as low as 530 °C.171 However, WO3 or MoO3−x requires a higher temperature of ∼1000 °C for vaporization.172,173 Thus the preparation of MoO3−xWxS2 monolayers is difficult; although heterostructures of MoS2/WS2 are possible.170 Although monolayer and multi-layered MoO3−xWxS2 metal sulfides were achieved by atomic layer deposition (ALD)174 and other methods,175–177 monolithic MoO3−xWxS2 ternary metal sulfide monolayers with tunable configurations and band gaps have not been reported by conventional CVD methods and are worthy of investigation.
 Fig. 9  (a) Diagram of MOCVD growth setup, precursors was red, Mo or W atom; yellow, S atom; white, carbonyl or ethyl ligand (b) grain size dissimilarity of monolayer MoS2 subject on the hydrogen flow rate; from left to right, 5 standard cm3 min−1 (sccm) (SEM image shown), 20 sccm (SEM) and 200 sccm (TEM). False-colour DF-TEM image showing a continuous monolayer MoS2 film. Scale bar, 1 μm; below images are ADF-STEM image. Scale bar is 1 nm. Monolayer MoS2 (c) and WS2 (d) films developed on 4 inch fused silica substrates, with drawings of their respective atomic structures. The left halves display the bare fused silica substrate for comparison, (e) photo of patterned monolayer MoS2 film on a SiO2/Si wafer (the darker areas are covered by MoS2), (f) optical absorption spectra of grown monolayer MoS2 (red line) and WS2 (orange line) films in the photon energy range from ∼1.6 to 2.7 eV. (g) SEM image and photoluminescence (PL) image (bottom inset, at 1.9 eV) of monolayer (ML) MoS2 membranes suspended over a SiN TEM grid with 2 μm holes (a diagram of the suspended film is shown in the top inset). Scale bar, 10 μm. Optical images (normalized to the bare substrate region) of patterned monolayer MoS2 (h) and WS2 (i) on SiO2, taken from the wafer-scale patterned films. Inserts show photoluminescence images at 1.9 eV (h) and 2.0 eV (i). Scale bars, 10 μm. Reproduced from ref. 93 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2015. 


4.2.2 Atomic layer deposition (ALD). 
ALD is a method capable of fabricating a variety of thin films from the vapor phase. This method is a subclass of the CVD technique and the films are grown by exposing the substrate surface to alternating precursors of gas molecules.41,178,179 As an alternative to CVD, ALD is associated with non-overlapping sequential pulses. During each pulse, molecules react with the substrate surface in a self-limiting mode. Therefore, it is a promising approach to produce materials with layer-by-layer precision. Recently, ALD has been used to grow monolayer and few-layer MoS2 on SiO2/Si or other substrates180–182 using precursors of vaporized MoCl5 and H2S. The thickness and composition of monolayer to few-layer MoS2 or WS2 or other sulfide materials can be adjusted by regulating the film deposition time and pulsing fraction.
ALD of FeSx prepared from bis(N,N′-di-tert-butylacetamidinato)iron(II) and H2S on a silica substrate using a home-built ALD reactor has been recently reported.183 However, compared to the wealth of literature on metal oxide fabrication by ALD, only a limited number of metal sulfide materials have been studied to date, including AlS,184 CuxS,185 CoS,186 BaS,187 CdS,188,189 CaS,190 SrS,191 In2S3,192 PbS,193 and heterostructures such as ZnS/SnS2/Cu2S/Si194 and Cu2ZnSnS4.195 Finally, in order to enhance the quality of the films formed by ALD, several samples were annealed in a sulfur environment, such as H2S or S atmospheres.


4.2.3 Spray pyrolysis. 
Spray pyrolysis is a rapid and relatively low-cost technique, which has been widely used to produce large area metal sulfide thin films, as shown in Fig. 10. Using this technique, metal sulfides which include ZnS,196,197 SnS,198 CdS,199 CuS,200 CdZnS201 and CuInS2,202,203 and their composites, were fabricated at moderate temperatures below 400 °C with good adhesion to the substrate. In particular, ways to tailor the thin film quality include the growth temperature, the molar ratio between metal (M) precursor and sulfur precursor in the precursor spray solution (i.e. M:S) and the use of post-deposition annealing. Recently, cubic nanocrystalline Co3S4 was prepared by spray pyrolysis using cobalt chloride and thiourea as the precursors.204 A possible reaction scheme is as follows: (4) (5) (6)
 Fig. 10  Common processing strategy for spray pyrolysis. 
Lee et al.205 prepared pristine nickel sulfide and nickel sulfide–rGO composite powders by a one-pot spray pyrolysis from spray solutions without post heat treatment. For nickel sulfide–rGO composites, the colloidal precursor solution contained dispersed graphene oxide with nickel nitrate (Ni(NO3)2) and thiourea ((NH2)2CS) in specific molar ratios. The nickel sulfide–rGO composites exhibited a uniform distribution of nickel sulfide nanocrystals within the highly conductive rGO matrix with well-developed mesopores and structural stability from electrochemical analysis.


4.2.4 Pulsed laser deposition/ablation. 
Pulsed laser deposition (PLD) is a physical vapor deposition (PVD) method. In contrast to CVD, the benefits of using PLD are cost-effectiveness, high growth rate, good control of morphology and thickness, low growth temperature, and its ability to fabricate versatile materials, specifically for those with chemical active surface areas.206,207 In addition, it is relatively easy to fabricate nanocomposites using the PLD method, since it is possible to simultaneously use several target materials inside the PLD chamber. Compared to other PVD methods, such as thermal evaporation and sputtering, the PLD technique has specific features208,209 which include (i) synthesis via a pulsed mode, so that a small amount of material can be grown in microseconds, and (ii) the target has fast and strong heating features, hence stoichiometric growth can be readily attained using PLD. In this aspect, Serrao et al.210 prepared highly crystalline thin films of MoS2 using PLD; MoS2 thin films have been grown on aluminium oxide (Al2O3)(0001), gallium nitride (GaN)(0001) and silicon carbide-6H (SiC-6H)(0001) substrates. One-inch diameter targets were prepared by cold isostatic pressing of MoS2 and sulfur powder with varying Mo to S atomic ratios. The targets were placed 5 cm away from single-side polished Al2O3(0001), GaN(0001), and SiC-6H(0001) substrates. A circular mask and UV transparent lens (focal length of 16.5 cm) were used to focus the beam to a 4 mm2 spot on the target substrate surface. The laser source was a pulsed KrF excimer 248 nm laser operating at a 30 ns pulse width with a replication rate of 4 Hz. The laser energy was ∼50 mJ per pulse and the substrate temperature was 700 °C. The chamber was evacuated to a base pressure of 10−6 Torr and the ablation was 0.1 mTorr. Moreover, PLD was employed to fabricate MoS2 films with sulfur defects, which were the defects created by the bombardment of the energetic large particles generated from the pulsed laser. However, it should be noted that the defects are prominent when the growth temperature was less than 300 °C. Theoretical estimates indicated that sulfur defects in MoS2 can reduce the band gap of few-layer MoS2 from 1.08 eV to 0.08 eV, which makes it applicable for fabricating saturable absorber devices.
In addition, a thin film of Ag coated on a silicon substrate was used to fabricate high quality WS2 films due to appropriate lattice matching of Ag2S formation at the interface.211,212 Bo et al.213 fabricated MoS2 quantum dots by a temporally shaped femto-second laser ablation method, as shown in Fig. 11(a). In order to prepare the MoS2 QD, an amplified titanium sapphire laser system was used to generate linearly polarized laser pulses. Ettlinger et al.214 used laser wavelength varied PLD deposition of copper tin sulfide and SnS-rich copper tin sulfide with a 248 nm KrF excimer laser with a pulse length τ = 20 ns and a 355 nm frequency-tripled Nd:YAG laser at pulse length τ = 6 ns. This comparative study of laser wavelength showed that the copper tin sulfide film growth rate per pulse for a 248 nm laser was three to four times lower than the 355 nm laser, as shown in Fig. 11(b–d).
 Fig. 11  (a) Schematic of temporally shaped femtosecond laser two-sub pulse train ablation of bulk MoS2 targets in water. Reproduced from ref. 213 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2017. (b) Pulsed laser deposition of copper tin sulfide (CTS); whereas Nd:YAG laser operating at 355 nm (third harmonic) with a pulse duration of 5–7 ns and a KrF excimer laser operating at 248 nm with a pulse duration of 20 ns. Deposition rate of CTS (c) and SnS-rich CTS (d), at 355 nm. Reproduced from ref. 214 with permission from Springer, copyright 2016. 


4.2.5 Thermal deposition. 
Thermal evaporation is one of the simplest techniques of PVD. Shimizu et al.215 used thermal evaporation to synthesize gold (Au) mediated hexagonal wurtzite structured zinc sulfide (ZnS) nanostructures including nanobelts, nanosheets, and nanorods, as shown in Fig. 12(a–f). Before deposition, a sapphire(0001) substrate was coated with a 70 nm thick Au film, whereas, ZnS powder was used as a starting material to fabricate a variety of structures. A high-purity inert Ar carrier gas was purged and during deposition the furnace temperature was raised and maintained under a pressure of 350 to 400 Torr. As a result, a highly crystalline ZnS was achieved. In addition, several nanostructures including ZnS nanowire (NW) arrays,216 CdS NW's,217 and ZnS:Ga nanowalls46,216 have been formed. A one-step thermal evaporation synthesis of ZnO–ZnS heterostructure NW arrays was also carried out using ZnS powders,218 as shown in Fig. 12(g and h). Microscopic analysis revealed the ZnO NWs were grown on top of the ZnS NWs through epitaxial growth, and the epitaxial correlation of ZnO and ZnS was (0001)ZnO//(0001)ZnS, (01−10)ZnO//(01−10)ZnS, and (2−1−10)ZnO//(2−1−10)ZnS. Furthermore, ZnS NW arrays were formed by etching ZnO–ZnS NW arrays with KOH solution. Single-crystalline nanowires of CoxZn1−xS with a variety of cobalt doping levels were also prepared by thermal evaporation using different volumes of CoCl2·6H2O powders as the dopant source.219 Huang et al.220 fabricated highly stable single crystalline wurtzite structured ZnO/zinc blende ZnS co-axial heterojunctions. During fabrication, the deposition temperature was responsible for the final crystalline phase of ZnS. Due to its wurtzite structure, the ZnO nanorods have hexagonal cross-sections, as shown in Fig. 12(i–u). In addition, voids were observed to form between the ZnO core and the ZnS shell during the coating process, which may be due to the Kirkendall effect. Moreover, a zinc blende of single crystalline ZnS nanotubes orientated along the [001] direction was obtained by etching ZnO/ZnS heterojunctions in acetic acid. A variety of nanostructures have also been prepared based on mono-metal sulfide221,222 and bi-metal sulfides.223,224
 Fig. 12  (a–e) SAED and microscopic images and (f) XRD patterns of single crystalline ZnS nanobelts and nanosheets. Reproduced from ref. 215 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2004. (g and h) Structure of ZnO–ZnS heterojuction NW arrays. Reproduced from ref. 218 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2009. (i) Atomic models of [001]-oriented zinc blende (ZB) ZnS (002) plane and [0001]-along wurtzite (WZ) ZnO (0002) plane from the dashed square region of (j); down side image, [110]-oriented ZB ZnS (220) plane and [2−1−10]-oriented WZ ZnO (2−1−10) plane from the dashed square region of (j); schematic illustrations of (j) the cross-sectional view and the plan-view of the ZnO/ZnS nanocable. (k and l) TEM and HRTEM (m) images of ZnS tubes, and the inset of (m) is a SAED pattern of the tube; (n) cross-section-view HAADF image and the corresponding EDX elemental mapping of the ZnS tube, scale bar = 100 nm; (o) HRTEM image along [001] orientation of the ZB structure, the inset corresponds to the FFT pattern. (p) TEM image of the obtained ZnO/ZnS core/shell nanorods; (q) corresponding SAED pattern; (r) HRTEM image and (s) atomic model of the core/shell heterojunction showing the interface region between ZnO and ZnS; (t) dark-field (DF) scanning (STEM) mode image as well as elemental mapping of the core/shell nanorod; (u) EDX data of the core/shell nanocable. Reproduced from ref. 220 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2014. 


4.2.6 Combustion. 
The combustion method is a rapid synthesis method that utilizes exothermic reactions to increase the reaction rate; it is also referred to as self-propagating high-temperature synthesis. In this aspect, Mani et al.225 produced CdS by the combustion method. The precursor contained cadmium nitrate tetrahydrate and thiourea. Combustion dehydration was achieved using a preheated furnace at 300 °C, and dissimilar oxidant/fuel (O/F) ratios (1, 2, 3, 4 and 10). Hosny et al.226 prepared CdS, SnS2 and HgS by the self-propagating combustion method. Nanoparticles of metals sulfides were manufactured by combustion of the as-prepared precursors (i.e. M(II) chloride where M = Cd, Sn and Hg and calcium thioacetate for sulfur source) on a hot plate. During the reaction, the decomposition metal thioacetate leads to the formation of CO2 and S2−. The redox reaction between S2− and CO2 is known to be a self-propagating exothermic reaction and the metal sulfide formation reactions are: (7) 2M–S–CH2− + 3O2 → 2H2S + 2CO2 + 2MO(8) 2H2S + CO2 → 2S + C + hν(9) 2MO + 2S + C → 2MS + CO2(10)where M = Cd2+, Sn2+ and Hg2+.
Using a similar approach, CdS nanowires,227 rGO/CdS nanocomposites,228 Mn2+ doped ZnS,229 Mn2+ doped CuS230 nanocrystallites and Zn1−xCdxS nanoparticles231 were also prepared. The combustion technique has a number of benefits, such as the low processing time, high purity of products, low external energy consumption, and high yield of fine particles. However, the application of combustion for the preparation of metal sulfides is not fully explored and is worthy of further examination.


4.2.7 Micro-emulsion. 
Typically, a micro-emulsion system consists of three components232 which include a polar phase (such as water), a non-polar phase (usually a hydrocarbon liquid or oil) and a surfactant. In this technique, two routes are possible.233,234 The first route is a micro-emulsion system, which is diffusion controlled since the trigger/reactant must diffuse over the interfacial wall of the micro-emulsion to encapsulate the first reactant to complete the nanostructure synthesis. The second route is the use of two micro-emulsions, whereby separate reactants are mixed together in appropriate ratios to complete the reactions. Zhang et al.235 prepared large quantities of CuS nanotubes by the micro-emulsion system under low temperature; the as-prepared nanotubes on a modified electrode were used as a glucose sensor. In this micro-emulsion system, oleic acid was used as a core and water as a shell and nanostructure growth was controlled by changing the reaction temperature. In a similar approach, CdS was produced in sodium bis(2-ethylhexyl)sulfosuccinate and Triton reverse micelles with cadmium lauryl sulphate and cadmium sodium bis(2-ethylhexyl)sulfosuccinate as the functional surfactant.236 The particle diameter was dependent on the relative composition of Cd2+ and S2− ions. In addition, the micro-emulsion method has also been used to synthesize semiconducting metal sulfides such as PbS,237 Cu2S,238 and composites of CdS–TiO2,239,240 CdS–ZnS,241–243 CdS–SnO2.244,245


4.2.8 Precipitation. 
Precipitation is the creation of nanostructured materials as a powdered solid from a solution.246,247 Using this technique it is possible to select the reaction conditions where a chemical substance precipitates in a highly pure and easily separable form. The common precipitates used to prepare sulfide materials are KOH, NaOH, ammonia, sodium dodecyl sulfate, NaHCO3, urea and tetramethylammonium hydroxide. In addition to the precipitants, several reductive and oxidative agents are often added to form the final materials.248 Finally, the precipitated materials can be heat treated by calcination or annealing to form metal sulfides. Vacassy et al.246 synthesized ZnS powders in aqueous solution by reacting different zinc salts with S2− anions generated by thermal decomposition of thioacetamide under acidic pH conditions salts. They indicated that nucleation was improved by acetic acid, however restricted particle growth was observed. Vattikuti et al.249 synthesized MoS2 nanorods through a precipitation method using precursors of ammonium heptamolybdate tetrahydrate and ammonium sulfide, where the citric acid was acted as a reductant. In a similar approach, the kinetics of Fe2S precipitation using a constant super saturation solution was explained by Rickard.250 The reaction mechanism for the precipitation reaction between Fe(II) and dissolved sulfide are: M2+ + H2S → MS(solid) + 2H+(11) M2+ + 2HS− → M(HS)2(solid)(12) M(HS)2(solid) → MS(s) + H2S(13)where M = metal ion. During these reactions, the reaction rates are direct functions of dissolved Fe(II) activity. Moreover, the pH effect of copper sulfide nanoparticles during synthesis was investigated by Yadav et al.251 in which copper acetate and sodium thiosulfate was used as a starting material. A solution of ammonia and dilute HCl were used to tune the pH of the precursor. Initially, at a pH = 5.5 CuS nano-particles were formed and the particle size reduced as the pH was increased; the structural variations with pH are shown in Fig. 13. Later, Zhang et al.252 used Cd(CH3COO)2·2H2O and Zn(CH3COO)2·2H2O to prepare Cd1−xZnxS using a precipitation method by slowly adding Na2S into the aqueous reaction solution. Recently, Jiang et al.253 prepared a g-C3N4–CdS photocatalyst using g-C3N4, CdCl2, a Na2S solution and NaOH. During synthesis, the samples with varied g-C3N4 and CdS ratios (1:5, 1:3, 1:2 and 1:1) were prepared. As a result g-C3N4–CdS was prepared with a ratio of 1:3, which exhibited a highly porous nature with enhanced photocatalytic activity. Chen and Ye254 prepared low band gap (1.7 eV) AgIn5S8 metal sulfide using AgNO3 and In(NO3)3 and Na2S; initially a brown Ag–In–S precursor was precipitated by the addition of Na2S aqueous solution. In a similar way, highly active bi-metallic sulfide silver tetrathiotungstate (Ag2WS4) electrocatalysts was prepared by Zhan et al.255
 Fig. 13  Controlling (a and b) structural and (c) optical properties of copper sulfide through pH dependent chemical co-precipitation method. Reproduced from ref. 251 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2017. 


4.2.9 Hydrothermal synthesis. 
The hydrothermal technique is one of the best methods to produce a variety of pure and nanostructured materials with desired properties. This method is widely used to fabricate nanoparticles of metal sulfides, and their composites with control over the structural, chemical, physical and optical features of the particles by changing the properties of near or supercritical solutions using different pressure and temperature conditions.38,256,257 The hydrothermal system typically uses precursors of metal salts to allow a reaction with the sulfide source materials or simply sulfur in a stainless steel autoclave, where a sequence of physicochemical reactions take place under moderate to high temperatures (e.g., ∼80–200 °C). The resultant products are metal sulfide powders of different shapes and sizes. The prepared nano-sized powders are often post-annealed at high-temperature to increase their crystalline quality and purity. Li et al.258 prepared a series of metal sulfide dendrites of PbS, CdS, ZnS, CoS, Cu2S via a facile hydrothermal method using metal acetates and 1-butyl-3-methlyimidazole thiocyanate ([BMIM][SCN]), and during synthesis [BMIM][SCN] acted as both a sulfur source and capping agent. As shown in Fig. 14(a–d), Dong et al.259 prepared hierarchical CoS nanostructures by a facile one-pot simple hydrothermal method. In a typical procedure, Co(NO3)2·6H2O and thiourea were dissolved in deionized water and/or ethanol, then placed into a Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave, followed by a heat temperature. During synthesis thiourea provides two roles. Firstly, it was decomposed to produce S2− for the final formation of CoS. Secondly, it served as a structural directing agent to control CoS crystalline growth. The morphologies of the structures formed were modified by fine-tuning the molar ratio of the reactants and solvents, reaction time, reaction temperature, and ligand type. The formation mechanism of CoS nanostructures during a hydrothermal reaction is closely related to the reaction between Co2+, H2O and SC(NH2)2 and are as follows: SC(NH2)2 + 2H2O → H2S + 2NH3 + CO2(14) Co2+ + NH3 → [Co(NH3)4]2+(15) NH3 + H2O → OH− + NH4+(16) H2S + 2OH− → S2− + 2H2O(17) [Co(NH3)4]2+ + S2− → CoS + NH3(18)
 Fig. 14  FE-SEM images of the CoS products prepared in a 10 mL aqueous solution containing 0.17 mol L−1 Co(NO3)2·6H2O and 0.17 mol L−1 thiourea at 160 °C for a given reaction time: (a) 12 h, (b) 24 h, (c) 36 h, (d) 48 h. Reproduced from ref. 259 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2011. (e) Schematic of the template-assisted formation of hierarchical MoS2 microboxes. I: MnCO3 microcube templates are coated uniformly with MoS2 nanosheets by an L-cysteine assisted hydrothermal method; II: microcube templates are removed by HCl wash to form hierarchical MoS2 microboxes, (f–h; g) and (j; i–k) are FE-SEM and TEM images of MnCO3 microcube and MoS2 microboxes respectively. Reproduced from ref. 262 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2014. (l) High magnification FE-SEM image reflecting the flowerlike pattern of well-arranged rods, higher-magnification image also inserted in the top right corner of (l) (the white scale bar is 50 nm), further indicating the rod structure of the Bi2S3 bundles and (m) TEM image of Bi2S3 bundles. Reproduced from ref. 263 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2006. (n and o) FE-SEM and HR-TEM images of heterostructured CN/C/ZnIn2S4 prepared via hydrothermal method. Reproduced from ref. 265 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2015. 
A flower-like CoS nanostructured growth mechanism involved three stages (i) the formation of nanoflakes, (ii) skeleton sphere formation by attachment of small-sized nanoflakes via an Ostwald ripening mechanism, and (iii) the development of the hierarchical flower-like nanostructures via a dissolution–recrystallization process. Similarly, by using CoCl2·6H2O with citric acid monohydrate (CAM) and ethanolamine (ETA), Xing et al.260 prepared three different morphologies of hierarchical CoS2 nanostructures, where carbon disulfide (CS2) was used as a sulfur source.
MoS2 nanostructures and its reduced graphene oxide (rGO) hybrid composite were produced by Kamila et al.261 The raw materials of ammonium heptamolybdate, polyethylene glycol and thiourea were used as the Mo and S source to prepare 2D nanosheet structured MoS2–rGO hybrids. Due to the structural features of the rGO/MoS2 nanosheets, it exhibited excellent electrochemical HER performance. In addition, uniform MnCO3 microcubes were decorated with nanosheets of MoS2 by a L-cysteine assisted hydrothermal method.262 Through a hydrothermal procedure, the MnCO3 templates were converted to MnS to form MnS@MoS2 microcubes. After selective removal of the MnS microcube templates by HCl washing, hierarchical MoS2 microboxes were obtained, as shown in Fig. 14(e–k). Subsequently, after annealing at high temperature in an inert atmosphere, highly crystalline MoS2 microboxes were obtained. A simple approach was used to fabricate flower-like Bi2S3 metal sulfide263 using L-cysteine, and amino acid, as shown in Fig. 14(l and m). In addition, other Bi2S3 nanostructures such as nanorod bundles, nanoflakes, network-like nanoflakes, and nanorods have been prepared by tailoring the reaction solution pH, the reaction temperature, and the Bi(NO3)3 to L-cysteine ratio. This morphology had a significant effect on the electrochemical HER reaction.
Recently, a two-step hydrothermal method was used to fabricate ZnFe2O4–ZnIn2S4 composites with different mass fractions.264 Initially ZnFe2O4 was prepared using Zn(NO3)2·6H2O and Fe(NO3)3·9H2O in absolute ethanol by a hydrothermal technique at 180 °C for 12 h. The precursor solution was then prepared using ZnFe2O4 in ethylene glycol, and Zn(NO3)2·6H2O, In(NO3)3·4.5H2O and L-cysteine was added in the solution and heated at 150 °C for 12 h. Interestingly, the specific surface areas were improved after the ZnFe2O4 was doped in ZnIn2S4 at mass ratios from 1 to 5 wt%, which may due to the sphere-like structure and reduced crystalline nature of the ZnIn2S4. A nanocomposite of g-C3N4/nanocarbon/ZnIn2S4 (CN/C/ZIS) was prepared by the hydrothermal method.265 The nanocarbon which connects both the CN and ZIS exhibited a low crystalline nature, as shown in Fig. 14(o and p). The nanocarbon can be used as an electron mediator and may enhance the electrical conductivity, which can assist in effective electron hole pair separation and transfer of photogenerated charge carriers.
In order to utilize the full structural features of metal sulfides, several types of conducting substrates including fluorine-doped tin oxide (FTO), indium tin oxide (ITO) coated glass, carbon cloth (CC), graphene and conductive polymers, have been used in hydrothermal method to coat or create metal sulfides. The use of a conductive substrate assists in electron transfer and stabilizes the nanostructures. As shown in Fig. 15(i), a three-dimensional, ternary CdS/ZnIn2S4/TiO2 heterojunction on a FTO substrate was formed via a simple three-step hydrothermal method.266 Initially, titanium butoxide mixed with hydrochloric acid and distilled water precursor was used to create the TiO2 surface on the FTO glass substrate. Secondly, the precursor of ZnSO4·7H2O, InCl3·4H2O, and C2H5NS were used to fabricate the ZnIn2S4 on the TiO2/FTO surface by a similar approach. For ternary heterojunction of CdS/ZnIn2S4/TiO2/FTO fabrication, the cadmium nitrate tetra-hydrate (Cd(NO3)2·4H2O) and thiourea were used. As a result, the prepared heterojunction films were used as a highly efficient, visible light-active photoanodes for PEC water splitting.
 Fig. 15  (i) (a) Fabrication of a ternary CdS/ZnIn2S4/TiO2 heterojunction and (b) XRD of prepared photoelectrodes. Reproduced from ref. 266 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2015. (ii) Crystalline phase transformation of NiSx particles via hydrothermal method using various amounts of precursor source materials and their corresponding XRD patterns. Reproduced from ref. 268 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2014. (iii) Core–shell structure of metal sulfides (Au–ZnS, Au–NiS, Au–Ag2S and Au–CuS) using hydrothermal method. Reproduced from ref. 271 with permission from Wiley-VCH copyright 2009. 
Zhu et al.267 prepared hierarchical CuS ultrafine nanoneedle arrays on a carbon nanotube (CNT) backbone (CNT@CuS). Initially, the functionalized CNTs were coated with a mesoporous silica layer to stimulate the growth of 1D Cu–silicate core–shell nanostructures on CNT@SiO2. Finally, the copper silicate nanoneedles were transformed into CuS nanoneedles through a hydrothermal method with Na2S. In addition, the fabrication of nickel sulfide hierarchical structures was achieved using ethylenediaminetetraacetic acid (EDTA) as a chelating and capping agent using nickel nitrate hexahydrate (Ni(NO3)2·6H2O) and thiourea (CH4N2S) as precursors during hydrothermal treatment.268 The influence of precursor materials concentration (i.e. Ni(NO3)2·6H2O, CH4N2S, and EDTA) on the growth mechanism of nickel sulfide structures and phase control was investigated. As shown in Fig. 15(ii), there are four different phases in the synthesized products with respect to the concentration. In a similar approach, metallic CoS2 nanocube growth and amorphous MoS2 nanosheet coating was performed via a hydrothermal method269 to obtain a CoS2@MoS2 hierarchical nanoarray. The hydrothermal treatment involved a precursor of CoCl2 and (NH4)6Mo7O24 on a titanium substrate, with thiourea (CS(NH2)2) being used as the sulfur source. Composites of MoS2/montmorillonite (MMT) nanosheets were synthesized by Peng et al.270 Initially, a natural clay mineral (MMT) was dispersed in aqueous solution with Na2MoO4 and H2NCSNH2 as the precursor material. The MoO42− anion could not be adsorbed on the MMT surface due to the presence of negative surface charges; however, the thiourea molecules were adsorbed on the surface of MMT via amidogen. Under a hydrothermal treatment at 200 °C for 24 h, the thiourea decomposed and attached on the surface of MMT. The subsequent nucleation and growth of MoS2 nanosheets progressed in situ with the MMT sheets acting as templates. Finally, MoS2/MMT composite nanosheets were successfully grown which exhibited a high BET specific surface area and total pore volume of ∼72.10 m2 g−1 and ∼0.1206 cm3 g−1, respectively; this high surface area and small pore structure provided increased adsorption and reactive sites which were beneficial for catalytic and photocatalytic reactions.
For core–shell structure formation, Au nanopolyhedrons were dispersed in cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB).271 Under a hydrothermal treatment at 140 °C for 3 h, metal thiobenzoates were decomposed and provided sulfur for Ag+ or Cu2+ ions to form sulfides. The resultant Ag2S or CuS were tightly bound to Au nanocrystals owing to the similarity in the chemical properties between Cu, Ag, and Au. They acted as a wetting layer for the deposition of other metal sulfides that were created hydrothermally from the consistent thiobenzoates. The function of Ag2S or CuS as a wetting layer was attributed to the partial cation exchange between the different metal sulfides, as shown in Fig. 15(iii). Recently, Yu et al.272 prepared NiCo2S4 on a nickel foam by a hydrothermal method using precursor materials of Ni(NO3)2·6H2O, Co(NO3)2·6H2O, and thiourea, where the Ni to Co ratio was 1:2; and for comparison various concentrations were used. X-ray diffraction revealed a low crystallinity, structural disorder and enhanced defect sites in the NiCo2S4, which can enhance electrocatalytic activity. Highly active nanorods of NiCo2S4 were prepared with Ni(OAc)2·4H2O, Co(OAc)2·4H2O and thiourea as the source of Ni, Co and S, whereas water/ethylene glycol was used as the solvent, and the reaction temperature was 160 °C.273 In addition, Mei et al.274 prepared mushroom-like CoNi2S4 arrays for energy applications and hydrothermally prepared cubes and/or flower-like α-MnS and γ-MnS have been produced and are of interest of energy materials.275–277
Porous and heterostructured materials have recently received increased attention for energy conversion and storage, since porous structures offer an exceptionally large reactive surface area and metal sulfides with hybrid structures have improved electron transfer mechanisms. MnS decorated N–S co-doped rGO were prepared by Liu et al.278 They used raw materials of graphene oxide (GO), thiourea, manganese acetate tetrahydrate with a processing temperature of 180 °C for 20 h, followed by annealing in Ar at 600 °C for 4 h. In addition, a rGO/MnS nanotube hybrid structure was prepared using source materials of GO, MnCl2·4H2O and Na2S279 at a reaction temperature of ∼120 °C for 12 h. The prepared material was subsequently reduced using sodium borohydride, which significantly enhanced the conductive nature of the rGO/MnS nanotubes. In a similar approach, GO anchored porous MnS nanocrystals were prepared by Tang et al.280 The MnS/GO-NH3 sample exhibited a high surface area of (∼44.8 m2 g−1) with exceptional electrochemical behaviour. Highly porous nanoflakes of In2S3 was prepared281 using the raw materials of In(NO3)3 and glutathione (GSH), and a pH conditioner of urea was added, with a reaction temperature of 160 °C for 12 h. Recently, a one pot hydrothermal method has been used to prepare hierarchical Bi2S3/In2S3 core/shell structured microspheres.282 The source materials of Bi(NO3)3·5H2O, poly (sodium-p-styrenesulfonate) (PSS), In(NO3)3·4.5H2O and L-cysteine with a processing temperature of 150 °C for 9 h.


4.2.10 Solvothermal synthesis. 
For the solvothermal method, the synthesis approach is similar to the hydrothermal approach except that an organic solution is used, instead of water, as the precursor medium. In addition, the reaction temperature is above the critical point of the solvent and therefore the pressure is high.283,284 2D materials have been extensively investigated due to their exceptional features, such as high surface area, and abundant surface sites. Recently, a temperature effect on the structural modification of CoS hierarchitectures has been investigated by Wang et al.285 They attained a variety of flower-like structures using raw materials of CoCl2·6H2O and thioacetamide (TAA) as the precursors, and the only varied factor was temperature, as shown in Fig. 16(a–h). In addition, FeS needles were synthesized by the decomposition of a [Fe3(μ3-O)(μ2-O2CCH2Cl)6(H2O)3]NO3·H2O precursor complex with thiourea at 150 °C for 12 h for solvothermal synthesis.286 Similarly, orthorhombic structured Bi2S3 nanorods were prepared using Bi particles and Na2S2O3 as source materials.287 The morphology transformation from Bi particles to Bi2S3 nanorods was achieved via the solvothermal method. The solvents of EtOH/EDA/H2O were used as the reaction medium. Likewise, NiCl2·6H2O, sulfur and glycol was used to fabricate cubic pyrite of NiS2 dodecahedrons at 200 °C for 6 h.288 Recently, Huang et al.289 prepared hierarchical core-branch Al-doped nanosheets of cobalt sulfide attached on nickel nanotube arrays combined with a carbon cloth for electrochemical energy storage applications.
 Fig. 16  One-pot solvothermal synthesis and structural transformations of CoS hierarchitectures (a) temperature effect by XRD pattern of cobalt sulfides, FESEM images of the samples synthesized at 160 °C for various dwell time: (b) 4 h, aggregate spheres, (c) 8 h, aggregates with angular protuberancs, (d) 16 h, defective flower-like hierarchitectures, (e and f) 24 h, perfect flowers (with 0.025 M CoCl2·6H2O and 0.05 M TAA) and (g and h) flower clusters (120 °C at 24 h) and (b) intersectional hexagonal nanoplates (200 °C at 24 h), whereas 0.05 M CoCl2·6H2O and 0.1 M TAA used. Reproduced from ref. 285 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2011. Two kinds of layered quaternary silver–antimony(III) sulfides with different strategies by solvothermal synthesis (i) along [100] direction showing crenelated layers of KAg2SbS3 with cations between the layers. Ag, pink; Sb, green; S, yellow; K, purple and (j) along the [001] direction of a pair of layered [Ag3Sb3S7]2− anion with cations between the layers. Ag, pink; Sb, green; S, yellow; K, purple. Reproduced from ref. 300 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2009. 
Moreover, the use of the solvothermal method with a dissimilar reaction time was reported for the synthesis of nano-rods of SnS using precursors of stannous chloride (SnCl2:H2O), sodium sulfide (Na2S) and the solvent of N,N-dimethylformamide (DMF).290 The prepared solution was solvothermally heated at 200 °C for 15, 30, 40 and 48 h. As a result of the optimum reaction times, the SnS nanorods exhibited a highly crystalline nature, as shown in Fig. 17(i). Subsequently, two new layered bi-metallic sulfides of (Haep)2·Ga2Sb2S7 and Mn2(phen)Sb2S5 were prepared by Lin et al.291 The structure of (Haep)2·Ga2Sb2S7 consisted of GaS4 tetrahedra and SbS3 trigonal pyramids and had a layered structure with 8-membered-ring windows. Also, the Mn2(phen)Sb2S5 had a neutral layered structure which comprised of infinite Mn–S sheets. The organic species were attached directly to the atoms of Mn within the layers by covalent bonds and protruded into the interlayer section; where aep = 1-2-aminoethylpiperazine and phen = 1,10-phenanthroline. Likewise, large surface area nanosheets of NaInS2 were prepared via a solvothermal method292 using a precursor of InCl3 and Na2S·2H2O, and absolute ethanol. In a similar way, Cu–NaInS2 nanosheets were also prepared by the same group. In addition, flower-like nanostructures of Sb2−xBixS3 were prepared by Sun et al.293 through varying the concentration of precursors of antimony diethyldithiocarbamate [Sb(DDTC)3] and bismuth diethyldithiocarbamate [Bi(DDTC)3] in the ratio of x = 0.4 and 1.0. As a result, flower-like architectures with an average diameter of ∼4 μm were formed that were composed of single-crystalline nanorods with orthorhombic structures.
 Fig. 17  (a) (i) XRD pattern of prepared SnS powder at different reaction times and (b) FE-SEM image of SnS nanorods structured powder prepared at 40 h reaction time. Reproduced from ref. 290 with permission from Springer, copyright 2018. (ii) Schematic of the hierarchically ZnIn2S4 NCMA synthesis process (a), (b–f) microscopic images of the ZnIn2S4 NCMAs at different magnifications and (g–i) elemental mapping of S, In and Zn. Reproduced from ref. 294 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2018. 
Tu et al.294 developed a one-pot solvothermal synthesis method to fabricate ZnIn2S4 on a Zn substrate without any template or surfactant, where thioacetamide was the sulfur source. During synthesis, intermediate nanowires of indium were initially created which acted as a self-sacrificial template for the development of ZnIn2S4 microwires. The pristine ZnIn2S4 microwires were made by vertical nanosheets, and were controlled by the duration of the reaction time, as shown in Fig. 17(ii). The proposed nucleation growth conditions are below, Zn + In3+ → Zn2+ + In(19) CH3CSNH2 + H2O → CH3CONH2 + H2S(20) H2S → S2− + H+(21) Zn2+ In3+ + S2− → ZnxInySz(22)
It was reported that, a small amount of three ions (In3+, Zn2+ and S2−) created in the solution combine together in situ to produce shell compounds of ZnxInySz. The ZnIn2S4 exhibited excellent photocatalytic activity towards organic pollutant degradation. NiCo2S4 was also prepared using the polyvinylpyrrolidone, cobalt acetate and nickel acetate with ethanol solution,295 whereas, thioacetamide (TAA) was used as a sulfur source.
Metal sulfides with graphene or heterostructures are a potential favorable electrode material for energy conversion and storage applications. In this regard, Wang et al.296 used a single step solvothermal method using a poly(acrylic acid) (PAA) additive to synthesise well-dispersed nickel–cobalt sulfide nanoparticles on graphene. The raw materials of Ni(CH3COO)2·4H2O, Co(CH3COO)2·4H2O and PAA with a molar ratio of 1:2:2.8 dissolved with 0.5 mg mL−1 GO suspension in ethylene glycol (EG), where thiourea was used as a sulfur source. After mixing, the precursor was heated to 200 °C for 10 h; the final products and synthesis method are presented in Fig. 18. For a comparative analysis, nickel–cobalt sulfide@graphene and the nickel–cobalt sulfide (Ni–Co–S) samples were prepared in a similar way. In addition, MoS2 attached to a rGO/MoS2 hybrid41 was produced using (NH4)2MoS4 and hydrazine in a DMF solution with graphene oxide at 200 °C. During this preparation, the precursor of (NH4)2MoS4 was reduced to MoS2 on the surface of rGO, which was created via the reduction of GO by hydrazine. Li et al.297 prepared hybrid of SnS NS/rGO using ethylene glycol solution and citric acid with raw materials of SnCl2·2H2O and (NH3)2S. Li et al.298 synthesized highly conductive hierarchy structured Co1/3Mo2/3S2/graphene, the precursors of CoC4H6O4·4H2O and Na2MoO4·2H2O and sulfur were dissolved in ethylene glycol and vigorously stirred with GO to form a homogeneous solution. The addition of Co exposed its role in achieving enhanced electronic conductivity, easing ion diffusion by decreasing the thickness of the nanosheets, and reducing the aggregation of Co1/3Mo2/3S2/graphene.
 Fig. 18  (a) Schematic of one-step solvothermal process to fabricate Ni–Co–S@G for traditional electrode (strategy I) and Ni–Co–S@G/ACC flexible electrode (strategy II) by employing ACC as substrate, (b and c) SEM images and (d) TEM image of the Ni–Co–S@G hybrid. Reproduced from ref. 296 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2017. 
Ding et al.299 reported a one-step high-temperature hydrated-sulfate aided solvothermal method to prepare nanocomposite spheres of TiO2/CdS, TiO2/Cu2S, TiO2/FeS, TiO2/Co9S8, and TiO2/ZnS, where they used the maximum temperature of 350 °C. The TiO2/CdS nanocomposite exhibited outstanding photocatalytic efficiency due to its active hetero-interface and highly crystalline nature. By using the mineralizer of thiophenol, Yao et al.300 prepared silver antimony sulfides. KAg2SbS3 was synthesized by a reaction of K2CO3, AgNO3, Sb2S3, and S in a mixed solvent of thiophenol/pyridine and ethylenediamine (EDA). Secondly, the K2Ag3Sb3S7 was synthesized by the reaction of K2CO3, AgSPh, and Sb2S3 in a mixed solvent of glycol/pyridine and EDA. KAg2SbS3 revealed a hexagonal net structure produced by condensation of rings of six-membered Ag2SbS3. These sheets are linked through Ag–S bonds to create double layers. K2Ag3Sb3S7 consisted of infinite (Sb2S4)2− chains and (Ag6Sb2S10)∞8− columns linked to form layers, as shown in Fig. 16(i and j).


4.2.11 Sulfidation. 
The fabrication of metal sulfides is a crucial step to obtain highly active sulfide catalysts, since it transforms from the catalytically inactive metal oxide phase into an active sulfide phase. There have been several reports highlighting the importance of sulfide based nanoparticle transformations.301–304 However, some nanostructures continue to suffer from attachment of the sulfur to its host lattice. Sulfidation is a method of fixing sulfide ions in a host material or molecule. This technique has been recently widely used to convert metal oxides to metal sulfides, but is also related to surface modification of the final product. Typically, sulfidation can be executed using the direct addition of H2S or a combination of H2S; generally 10–15%, v/v in hydrogen.304 Single-crystalline iron pyrite (FeS2) nanorods, nanobelts, and nanoplates have been manufactured via a sulfidation reaction with iron dichloride (FeCl2), iron dibromide (FeBr2) and sulfur powder at high temperatures (≥450 °C).305 The same research group have fabricated single-crystalline FeS2 nanowires via thermal sulfidation of steel foil.306 Recently, Li et al.307 prepared nanoparticles of ZnS decorated on a nitrogen-doped porous carbon (NPC) polyhedral structure. In this work a zeolitic imidazolate framework-8 (ZIF-8) was used as the precursor over successive carbonization and sulfidation processes, as shown in Fig. 19(i). The resulting ZnS/NPC features exhibited a regular polyhedral morphology with a porous structure, high specific surface area, and a nitrogen-doped conductive carbonaceous network, offering a high conductivity and stability.
 Fig. 19  (i) (a) Schematic of preparation of ZIF-8 through a precipitation method, carbonization under a nitrogen atmosphere followed by ZnS/NPC via sulfidation. FESEM images of ZIF-8 (b and e), the carbonized sample (c and f), and ZnS/NPC (d and g). (h and i) TEM images of ZnS. Reproduced from ref. 307 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2017. (ii) (a) Basal orientation of MoS2 slabs on the C (0 0 0 1) plane of α-Al2O3 and change of polarization with respect to the synchrotron beam: parallel on the left and perpendicular on the right. k → is the wavevector and E → is the electric field vector, (b) sulfidation degree for model Mo/α-Al2O3 catalysts. Reproduced from ref. 308 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2016. (iii) Schematic of CuPt alloy nanoparticle synthetic routes and experimental conditions: (a) Cu-based routes and (b) Pt-based synthesis, and their following sulfidation process forming Pt–CuS heterodimers. TEM images of (c) 5–15 nm Pt–CuS (d) 10–12 nm and (e) 12–12 nm Pt–CuS heterodimers. Reproduced from ref. 314 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2017. (iv) Schematic of ZnO sulfidation with sulfur and iron oxide. Reproduced from ref. 316 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2017. 
To fabricate MoS2, the effect of the degree of sulfidation on MoO3 was reported by Bara et al.308 It was observed that the degree of sulfidation depended on the surface orientation of the α-Al2O3 substrate. In order to execute sulfidation, 15 mol% H2S/H2 gas phase sulfidation was performed with MoO3. The degree of sulfidation, i.e. the relative contribution of MoS2 to the total involvement of Mo, was determined at different sulfidation temperatures, as shown in Fig. 19(ii-a and b). As a result, a maximum degree of sulfidation was ∼90% at a low temperature of 300 °C for the C (0 0 0 1) plane, with a larger MoS2 slab size as shown in Fig. 19(ii: c). The degree of sulfidation influenced the surface morphology of the final MoS2 and WS2 particles and the way in which promoters were incorporated at the edges for MoS2 and WS2-based metal sulfides. In addition, at low temperatures the sulfidation of hematite (α-Fe2O3) NW arrays with H2S resulted in the formation of highly oriented FeS nanotubes.309 Likewise, three types of metal sulfides such as pyrrhotite (Fe1−xS), Co1−xS and α-MnS nanocrystals were prepared by Chen et al.310via sulfidation. Initially the oxides of Fe, Co and Mn nanoparticles were prepared, and then sulfur powder with ∼1.5 equivalents of MnO, FeO and CoO was added to the nanoparticle suspension solution. As a result, hollow structures of Fe1−xS, MnS and Co1−xS nanoparticles were formed with diameters of ∼26.8 ± 1.5 nm, 26.8 ± 1.5 nm and 16.5 ± 2.3 nm respectively. It was stated that, sulfur atoms attaching to the surface of the nanoparticles could promote the formation of vacancy defects. This can lead to an increase of vacancies in the form of voids in the center or on the surface of nanoparticles; this may be the reason for the formation of hollow structures.
Recently, Ag2S was prepared from synthetic Ag-NPs reacted with Na2S, which revealed substantial alterations in aggregation state, surface properties, surface charge, and dissolution rates. In order to investigate the controlled formation of Ag2S, the Na2S to Ag-NPs ratio was varied.311 It was proposed that part of the Ag2S was amorphous. Chen et al.312 prepared Ag2S by sulfidation reaction between silver nanoparticles (AgNPs) and sulfide species as below: 4Ag + O2 + 2H2S → 2Ag2S + 2H2O(23) 4Ag + O2 + 2HS− → 2Ag2S + 2OH−(24) 4Ag + O2 + 2H2O + 2S2− → 2Ag2S + 4OH−(25)
In order to attain CuS nanoparticles, sulfidation of CuO nanoparticles was conducted by Ma et al.313 During synthesis, dispersed CuO nanoparticles (100 mg L−1) in 10 mM sodium nitrate (NaNO3) were reacted with sulfide (Na2S) in N2-purged 50 mL propylene Falcon tubes. The molar ratio of S/Cu was varied from 0.21 to 2.16 to examine the properties of sulfidized nanoparticles to different extents. The sulfidation of CuO was as below: 8CuO + 5S2− + 8H+ = 4Cu2S + SO42− + 4H2O(26) CuO + S2− + 2H+ = CuS + H2O(27) 8Cu2+ + 5S2− + 4H2O = 4Cu2S + SO42− + 8H+(28) Cu2+ + S2− = CuS(29)
