Eqn (26) and (28) are redox reactions in which Cu(II) is reduced to Cu(I) while S(−II) in sulfide is oxidized to S(VI) in sulfate. However, eqn (27) and (29) are not redox reactions. The normal Gibbs free energy of reaction of eqn (26) and (27) are −1432 kJ mol−1 and −246.8 kJ mol−1, respectively. For eqn (27) reaction conditions,  specifying a spontaneous reaction. Therefore, the formation of CuS and Cu2S are thermodynamically favorable by the above reactions.
It is well known that, phase transfer methods hold extraordinary benefits for the synthesis of inorganic nanomaterials. Recently, Pt coupled CuS heterodimers were prepared by Ding et al.314 where the heterostructured Pt/CuS NPs were synthesized by use of Cu–Pt alloy nanoparticles with a sulfur source of oleamine and its subsequent phase segregation, as shown in Fig. 19(iii). These results specify that temperature and the reactivity of the sulfur source hold key roles in the sulfidation of Cu in a Cu–Pt alloy. The same group also reported a Au–Cu2S alloy formed by a similar approach.315 Recently, ZnO sulfidation with sulfur and iron oxide at high temperatures was investigated by Han et al.316 Zinc oxide (ZnO), sulfur, and Fe2O3 were used as raw materials to fabricate metal sulfides, as shown in Fig. 19(iv), and the growth behavior of both ZnS and FeS at high temperatures were systematically studied. The sulfidation behavior of zinc, phase transformations, morphology changes, and surface properties were also investigated. One dimensional ZnO–ZnS core–shell nanofibers were also formed using the precursors of polyvinyl alcohol (PVA), zinc acetate dehydrate and thioacetamide.317 Initially, fibers of ZnO were prepared by electrospinnng, then the surface of the ZnO nanofibers were converted into a shell layered ZnS via sulfidation, and the increased sulfidation time enhanced visible absorption behavior. Similarly, ZnS–ZnO nanocomposites were fabricated by the solvothermal method, followed by the partial sulfidation of ZnO at low temperatures.318
In addition to the above morphologies, yolk–shell,319 nanobubble,320 hollow and hierarchical features321,322 offer advantages for building progressive materials for green energy applications. Recently, Heift et al.323 prepared iron sulfide nanoflakes by sulfidation of 9 nm zerovalent iron nanoparticles with H2S using the precursor of bis[bis(trimethylsilyl)amido]iron(II). The iron sulfide flakes formed consisted of Fe2S2 and Fe7S8, which exhibited magnetic behavior. Vu et al.324 prepared nickel supported CdIn2S4 (Ni-CIS) sponge-like spheres using alcoholysis, followed by a sulfidation process. The CdIn@glycerate solid spheres were prepared on Ni foam and then calcined at 500 °C in air for 3 h. Then the CdIn2O4 was treated under a H2S atmosphere to fabricate sponge-like spheres of CdIn2S4 (Ni-CIS). Yin et al.325 prepared hollow structured Co9S8 and Co3S4 using thermal decomposition of Co2(CO)8 to form CoO nanocrystals, followed by sulfidation of sulfide in an anhydrous o-dichlorobenzene medium. The use of sulfidation of Na2S with Co3O4 and Co(CO3)0.5(OH)x·11H2O has been used, while CoS326 and Co9S8327 were effectively transformed by anion exchange. CoS polyhedral nanocages were also prepared from a mesoporous metal–organic framework (MOF).328 Shen et al.303 reported a facile anion exchange method to fabricate a novel ball-in-ball hollow structured ternary nickel cobalt sulfide (NiCo2S4). The production method involved a facile solvothermal synthesis of metal–glycerate solid spheres, followed by sulfidation with thioacetamide (TAA). The insides of the as-prepared NiCo2S4 hollow spheres were simply modified by varying the reaction temperature during the anion exchange process, as shown in Fig. 20(i). When used as an electrode material for electrochemical studies, the NiCo2S4 ball-in-ball hollow spheres provided excellent performance. Su et al.329 prepared unique yolk–shell CdS microcubes from Cd–Fe Prussian blue equivalents (Cd–Fe–PBA). To examine the growth process and the formation mechanism of the yolk–shell CdS microcubes, a time-dependent sulfidation treatment was carried out at 150 °C. Initially, a sulfidation treatment at 150 °C for 0.5 h revealed a microcube structure with a rough surface. The separated cores and shells of the microcubes were observed at 150 °C for 1.5 h (Fig. 20(ii)), which signified the initial conversion of yolk–shell microcubes. When the sulfidation time was ∼2 h, the core and shell of the microcubes separated completely, and distinct yolk–shell microcubes were attained. As a result, the yolk–shell CdS microcubes exhibited a narrow band gap of ∼2.24 eV with a large surface area of 94.0 m2 g−1. These properties greatly improved the visible light photocatalytic activity. Moreover, a variety of structures such as MS (M = Ni, Cu, Mn) box-in-box hollow structures,330 CoS2 nanobubbles of hollow prisms,331 double shelled ZnCoS,332 NiCo2S4 hollow nanocolloids,333 hollow hybrid of CoS2 with porous carbon,334 and carbon coated hollow CoS2 nanocages335 have been formed. Owing to their highly conductive and porous nature, their energy conversion and storage performance was greatly enhanced.
 Fig. 20  (i) Schematic represents, (a) synthesis involves a facile solvothermal synthesis of metal–glycerate solid spheres and subsequent sulfidation in the presence of thioacetamide (TAA) to form NiCo2S4 ball-in-ball hollow spheres, (b–i) corresponding structural evolution (b and f) NiCo glycerate solid sphere (c and g) core–shell structure (d and h) yolk–shell and (e and i) ball in ball hollow sphere. Reproduced from ref. 303 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2015. (ii) TEM images of the yolk–shell CdS microcubes products after reactions for (a) 0 h, (b) 0.5 h, (c) 1.5 h and (d) 2 h, and (e–l) the corresponding schematic illustration of the formation process of the yolk–shell CdS microcubes, (m and n) N2 adsorption–desorption isotherms and Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) pore size distributions of the as-prepared samples. Reproduced from ref. 329 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2017. 


4.2.12 Microwave irradiation. 
Typically, microwaves generated by magnetrons are electromagnetic waves, and lie in the wavelength region from 1 mm to 1 m, and their frequency range is about 0.3–300 GHz and only a limited frequency of ∼0.9 and 2.45 GHz are acceptable for microwave heating drives.336,337 Heating via microwave radiation leads to the conventional interaction amid microwaves and materials,338,339 hence the electromagnetic energy is transformed to thermal energy. Heat is produced from inside the material, which is in contrast to conventional heating approaches where heat is transported from outside to inside. This internal heating allows a shorter reaction time and saves energy. Hence, irradiation by microwaves is faster and higher efficiency compared to conventional approaches. By using the microwave method, Chen et al.340 prepared metal sulfides of CdS, ZnS, CoS, PbS, CuS, Bi2S3, Sb2S3 and Ag2S, where the metal–thiourea complexes undergo thermal decomposition in ethylene glycol (EG) under microwave irradiation of 800 W for 20 min to produce final metal sulfides. The reactions taking place can be expressed as follows: (30) (31)in which the EG and microwave irradiation played a major role for the preparation of metal sulfides.
Butala et al.341 rapidly prepared FeS2, CoS2, Fe0.5Co0.5S2 and Cu1−xTi2S4 metal sulfides using starting materials of sulfur, titanium, iron, cobalt and copper. The metal to sulfur ratios of the powders ground for each compound was: for CoS2, 1:2.3; FeS2, 1:2.25; Fe0.5Co0.5S2, 1:1:4.6; and CuTi2S4, 1:2:4.25. Initially, FeS2, CoS2, and their solid solutions were heated at 30% power (360 W) for 20 min and slowly cooled to room temperature for a minimum of 2.5 h. The same approach was used to prepare composites. As a result, all the samples exhibited a highly crystalline nature. Recently, the temperature effect on the formation of various NiS2 nanostructures was reported by Pang et al.338 using the raw materials of Ni(NO3)2, sulfourea, and polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP) in NaOH solution. After treating the mixture at a temperature of 150 °C for 2, 3 and 4 h under microwave irradiation nanospheres, a mixed structure of cubes with spheres and nanocubes were achieved respectively, as shown in Fig. 21. Later, Krylova et al.342 used a microwave assisted solution–liquid–solid synthesis method to synthesise copper indium sulfide nanowires for efficiently harvesting solar energy using a binuclear single-source precursor (SSP) complex [(PPh3)2Cu][(μ-SPh)2In(SPh)2] (Ph = phenyl), as shown in Fig. 21. In addition, important parameters such as solution temperature, irradiation time, and precursor concentrations were varied systematically. Preliminary experiments suggested that 300 °C was required to thermally decompose the less reactive portion of SSP containing indium. Hollow cobalt sulfide nanoprisms were obtained using precursors of cobalt acetate hydroxide with thioacetamide.343 Compared to the conventional solvothermal synthesis, the microwave synthesis of cobalt sulfide nanoprisms provided materials that exhibited higher hydrogen evolution catalytic activity and better surface area. Jiang et al.344 prepared single-crystalline nanorods of Bi2S3 and Sb2S3 using the ionic liquid of 1-butyl-3-methylimidazolium tetrafluoroborate ([BMIM][BF4]). As a result, the morphology of the final products was controlled by reaction time. Similarly, nanorods and wires of ZnS and CdS was prepared by a single precursor source of metal ethyl xanthate, where DMF was used as solvent.345 The SnS and SnS2, prepared using tin(IV) chloride and sodium thiosulfate or thiourea for sulfur source were mixed with ethylene glycol.346 For synthesis, the precursors were irradiated with 900 W at 2.45 GHz.
 Fig. 21  (1) TEM images of NiS2 nanocubes prepared via microwave assisted synthesis, the reaction times are (a) 30 min, (b) 2 h, (c) 3 h, and (d) 4 h. Reproduced from ref. 338 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2014. (2) (a) Schematic, (b) TEM image, and (c) elemental profile of the solidified copper indium sulfide (CuInS2) catalyst nanodroplet. Scale bar: 10 nm. Reproduced from ref. 342 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2015. (3) Controlled electrical properties of MoS2 and MoS2/GR composite using microwave method. Reproduced from ref. 348 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2014. (4) (a) Schematic of fullerene like MoS2 and WS2 particles fabrication, (b and c) and (d and e) are the TEM images of MoS2 and WS2 fullerenes. Reproduced from ref. 347 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2016. 
Recently, fullerene-like metal sulfides of MoS2 and WS2 have been prepared using the precursor of polypyrrole nanofibers (PPy NF), sulfur, Mo(CO)6 and W(CO)6.347 The solid mixture was irradiated by 1250 W of microwave (MW) power for 60 s. Importantly, the fast microwave reaction with extreme high temperature (∼1000 °C) led to nanoparticles only a few nanometers in size, as shown in Fig. 21. Similarly, highly efficient and stable MoS2@graphene sheets hybrids were synthesized using MoCl5 dissolved in ethanol, which was added to a GO solution. A stoichiometric amount of sulfur source and thiourea was added to the solution.348 The reduced graphene further assisted the reaction by efficiently absorbing the microwave and transferring the heat to MoS2 crystals. Finally, a highly crystalline MoS2/graphene was attained within 45 seconds, which enhanced the HER activity, as shown in Fig. 21. In addition, hexagonal structured ZnS, CdS and CdS@ZnS were prepared by Raubach et al.349 where the precursors were irradiated at 453 K for 32 min. As a result, the CdS and ZnS interfaces yield an electron charge transfer from the CdS electron-populated clusters to the ZnS hole-populated clusters, which enhanced the photocatalytic performance of the system.
The formation of CdCO3–CdS core–shell nanocubes was proposed by Yang et al.348 Initially, a uniform precursor of CdCO3 nanocubes were attained via precipitation, then a conformal CdS nanocrystal layer was deposited on the CdCO3 core–shell nanocubes via a microwave method and irradiated at 400 W for 15 min. Finally, the CdCO3–CdS core–shell nanocubes were changed to porous CdO–CdS core–shell nanoboxes via a thermal decomposition process.


4.2.13 Electrodeposition. 
Electrodeposition is an alternative and conventional surface modification process to improve the surface features, decorative and highly functional properties of a wide range of materials within a short time period. The basic principle works on Faraday's laws of electrolysis.350–352 Usually, the electrodeposition method includes three electrodes, (i) a working electrode (ii) a reference electrode such as Hg/HgO or Ag/AgCl and (iii) a counter electrode (usually Pt). In this process the working electrode, with a reference electrode, makes a voltage loop to test the potential. A working electrode such as nickel foam, stainless steel or conductive glass is used to make the electrochemical reaction and to load the generated product. Mostly, electrodeposition can be galvanostatic or potentiostatic deposition. All deposition reactions were made when the electrodes were immersed in an electrochemical bath and direct current (DC) is applied. The bath comprises of a chemical salt solution of the metal to be plated. Using this method a variety of nanostructures such as nanosheets, nanowires, tubes and particles of metal sulfides, metal sulfide composites and hybrids have been prepared.353–356 In order to electrodeposit metal sulfides throughout the channels of the template, a porous material of nickel foam was mounted on a bath; as shown in Fig. 22. Initially the pre-cleaned nickel foam was used as a working electrode and the auxiliary electrode (counter electrode) was a platinum wire and Ag/AgCl (3 M KCl) as the reference electrode. The analyte solution consisted of Ni(NO3)2·6H2O and Fe(NO3)3·9H2O and thiourea.357 Chronoamperometry was then carried out at −1.0 V at 10 °C. Finally the deposition time was varied from 2.5 to 5 min and well-ordered Ni2FeS4 was obtained by the following reactions, Ni2+ + 2Thiourea → Ni(Thiourea)22+(32) Ni(Thiourea)22+ + NiFe → Ni2FeS4(33)
 Fig. 22  Schematic of electrochemical deposition of nickel iron sulfides on nickel foam: (a) nickel iron deposition on nickel foam and (b) sulfide deposition on nickel iron foam. Reproduced from ref. 357 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2016. 
In addition, electrodeposition was used for the formation of ternary CdxZn1−xS on Ag(1 1 1) by Loglio et al.358 Similarly, Lai et al.359 reported the template-assisted electrochemical synthesis of Cu2S nanotrees. Initially, Cu nanowires were electrodeposited on an aluminum template. For instance, CuSO4·5H2O was used as a Cu source and the electrolyte contained H3BO3 and 20 vol% methanol. Afterward, the template was removed using 1 M NaOH aqueous solution, and Cu2S nanotrees were produced by H2S sulfidation of metallic copper nanowires. The CuS nanorods were prepared by depositing in the template pores at a constant potential. Na2S2O3 and CuSO4 in H2O were used as precursors and the electrolyte was tartaric acid with a pH below 2.5.360
Recently, template-free electrodeposition of CuS and Cu2−xS nanowires was reported using CuSO4·5H2O and thiourea as the sources of copper (Cu2+) and sulfur, respectively; and hydrochloric acid (HCl) was used as electrolyte.361 The growth mechanism of nanowires was credited to the low concentration of Cu2+ and thiourea species, with an applied potential of −0.85 V vs. Ag/AgCl and deposition time between 30 min and 3 h. In addition, galvanostatically electrodeposited Ni3S2/Ni nanocomposites on Cu foils (4 × 5 cm2) were prepared at room temperature using a two-electrode configuration, where NiSO4·6H2O, thiourea, and citric acid were the sources of nickel and sulfur and pH buffer reagent.362 A number of researchers pointed out that the thiourea concentration was a key parameter influencing sulfur content in the metal–S deposit. Lin et al.363 undertook cathodic deposition of a porous CoS on FTO glass substrates in a near-neutral deposition bath (NH4OH with pH ∼ 6) at room temperature where the Co and S sources were CoCl2·6H2O and thiourea, CH4N2S (ARCOS, 99%) in a three-electrode setup. The deposition by potentiodynamic was set between −1.2 V and 0.2 V vs. Ag/AgCl with a scan rate of 5 mV s−1 for fixed cycles. As a result, highly porous flakes of CoS were acquired. Later, Li et al.364 electrodeposited 3D macroporous nickel sulfide on graphene-covered make-up cotton as a flexible electrode through “dip and dry” and electrodeposition methods. The electrodeposition solution was composed of nickel chloride hexahydrate (0.1 M) and thiourea (1 M). The deposition voltage was ∼0.9 V and continued for several minutes at room temperature. In a similar approach, different morphologies of nickel cobalt sulfides were attained by controlled composition onto the flexible carbon cloth.356 The electrodeposition solution contained CoCl2·6H2O with different concentrations of NiCl2·6H2O and thiourea, whereas the solution pH ∼ 6 was adjusted using diluted NH3·H2O. Similarly, SnS was electrodeposited onto single-crystal Au(100) using a solution of SnCl2, thiosulfate, and L-tartaric acid at pH 2.5.365 During deposition, the potential widow for common aqueous or organic media was low.366 Moreover, ionic liquids display significant advantages that enable them to overcome the limits imposed by common aqueous media, such as long electrochemical window of ∼6 V and exceptionally low vapor pressures, which enable electrodeposition above 100 °C.367 Such attractive characteristics are beneficial to manufacture metal sulfide compounds in an effective manner. Recently, it was demonstrated that ionic liquids can solubilize molecular sulfur at the melting point of ∼115 °C.368 Such a discovery is a motivation to discover the growth of new metal sulfides by this route. Chen et al.369 deposited CuS thin films using an electrolytic bath that consisted of different molar ratios of Cu(TFSI)2 and sulfur powder in the ionic liquid [EMIm]TFSI. Finally, single-crystallite of covellite CuS thin films with a flake-like nanostructure was obtained. By using the room temperature ionic liquid of PP13-TFSI (RTIL), MoS2 nano-particles and flakes were synthesized by Murugesan et al.370 whereas, molybdenum glycolate and 1,4-butanedithiol was used as the source materials.

4.3 Other miscellaneous routes
A variety of other techniques also have been used by other research groups and are discussed below.

4.3.1 Ion exchange method. 
The ion exchange method is a self-template technique371 and it is a cost-effective method since it facilitates nanostructure control via a top-down route, leading to multi-faceted hierarchical structures.47,303 Specifically, anion-exchange leads to hollowing or tunneling of solid precursor wires, rods, and spheres without destroying the morphologies and structures. For example, the microstructure and composition of the Ag2S/Ag3PO4 nanocomposites were varied by an anion exchange method.372 During synthesis, the S2− ions (from Na2S) substitute for the [PO4]3− units on the surface of Ag3PO4 dodecahedrons to form Ag2S nanoparticles, due to the much smaller solubility constant (Ksp) value ∼6.6 × 10−50 of Ag2S compared to Ag3PO4 ∼1.4 × 10−16. The condition of nucleation and growth of Ag2S nanoparticles is highly dependent on the concentration of S2− during this ion-exchange process.
Metal sulfide materials have different solubility constants (Ksp) in water. According to their different solubility, metal sulfides could transform via ion exchange. Nanosheets and hollow nanorods of CdS were synthesized using CdCl2·2.5H2O and Na2S·9H2O.373 Initially, the precipitation of nanoporous Cd(OH)2 nanosheets and nanorods were obtained and then CdS nanosheets and hollow nanorods were fabricated followed by S2−/OH− ion-exchange conversion of Cd(OH)2 to CdS. Similarly Wu et al.374 reported a two step ion exchange method to prepare a nanotube network of MCo2S4 (M = Ni, Fe, Zn). A precursor of Na2S was used as a sulfur source. During synthesis, the cations of Co2+, Ni2+, and NH4+ react with the anions of CO32− and OH− is released from the urea hydrolysis, leading to the formation of hexagonal solid thin microsheets of NiCo(OH)x(CO3)y(HCO3)z with smooth surfaces, as shown in Fig. 23. After a 2 h reaction, several small needles on the edges and corners of each hexagon were observed. After 8 h, these microsheets are converted to a 3D porous architecture of NiCo2(OH)3(CO3)1.5 hexagons with a NW-interwoven and a round central hole over an anion replacement of HCO3− by CO32− and OH− ions was observed. Zhuo et al.375 prepared hierarchical nanosheet based 2H-MoS2 (i.e. semiconducting 2H phased MoS2) nanotubes by an anion exchange reaction of the inorganic–organic hybrid nanowires of Mo3O10(C2H10N2) (ethylenediamine trimolybdate, denoted as MoO3–EDA) with a sulfur source of L-cysteine at elevated temperature. The ethylenediamine (EDA) dissolution in the aqueous solution solvent and the concentration gradient with driven outward diffusion of MoO3–EDA during the anion exchange reaction were found to play a major role in the creation of the nanotubes of the hierarchical nanosheet. Moreover, the prepared hierarchical nanosheet based MoS2 nanotubes were found to be highly active for electrochemical and visible light photoelectrocatalytic hydrogen evolution reactions. Remarkably, a sequential ion-change strategy has been developed by Lou et al. to fabricate onion-like NiCo2S4 metal sulfide particles.69 Due to the complex onion-like morphology of the NiCo2S4 particles, it exhibited an excellent electrochemical performance with long term durability. From this study, it is clear that an onion-like morphology of NiCo2S4 has a significant role in the performance of an electrode, which provides a large active surface area and relatively short diffusion pathway for ions, leading to high utilization of the active materials.
 Fig. 23  Schematic of the construction of NiCo2S4 HPHTMSs by well-interwoven NTs. In the presence of NH4F, a slow anisotropic growth leads to compound I of NiCo2(OH)x(CO3)y(HCO3)z, while the product is already compound II of NiCo2(OH)3(CO3)1.5 after only 0.5 h and its morphology is spherical with a rough NW-covering without NH4F. The nanostructure and composition change with time during the first ion-exchange process from compound I to compound II is presented in detail. The second anion-exchange results in the formation of compound III of NiCo2S4 HPHTMSs. The insets illustrate local magnifications of several interwoven NWs and sulfides NTs. A wire-interwoven network structure in a steel mesh with strong mechanical stability is also presented. Reproduced from ref. 374 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018. 


4.3.2 Template engaged technique. 
The template synthesis technique of micro- and nano-materials has been a cutting-edge technology advanced since the 1990s. This method is not sensitive to synthesis conditions, is easy to control and implement, and can control the morphology, structure, and size of nanomaterials through the template material.47,376,377 Moreover, the template approach has resulted in improved dispersed products with large specific surface areas with desired structures. Notwithstanding the above stated developments, in most cases, the resultant hollow structures are spherical or square in shape. Recently, hollow Co3S4 was fabricated by Liang et al.378 using a ZIF-67 as the template and thioacetamide as the sulfide source. A hierarchical NiS box-in-box hollow nanostructure was formed using the initial templates of uniform silica nanoboxes which was first prepared using Fe2O3 nanocubes as the template.330 Subsequently, template nickel silicate box-in-box hollow nanostructures were synthesized using nickel acetate, ammonia, and ammonium chloride. Upon completion of the reaction, the as-prepared box-in-box nickel silicate structure was transformed into nickel sulfide box-in-box hollow nanostructures over a solution-based sulfidation method in the existence of Na2S. A similar approach was adopted to make CuS and MnS box-in-box hollow structures.
Among the existing synthetic procedures, hard template methods have been established as a versatile approach to make well-defined hollow structures with a controlled shell number and high uniformity. Typically, the synthesis of metal sulfides in nanostructured form or as a composite can successfully overcome this pulverization, specifically by using porous carbon as the host structure for the metal sulfides, since the carbon can adsorb and capture the intermediates of polysulfide responsible for the shuttle effect, subsequently leading to longer cycle life of the final materials.379,380 A 3D CoS2/carbon composite was prepared by Zhang et al.381 The formation of this 3D porous structure was created by self-stacking NaCl crystals, which acted as a template and L-cysteine was selected as a sulfur source. Similarly, molecular templates were used to synthesize CuS nanorods.382 Using the EDA solvent template, shape and phase controlled ZnS nanocrystals and ZnO flake-like dendrites were prepared from a lamellar molecular precursor ZnS·(NH2CH2CH2NH2)0.5 by Yu et al.383 In addition, mesostructured metal germanium sulfide materials were prepared using the precursor of non-aqueous surfactant-templated assembly of adamantanoid [Ge4S10]4− cluster.384 In the presence of quaternary alkylammonium surfactants, the [Ge4S10]4− anions in formamide solution self-organize with metal cations such as Co2+, Ni2+, Cu+ and Zn2+ created regular metal germanium sulfide mesostructures with a hexagonal structure, and some fiber-like morphologies were also observed.
1D nanotubular structures hold potential for use in numerous fields such as composite materials, catalysis, batteries and photocatalysis. In this aspect, layer-rolled NiS rolls with a diameter of 0.5 ± 1.0 μm and about 5.0 μm in length was prepared by Jiang et al.385 During fabrication, it was observed that the ammonia molecules play multiple roles in the formation of layer-rolled NiS structures, serving as the architecture template, coordinating agent of Ni(NH3)62+, the attacking agent of CS2, and the source of hydrogen bonds. In addition, ammonia served as the reaction template. Hydrogen-bond interactions (NH-OH2) also played an important role in the growth of 2D layered NiS materials. Wire-like CdS nanoparticles with diameters of 4–5 nm and length of ∼170 nm were prepared by Zhang et al.386 using single molecule templates of well-ordered amphiphilic core–shell cylindrical polymer brushes ([AA25-nBA61]1500 contained 0.077 mmol of acrylic acid). The coordination of cadmium ions with carboxylate groups in the core of the brush favored growth, in which the creation of CdS nanoparticles inside the polymer brush was through the reaction of the coordinated Cd2+ ions with H2S. If the formation of CdS nanoparticles was completed, then the polymer brush starts again with its original morphology and chemical structure and can therefore continue to be used as template. Similarly, a NiS@C hybrid material was produced by a controllable template approach.387 As shown in Fig. 24(i), uniform diameter ∼450 nm SiO2 nanospheres were used as a hard template during synthesis. After reacting with a precursor of nickel nitrate aqueous solution and coated by a resin, SiO2@Ni-silicate@RF spheres were fabricated, followed by carbonization. Subsequently, NiS@C with a well-maintained spherical morphology was fabricated by a sulfidation process using Na2S. Likewise, acid-functionalized MWCNTs were used as both ligands and templates to prepare the Cu2S–MWCNT hybrid nanostructures.388
 Fig. 24  (i) (a) Schematic of template engaged fabrication process of NiS@C-hollow sphere (HS). TEM images of (b) SiO2, (c) SiO2@Ni-silicate@RF, (d) SiO2@Ni@C, (e) NiS@C-hollow sphere, and inset is the HRTEM of NiS. Reproduced from ref. 387 with permission from Wiley-VCH, copyright 2017. (ii) (a) Formation process of CuCo2S4@N/S-G via sacrificial template method. Reproduced from ref. 397 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018. 


4.3.3 Self-assembly method. 
Conventional template methods for the preparation of hollow spheres usually require a removable or sacrificial template such as carbon, silica spheres, reducing metal nanoparticles and monodisperse polymer latex, to direct the growth of inorganic nanoparticles on their surfaces by adsorption or chemical reactions. However, the use of such templates suffers from disadvantages of high cost and tedious synthetic approaches, which can prevent them from being used in large-scale applications. In contrast, a one-pot template-free method for controlled preparation has been developed to design hollow nanostructures with rationally considered parameters.389,390 Recently, a number of one-pot template-free methods for generating various inorganic micro- and nano-structures have been established using physical phenomena such as Ostwald ripening391,392 and Kirkendall type diffusion.388,393 In this aspect, Yu et al.394 prepared monodisperse ZnS hollow spheres using a one-pot template-free method. A defect rich WS2 was synthesized via a self-template method,395 in which tungsten oxide was partially sulfurized to form nanosheets of WS2/WO3 at 450–750 °C. Furthermore, a porous structure of WS2 was designed after the removal of the WO3 template by alkaline solution and additional edge defects were acquired.
Hollow nanostructures are of interest for a range of applications. Yu et al.396 prepared an initial sacrificial template of tetragonal nanoprisms of nickel–cobalt acetate hydroxide precursors with controllable Ni/Co molar ratio to fabricate NixCo3−xS4 hollow nanoprisms. In addition to chemical composition design, the rational structural design of electrode materials is important for the performance of electrochemical reactions. Recently, a template free method was used to form CuCo2O4 hollow spheres with thiourea, and to fabricate hollow spheres of CuCo2S4 without destroying the hollow structure;397 N/S doped graphene was then coated simultaneously through an electrostatic self-assembly method, as shown in Fig. 24(ii). In addition, C@Bi2S3 core–shell heterostructures were synthesized via a sacrificial template-free method by Vattikuti et al.398 This C@Bi2S3 core–shell feature offered an efficient charge separation and transfer due to the heterojunction. Wang et al.399 prepared Cu2−xS@C, Cu2−xS@TiO2 and Cu2−xS@MoS2 hollow spheres via a self-templating thermolysis strategy using CuS. All the prepared composite materials were tested for electrochemical properties; and all materials showed excellent stability. This was attributed to the coverage of carbon on Cu2−xS that prevents the decomposition of Cu2−xS. Moreover, other metal sulfides and/or their composites/heterostructures were also prepared using some synthesis techniques such as chemical bath deposition and similar techniques.

4.4 Perspectives on fabrication methods
A number of effective fabrication methods have been established to prepare high yield, large scale and low-cost production of 0D, 1D, 2D and 3D metal sulfide nanostructures. The outstanding performance of these prepared nanostructures for specific needs, especially in electrocatalysis (for instance, catalytic HER reactions), have been revealed. From the view point of the controlled synthesis process, the 2D (especially MoS2, WS2 and their heterostructures) and 3D (for example hollow spheres, boxes, cubes, ball-in ball-structures) templated synthesis method has proven to be a promising strategy. In addition, surface morphologies from the bulk to diverse nano- and micro-structures have been generated via simple strategies; for example hydrothermal, solvothermal, and sulfidation. The achieved nanocrystals have varied from the highly crystalline to the formation of defect-enhanced structures. The nanocrystalline nature of the materials formed have been controlled via experimental parameters, such as the precursor concentration, sulfur source, solvent type, pH of reaction medium, reaction temperature and surfactants. Increasing number of approaches is being examined, and new types of metal sulfides have been manufactured and proposed. With the rational strategy of composition, structure, morphology, defect, loading substrate, temperature and porosity, metal sulfides can be applied in a variety of energy fields. However, many of the mechanisms underlying the growth of 1D metal sulfide nanostructures, ultrathin 2D nanosheets and 3D (for example, hollow boxes, ball in ball shape, hierarchical etc.,) nanomaterials remain unclear. Thus, the investigation of the mechanisms behind these nanocrystal growth routes and understanding of their final properties is of interest in future research efforts.
5. Fundamentals of electrocatalytic hydrogen evolution reaction, photocatalytic hydrogen evolution reaction and photoelectrochemical water splitting
5.1 Basics of hydrogen evolution reaction (HER)
The fundamental and practical aspect of the hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) is of interest to reduce our reliance on fossil fuels. As with other electrochemical reactions, such as the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) and oxygen evolution reaction (OER), the HER requires an overpotential, and hence, it is vital to develop suitable electrocatalysts to exploit the reaction efficiency.400–403 Noble metals such as platinum are ideal electrocatalyst for HER reactions in terms of low overpotential and efficiency. However, their high cost, lack of stability and CO poisoning effects has led to a continued quest for developing low-cost, durable and naturally abundant electrocatalysts.404–406
A range of electrocatalysts have been examined for use in HER in both acidic407–409 and alkaline environments.410,411 The HER begins by the adsorption of hydrogen (Hads) on the electrocatalyst surface,412 which is represented as the Volmer reaction (eqn (34) and (35)). Subsequently, depending on the nature of electrocatalysis, this can progress with the reaction of two adsorbed hydrogen (i.e., the Tafel reaction) or an adsorbed H with H+ reaction from the electrolyte (i.e., the Heyrovsky reaction) to generate a H2 molecule. Regardless of the catalytic reaction pathway, H adsorption is an important step, and thus, it is an energetic task of the highly active electrocatalyst to facilitate the adsorption reactions. Remarkably, the adsorption free energy of hydrogen (H) on platinum is close to the thermoneutral state (ΔGH* ≈ 0; Gibbs free energy). This is the main reason that platinum is considered as an efficient HER electrocatalyst. The mechanism of the electrocatalytic HER reactions via Volmer–Heyrovsky mechanisms (eqn (34)–(37)) or the Volmer–Tafel mechanism (eqn (38)–(40)) are given below:412,413 H3O+(aq) + e− + * ⇌ Hads + H2O(l), Volmer reaction (acidic)(34) H2O(l) + e− + * ⇌ Hads + OH−(aq), Volmer reaction (alkaline)(35) Hads + H3O+(aq) + e− ⇌ H2(g) + H2O(l) + *, Heyrovsky reaction (acidic)(36) Hads + H2O(l) + e− ⇌ H2(g) + OH−(aq) + *, Heyrovsky reaction (alkaline)(37) H3O+(aq) + e− + * ⇌ Hads + H2O(l), Volmer reaction (acidic)(38) H2O(l) + e− + * ⇌ Hads + OH−(aq), Volmer reaction (alkaline)(39) Hads + Hads ⇌ H2(g) + 2*, Tafel reaction (acidic and alkaline)(40)where * designates an empty active site. The basic factors in both reactions are the binding of the intermediate hydrogen to the surface of the catalyst.414,415 This leads to plotting the catalyst activity against the hydrogen binding free energy via volcano plots. Since the Hads is involved in the complete processes, hydrogen adsorption free energy (ΔGH) is commonly considered as an important factor to lead the progress and optimize the non-noble metal catalysts for HER.

5.1.1 Assessment of hydrogen evolution reaction (HER). 
As shown in Fig. 25(a), a conventional HER experimental setup consists of a reference electrode (usually Ag/AgCl or RHE), counter electrode (platinum wire or mesh) and a working electrode. Typically, for the working electrode, the catalysts are often coated on a nickel foam, titanium mesh, carbon cloth, carbon fiber cloth or glassy carbon electrode. Similarly, Fig. 25 shows the linear sweep voltammograms (LSV) which offer specific visual evidence to evaluate the HER. The onset potential (Eonset), directs the HER to start in the system. In addition, ηj is the overpotential under a specific cathodic current density of ∼10 mA cm−2 and jd is the diffusion-limiting current density. The Eonset, and ηj can be used to estimate the effort of the HER reaction. In general, a lower onset potential, lower overpotential with more negative current designates a more rapid HER reaction. The HER mechanism is reflected by the Tafel slope;148,416 where the current (I) vs. potential (V) has an exponential manner, which may be written as eqn (41) as the supposed Tafel equation (below shown for cathodic currents) j = j0e−(η/b)(41)
 Fig. 25  Schematic of experimental setups and corresponding results of (a) electrocatalytic hydrogen generation (b) photocatalytic powder solution based hydrogen generation and (c) photoelectrochemical water splitting. 
In the above equation, the current density is represented as j, exchange current density is j0, Tafel slope is b, and η is the overpotential. Notably, an ideal catalyst gives the highest currents at the smallest overpotential; it can be perceived from the Tafel calculation if an electrocatalyst has a low Tafel slope (b) and a large exchange current density j0. The significance of the Tafel slope can be predicted when one recalls the different orders of magnitude of the HER currents that both the electrocatalytic and PEC hydrogen evolution involve.417 If the Tafel slope is too high, the catalyst needs substantial overpotentials to generate the required currents. Importantly, the Tafel slope depends on several factors, including the reaction pathway and the active sites adsorption conditions as well as the material synthetic methods of the materials. In order to enhance the hydrogen generation production rate, sacrificial agents are often used.418–420

5.2 HER in powder-based solution photocatalysts
Recently, photocatalytic H2 generation reactions at a semiconductor powder photocatalysts dispersed in aqueous solution have gained interest, since it is a promising technology that could offer a simple, low-cost and safe procedure. The photocatalytic water splitting mechanism is an uphill reaction, and the Gibbs free energy upturns by 237 kJ mol−1.95,421,422 The energy required to drive photocatalytic water splitting is conveyed by external light, preferably sunlight, as shown in Fig. 25(b). When an optimum amount of photocatalysts with a precise set of properties is dispersed in an aqueous electrolyte and exposed with external light, photon (light) energy is converted into electrochemical energy423 that can split water into usable hydrogen and oxygen. Accordingly, intermittent solar energy is then converted into an inherently more storable form of energy within chemical bonds. Upon the irradiation of light, the powder photocatalysts absorbs light photons with energies higher than its band gap energy, and electrons and holes are produced in the semiconducting photocatalyst particles through band-gap excitation. Subsequently, the photoexcited charge carriers are transported to surface active sites and are then consumed by surface redox reactions in the liquid (electrolyte) solution, where they engage in oxidation and reduction reactions. Moreover, mass diffusion of reactants and products must also continue. In addition, photoexcited carrier recombination also occurs along with these reactions. Hence, for efficient hydrogen evolution via water splitting, the separation of charge in photocatalyst particles and the redox reactions on their surface must progress within the lifetimes of the photoexcited carriers. To complete photocatalytic water splitting using a photocatalyst, the band gap of the material must straddle the reduction and oxidation potentials of water. Finally, the amounts of evolved gases (O2 and H2) were measured through gas chromatography with respect to reaction time, as shown in Fig. 25(b).
5.3 Photoelectrochemical (PEC) water splitting

Fig. 25(c) illustrates the basic experimental setup in a photoelectrochemical (PEC) device. A three-electrode system comprises of a single absorber photoelectrode, whereas, if the photoelectrode was a photocathode (p-type materials) it favors the HER reaction and if the photoanode (n-type materials) is involved it favors the OER reaction.1,424,425 PEC water splitting is relatively complex and it is the combination of above said two systems; see Sections 5.1 and 5.2. When a photoelectrode is immersed in an electrolyte solution and irradiated with light, electrons in the valence band jump into the conduction band, leaving holes in the valence band, whereas electron and holes transfer takes place among the semiconductor photocatalysts and the electrolyte solution. As a result, the excited electrons can drive the H+ reduction reaction to generate H2, where the holes can drive H2O oxidation reaction to produce oxygen (O2). In photoanodes, upon light irradiation, photoexcited holes accumulate on the surface of the semiconductor and favor the OER reactions, see Fig. 25(c), whereas, the electrons are transported to a counter electrode and further used in reduction reactions. For efficient production of oxygen, the valence band of the photoanodes photocatalyst need to be more positive than the OER potential.426 As shown in Fig. 25, a p-type photocatalyst works as a photocathode for efficient hydrogen evolution when the conduction band edge needs to be more negative than the H2 evolution potential. Therefore, PEC reactions on photoelectrodes are determined by photoexcited minority carriers in both cases. Additionally, an external voltage can be applied between a counter electrode and photoelectrode to alter the potential to drive redox reactions on a counter electrode even if the photoelectrode's Fermi level is located at an undesirable potential.
5.4 Description of efficiencies

5.4.1 Solar-to-hydrogen (STH) conversion efficiency. 
The efficiency of energy conversion is an essential factor for any water splitting system, either for a photocatalytic or PEC water splitting cell. The usual solar-to-hydrogen (STH) conversion efficiency (ηSTH) can be stated as the ratio between the total energy produced and the input energy from standard sunlight irradiation (AM 1.5G, 100 mW cm−2) and they are as follows,427,428 (42)where ΔG is the Gibbs free energy (∼237 kJ mol−1), rH is the rate of hydrogen evolution in moles per second; Isun is the intensity of incident light (1 sun power ∼100 mW cm−2) and A is the illuminated photoelectrode area (cm2).


5.4.2 Faradaic efficiency. 
As estimation of faradaic efficiency is the most valuable way to validate that the generated photocurrent is in fact due to water splitting and not photocorrosion of the electrodes or other side reactions. This is defined as the proportion of the actual evolved gas divided by the theoretically evolved gas made on the measured photocurrent. (43) (44) (45)The unit of gas evolution/produced is moles; Jphoto is the photocurrent density (A cm−2) produced during the measurement time T (s); A is the light exposed photoelectrode area (cm2); e is the electron charge (1.602 × 10−19 C) and NA is the Avogadro constant (6.02 × 1023 mol−1). The amounts of evolved gases can be measured using gas chromatography (GC) or, in the case of oxygen, by using an appropriate oxygen electrode or fluorescence detector.


5.4.3 Incident photon-to-current efficiency (IPCE). 
The incident photon-to-current efficiency (IPCE) can be measured at fixed incident wavelengths. The IPCE may define as the number of photogenerated charge carriers involved in the photocurrent per incident photon, which can be denoted by the following equation: (46) (47)where Jphoto is the photocurrent density at that specific wavelength of incident light (mA cm−2); e is the electron charge (1.602 × 10−19 C); h is the Planck's constant (6.626 × 10−34 J s); c is the speed of light (3.0 × 108 × 109 nm s−1); λ is incident light wavelength (nm); I(λ) is the incident light intensity at that particular wavelength (mW cm−2).
In the IPCE calculation, optical losses such as transmission and reflection are ignored. In order to rectify these optical losses, the absorbed photon-to-current conversion efficiency (APCE) can be used to define the quantum efficiency. The APCE is defined as the number of photogenerated charge carriers involving in the photocurrent per absorbed photon, which is called the internal quantum efficiency, and can be stated by the following equation:429,430 (48)where A, R, T are the optical absorption, reflection, and transmission, respectively.


5.4.4 Apparent quantum yield (AQY or QE). 
The quantity of evolved hydrogen and oxygen can be measured using a gas chromatograph. The fuel gas evolution rate is classically assessed by the amount of fuel per unit time. The apparent quantum yield (QE) is normally used to estimate the performance of photocatalysts as follows:429 (49) (50)


5.4.5 Solar-to-chemical energy conversion efficiency. 
In a PEC system, the photoelectrode is made of a semiconducting photocatalysts, which is sustained on a conductive substrate as the working electrode (p-type photocatalyst as the cathode and an n-type photocatalyst as the anode). In contrast to the particulate method, the photocatalytic reduction and oxidization reactions occur on different electrodes. For instance, if we use an n-type semiconductor photoanode for overall water splitting in a PEC system, in which hydrogen is produced at the cathode while oxygen is generated at the photoanode surface. Therefore, the reaction products could be collected in separate chambers. Furthermore, a PEC is a good tool for investigating the photoinduced charge separation and charge transfer processes in semiconducting materials, the reaction kinetics and the mechanism at the semiconductor/electrolyte interface. Hence, the efficiency of separate photoelectrode can be calculated as:425 (51)where J is the measured photocurrent density (mA cm−2), Vredox and Vapp are the redox potential of a reaction, (Vredox = 1.23 V) and the applied external bias (Vapp) respectively, Ilight is the measured intensity solar radiation (mW cm−2). This efficiency is also called as applied bias photon-to-current efficiency (ABPE).
In addition, the photocurrent is a function of the incident wavelength, and thus the incident photon-to-electron conversion efficiency (IPCE) is a more appropriate factor to describe the photoconversion efficiency, which can be written as;36 (52)where λ is the incident light wavelength (nm), J is the photocurrent density and Iλ is the irradiated light power at specific wavelength (mW cm−2).

6. Electrochemical hydrogen generation using metal sulfides
6.1 Hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) enabled by mono-metal sulfides
Typically, hydrogen generation through the electrochemical method depends on the use of platinum metal as a well-known HER catalyst. Alternative materials are sought to advance cost- effective, abundant and highly effective HER catalysts.431 Recent advances in material research have identified that metal sulfides, particularly MoS2,414,432–434 WS2,435,436 CoS,437,438 FeS,438 and NiS439 are potentially low-cost alternatives to Pt for the generation of H2 from water by electrochemical reactions.

6.1.1 MoS2 and WS2. 
Hinnemann et al.440 recognized the potential of using the edge sites of MoS2 as a hydrogen evolution catalyst. In principle, when the free energy of adsorbed atomic hydrogen is near to the reactant or product i.e., , a material is potentially a good catalyst for HER reactions.145,441 Similarly, Jaramillo et al.414 reported that the HER activity of MoS2 nanocrystals is proportional to the edge length of the crystals, but not the surface area. This was verified by both experimental and theoretical calculations, as the preferential exposure of edge sites resulted in more active MoS2 electrocatalysts.432,442 In addition, amorphous MoS2 is a highly active and versatile catalysts for HER in acidic solutions. The enhanced HER activity of amorphous MoS2 was thought to be mainly due to the short-range atomic arrangements.443,444 Merki et al.445 reported that the amorphous MoS2 film can function in water and is well-suited for a wide pH range from 0–13. Significantly, the amorphous catalyst can achieve a geometric current density of 15 mA cm−2 at a small overpotential of ∼200 mV. It also exhibited a low Tafel slope of ∼40 mV dec−1, which is lower than that of MoS2 nanoparticles, and is among the most active low-cost HER catalyst they prepared. Due to the amorphous nature, MoS2 films could retain more unsaturated active sites on the edges than single crystalline and nanoparticles of MoS2.
Liang et al.446 prepared ultrathin nanoflakes of WS2 by a high temperature solution method, which possessed a monolayer thickness and abundant edges. The enhanced HER activity of this catalyst under an acid medium was due to structural features that provided a low overpotential of 100 mV and a Tafel slope of 48 mV dec−1. Through appropriate compositional and structural engineering, this WS2 catalyst was able to catalyze hydrogen generation at low overpotential of ∼100 mV with outstanding durability in acidic environment. By exfoliation, WS2 nanodots were prepared by Zhao et al.447 where a high concentration of octahedral phase (1T) of WS2 nanodots was responsible for the enhanced catalytic HER activities with a low onset potential ∼90 mV and small Tafel slope of ∼51 mV dec−1. This performance is superior to the hexagonal (2H) WS2 nanodots.


6.1.2 CoxSy, FexSy, and NixSy. 
Interestingly, within the group of transition metal sulfides, iron disulfide (FeS2), cobalt disulfide (CoS2), and nickel disulfide (NiS2) are extensively investigated. Recently, Faber et al.438 provided a comparative HER analysis on FeS2, CoS2, NiS2, and their alloys, they are shown in Fig. 26(i). Their performance, as distinguished by their ability to enable high catalytic current densities at small overpotentials tends to increase with increasing cobalt content, with CoS2 being superior, suggesting an important role of cobalt in facilitating the HER. In addition, they compared the polysulfide reduction mechanism of FeS2, NiS2, and their alloys to that of CoS2 and identified that NiS2 possessed a particularly high activity. Later, the same research group353 reported a high HER performance of nanowires of CoS2, where they exhibited current densities of 10 mA cm−2 at low overpotentials of −145 mV vs. RHE, while microwire CoS2 had an overpotential of −158 mV vs. RHE, respectively as shown in Fig. 26(ii). An increased operational stability and cycling durability of the CoS2 was credited to its micro- and nano-structural effects. Similarly, CoS films showed high current densities at a low potential with near 100% faradaic efficiency in a neutral pH and also revealed an excellent HER activity in 1.0 M KOH solution.448 However, it was observed that, under strong acid solution of 0.5 M H2SO4, the CoS film rapidly dissolved with a decreased HER performance. In addition, uniform needle arrays of CoS2 were grown on a titanium mesh to investigate the urea assisted electrolytic H2 production.449 The nano arrays grown on a titanium mesh afforded more exposed catalytic active sites, thus an excellent HER activity at a voltage of ∼1.59 V for 10 mA cm−2 in 1.0 M KOH with 0.3 M urea solution was observed. Furthermore, an electrodeposited cobalt sulfide film450 performed as a robust HER catalyst in neutral pH water, as well as other aqueous media, achieving a low overpotential of 43 mV, 100% faradaic efficiency, Tafel slope of 93 mV dec−1 and high current densities of 50 mA cm−2 that could operate for at least 40 h. In addition, the amorphous CoSx catalyst451 required only ∼83 mV overpotential for an operational current of ∼2 mA cm−2. It was believed that disulfide anions S22− on CoS2 surfaces play an important role for the HER reaction, as in MoS2. However, after electrolysis for 3 h, the Co:S ratio was analyzed by EDS and decreased ratio from 1.4:1 to 0.98:1, indicating that a fraction of the cobalt had dissolved into the electrolyte.
 Fig. 26  (i) Electrochemical analysis of FeS2 (black traces and circle markers), CoS2 (red traces and square markers), NiS2 (blue traces and down-pointing triangles), PyS2 (green traces and up-pointing triangles), (Fe0.48Co0.52)S2 (orange traces and thin diamond markers), and (Co0.59Ni0.41)S2 (violet traces and diamond markers) thin films prepared on graphite disk substrates toward the HER. Polarization curves (corrected for background currents and IR losses) over (a) wider and (b) narrower ranges of current density comparing the performance of the pyrite-phase electrocatalysts. The dashed trace presents the NiS2 data normalized by its relative surface area. (c) Tafel analysis of the data presented in panel a, with the specific Tafel slopes given in the figure legend, and (d) plot showing the method of extracting the double layer capacitance (Cdl) for each electrode. Reproduced from ref. 438 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2014. (ii) Electrochemical characterization of CoS2 film, microwire (MW) array, and nanowire (NW) array electrodes for HER electrocatalysis. Polarization curves at (a) higher, (b) lower applied overpotentials display the relative activity of the CoS2 electrodes (film, open circles; MW, open squares; NW, open diamonds) in comparison to a standard Pt wire (open triangles) and (c) schematic of hydrogen gas evolution on the surface of CoS2 NWs and MWs full the larger H2(gas) bubbles and maintaining the solid–liquid interface. Reproduced from ref. 353 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2014. 
Using a 2D Ni(OH)2 template, NiS2 nanosheets on a nickel foam were prepared by Ma et al.,452 as shown in Fig. 27(i). The NiS2 nanosheets exhibited an activation period during HER with a prominent structural and compositional change in an alkaline environment. Surprisingly, the NiS2 nanosheets were converted into ultrathin metallic nickel nanosheets under an alkaline environment, which was confirmed by XPS spectra. Moreover, during HER, the nickel acted as a catalytic active species with low overpotential of 67 mV to reach 10 mA cm−2, see Fig. 28(i). Likewise, flakes of Ni3S2/NF were prepared by a hydrothermal method.453 The Ni3S2/NF provided a current density of ∼10 mA cm−2 at a lower overpotential of −223 mV for HER in a basic solution. The result showed that Ni3S2/NF retains its catalytic activity for at least 200 h. Based on the moles of Ni in Ni3S2 after 200 h, a total turnover number of 1.87 × 103 was found. The Ni3S2/NF showed an excellent stability after ∼10000 cycles. Finally, after several recycling tests, the final morphology and microstructure of the Ni3S2/NF were also intact, as shown in Fig. 27(ii). Ho et al.454 indicated that ALD deposited Ni3S2 thin films on Au and SiO2 can act as an excellent cathode and anode in an alkaline environment. Without thermal annealing, the crystalline Ni3S2 layers exhibited highly efficient and stable performance for the HER with a low overpotential of ∼300 mV and a high turnover frequency with a stable operation for 100 h at a current density of 10 mA cm−2. The fabrication of an amorphous interface in an interwoven NiS/NiS2 structure was reported by Li et al.455 Such an amorphous interface consisted of an intermediate state species in the conversion of NiS2 to NiS, and reduced the interfacial energy for rapid ion transport, which resulted in a geometrical current density of 100 mA cm−2 at a small overpotential of 248 mV. It was observed that the amorphous interface in NiS/NiS2 was expected to separate the H* and H− intermediates onto dissimilar interface sites, and reduce the interfacial energy and charge-transfer resistance.
 Fig. 27  (i) HER performance of NF-NiS2 catalyst (a) chronopotentiometry (CA) at a constant cathodic current density of 20 mA cm−2 of NF-NiS2; inset is a Linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) of NF-NiS2 at the first, 10th and 100th cycle with a scan rate of 10 mV s−1. (b) LSV of NF-Ni(OH)2, NF-NiS2, NF-NiS2-A and Pt/C in 1 M KOH (IR corrected), (c) Tafel plots of the polarization curves in b and (d) double-layer capacitance of NF-Ni(OH)2, NF-NiS2 and NF-NiS2-A. Reproduced from ref. 452 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2017. (ii) (a) HER activity at pH 7 over nickel foam (NF), Ni3S2 nanoparticles, Ni3S2/NF, and 20 wt% Pt/C, (b) efficiency of H2 production over Ni3S2/NF (electrode area: 0.7 cm × 1 cm) at a current density of ∼20 mA cm−2, measured for 70 min, (c) most stable planes (20) (c) and (001) (d) surfaces of Ni3S2. (e) calculated free-energy diagram of HER over (20) and (001) surfaces at equilibrium potential. The blue and yellow spheres signify Ni and S atoms, respectively, of Ni3S2. Reproduced from ref. 453 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2015. 
 Fig. 28  TEM images of (a) hexagon and (b) nearly “half-hexagon” shape for individual 1T-VS2 nanosheets, (c and d) are the optical images of hexagon and nearly “half-hexagon” shaped VS2 nanosheets. Electrocatalytic HER performance of 1T-VS2 crystals, (e) LSV curves after iR-correction, (f) Tafel plots, (g) stability and (h) Nyquist plots after 12000 cycles of VS2 crystals. Reproduced from ref. 461 with permission from Wiley-VCH, copyright 2015. 
The structure dependent active sites by both experimental and theoretical results affirmed that, owing to the strong affinity of hydrogen, NiS has a higher activity than Ni3S.456 For Ni3S2 nanoparticles, the rate determining step is the Volmer step whereas for NiS it is the mixed Volmer and Heyrovsky reaction, as proven by Tafel analysis. It was estimated that regulating the facet or nanoparticle of the nickel sulfide can provide potential candidates for HER electrocatalysts. Recently, Yu et al.377 reported NiS frame-like nanostructures for electrochemical reactions in alkaline electrolyte. The HER activity of NiS nanoframes was significantly higher than bare nickel foam. NiS nanoframes with a size of 100 nm had overpotential of ∼94 mV to drive a current density of 10 mA cm−2, whereas 300 and 600 nm nanoframes required a higher overpotential of 115 mV and 148 mV, respectively to reach 10 mA cm−2. Moreover, the Tafel slope of the 100 nm NiS nanoframes was ∼131 mV dec−1 and this was smaller than bare nickel foam ∼146 mV dec−1, indicating facile electrochemical reaction kinetics by smaller sized NiS nanoframes with higher specific surface area.


6.1.3 Other mono-metal sulfides. 
The optimization of active electronic sites is important for developing highly active catalysts, since thermodynamics itself may not form an optimal electronic state. Through intrinsic charge engineering, auto-optimization of HER activity of ReS2 were investigated by Zhou et al.457 Their experimental and theoretical results exposed that intrinsic charge compensation from S to Re–Re bonds could influence the active electronic states, allowing hydrogen to absorb on the active sites weakly nor strongly. In addition, in ReS2, the two S sites exhibited optimal H2 adsorption free energies ΔGH* of 0.016 and 0.061 eV, which are nearby to zero equivalent to the maximum HER activity. This auto-optimization by charge engineering was further verified by a higher turnover frequency per sulfur atom of 1–10 s−1 and smaller overpotential of −147 mV at 10 mA cm−2.
Monolayer VS2 has the potential to exhibit a higher HER activity than other metal sulfides.458,459 Additionally, it has been shown that the catalytic activity of VS2 nanostructures depends on edge structure and configuration.460 To examine this, hexagon and nearly half hexagon structured single crystalline VS2 nanosheets, as shown in Fig. 28, were prepared via CVD on a SiO2/Si substrate for HER.461 During HER testing, VS2 showed an extremely low overpotential of −68 mV (at 10 mA cm−2), approaching that of −43 mV for platinum and also small Tafel slopes of ∼34 mV dec−1, along with good stability, signifying its potential as a candidate for non-noble metal catalyst for the HER, particularly since the overpotential was lower than for other 2D structures such as 2H-, 1T-MoS2158,414 and 2H-, 1T-WS2.145 In addition, the 1T-VS2 catalyst only required a small overpotential of −0.34 V to achieve 100 mA cm−2. The high activity of VS2 possibly originated from the robust activity of both basal plane and edge sites.
Low dimensional nanostructures are of great interest for HER owing to their large surface area and rich edge sites. A facile hydrothermal method was employed to grow vertical VS2 nanoplates with high density on carbon paper.462 The nanoplate arrays of VS2 showed a high HER active nature with a low Tafel slope of 36 mV dec−1, a high geometric exchange current density of ∼0.955 mA cm−2, and a small overpotential of 42 mV at a standard current density of 10 mA cm−2 in 0.5 M H2SO4. Additionally, the VS2 nanoplates exhibited long-term stability toward the HER. The direct contact between VS2 and carbon paper (i.e. current collector) possibly enhanced charge carrier transport, and the array structure promoted the release of evolved hydrogen gas bubbles from the catalyst surface and enhanced the HER performance. Qu et al.463 fabricated 1T-VS2 nanoflowers via a facile hydrothermal approach. In acid environments, the prepared VS2 nanoflowers showed a similar HER performance to Pt, with an ultra-low onset potential of ∼32 mV, and a Tafel slope of ∼34 mV dec−1. High long-term stability and almost 100% faradaic efficiency indicates the potential practical application of VS2 nanoflowers in the HER. From a first principles analysis, it was observed that thermoneutral Gibbs free energy of hydrogen adsorption on both the basal and edge sites and the conductivity of the 1T-VS2 nanoflowers are key factors for increased HER performance. Theoretical simulations have indicated the potential of TaS2 as an active and stable electrocatalyst. Shi et al.464 prepared ultrathin 2H-TaS2 films on gold foil by low pressure CVD (LPCVD). Notably, the values of ΔGH* for Ta-edge, S-edge, and basal-plane were calculated to be −0.04, −0.10, and 0.15 eV, respectively. These results specified that the active sites of 2H-TaS2 are focused both at the edges and in the basal-planes, which is in sharp contrast with MoS2 that has inert surface catalytic properties. As shown in Fig. 29, 2H-TaS2 flakes with different thicknesses revealed a current density of 10 mA cm−2, the overpotentials (η) of 2H-TaS2 films were 65–150 mV, yielding Tafel slopes of 31, 33–42, and 110 mV dec−1 for Pt, 2H-TaS2/Au and Au foil, respectively, indicating the exceptional HER activity of 2H-TaS2. It is notable that the Tafel slope ∼33 mV dec−1 for 2H-TaS2, is close to that of platinum ∼31 mV dec−1 and exceeded those reported for metal sulfides. Furthermore, the electrocatalytic performances of mono-metal sulfides suggest that alloying and/or composite formation could be an effective method to improve the electrocatalytic activity of metal sulfides, which can increase their areal density of exposed active sites for catalysis.
 Fig. 29  (a) 2H-TaS2 hexagonal flakes fabrication by APCVD (b–e) microscopic images and SAED pattern of 2H-TaS2 samples, (f–h) possible HER mechanism and Gibbs free energy analysis, (i) LSV polarization curves for 2H-TaS2 electrocatalysts and their corresponding Tafel plot (j). Reproduced from ref. 464 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2017. 

6.2 HER capacitated by bi-metal sulfides
It is believed that bi-metallic sulfides can demonstrate improved intrinsic HER activity compared to binary systems or mono-metallic sulfides, these will now be discussed.

6.2.1 MoSx and WSx based bi-metal sulfides. 
The abundant and cost-effective inorganic catalyst based on Cu2MoS4 was reported by Tran et al.465 Cu2MoS4 displayed a highly active nature in aqueous electrolyte solutions over a wide range of pH values from 0–7 with only a low ∼135 mV overpotential, an apparent exchange current density of ∼0.040 mA cm−2 and a Tafel slope of ∼95 mV dec−1, irrespective of the pH value. This Cu2MoS4 catalyst was found to be highly stable through electrocatalytic HER and it is believed that this catalyst will be an attractive alternative to platinum. Moreover, ternary sulfides of nickel tungsten sulfide (NiWSx) and cobalt tungsten sulfide (CoWSx) which have WS2-like layered assemblies and comprise MII–S–WIV (M = Co or Ni) bi-metallic sulfide centres.466 These MWSx materials are highly electroactive for the HER with only a modest overpotential, as shown in Fig. 30. The catalytic activities were centered on MIIS clusters whereas the WS2 framework is expected to be related to the role of a coordination ligand that stabilizes and adjusts HER activity. Under a neutral pH based hydrogen evolution, NiWSx, CoWSx and CoMoSx catalysts showed a low overpotential of 165, 95 and 75 mV respectively.466 Moreover, values for Tafel slope were determined to be ∼96 mV dec−1 for NiWSx, ∼78 mV dec−1 for CoWSx, and ∼85 mV dec−1 for CoMoSx, as previously reported for amorphous MoS2 and M-promoted MoS2 catalysts ∼86–96 mV dec−1,467 and this was indicative of substrate mass-transport limitation within the catalytic films. In addition, the exchange current densities were calculated to be ∼10−5.66 A cm−2 for NiWSx and ∼10−5.25 A cm−2 for CoWSx and the CoMoSx showed 10−5.89 A cm−2. The MS centres and Co as the second metal (M) resulted in improvement of both the intrinsic catalytic properties and active electrochemical surface area, as a result, CoWSx displayed improved catalytic performance.
 Fig. 30  (a) HER catalytic current achieved with CoSx (black star curve), NiWSx (red triangle curve), CoWSx (blue circle curve) and CoMoSx (olive square curve) electrodes. Lower limit turnover frequency normalized per mole of deposited materials: CoSx (black bulk curve), NiWSx (red bulk curve), CoWSx (blue bulk curve) and CoMoSx (olive bulk curve). Lower limit turnover frequency resulted from normalization per relative electrochemical surface area of CoWSx (blue dashed curve) and CoMoSx (olive dashed curve) electrodes compared with the NiWSx electrode were also plotted. (b) Tafel plots for these catalysts. Reproduced from ref. 466 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2013. 
Similarly, the HER activity in an acid electrolyte of a new ternary bi-metallic sulfide of Ag2WS4 was reported by Zhan et al.255 It is remarkable that the Ag2WS4 showed an onset potential of ∼−212 mV (vs. RHE) and a low overpotential of 329 mV for a current density of 10 mA cm−2 with a Tafel slope of ∼62 mV dec−1. The results demonstrated that the enhanced performance was attributed to its unique chemical composition, large electrochemically active surface area, and high electric conductivity compared to bare WS2 and Ag2S. Due to the synergistic benefits of bi-metallic sulfides as well as interwoven carbon nanosheets for efficient mass/charge transport, the NiWS/CF electrode exhibited an excellent HER performance in different pH media.468 For example, under acid and alkaline environments the NiWS/CF revealed a low overpotential of ∼56 mV and ∼38 mV to generate 10 mA cm−2, which is much smaller than NiS2/CF, WS2/CF and NiO/CF. Recently, several materials including CoMoS4,469,470 CoMoS3,469 and NiMoS4469 have been widely investigated in acidic or alkaline media due to their promising HER activity, low cost, simple preparation, and high stability.


6.2.2 M1−xCoxSy (M = Ni, Fe, Cu) based bi-metal sulfides. 
The chemical composition of an electrocatalysts has a large impact on its electrochemical performance. Tie et al.471 prepared different compositions and mixed phases of NiCo2S4 produced by a solvothermal method. The electrochemical measurements showed that NiCo2S4 with a theoretical Ni/Co molar ratio of 1:2 (NCS-1) demonstrated a superior HER performance in 1 M KOH aqueous electrolyte with a lower overpotential of 282 mV for a current density of 10 mA cm−2. The enhanced performance was mainly due to the flake-like structure, optimal chemical composition and relatively small resistance. A recent approach for direct growth of 1D NW arrays of NiCo2S4 on a nickel foam (NF) was described by Sivanantham et al.405 The binder-free NiCo2S4 NW/NF electrode provided a HER current density of ∼10 mA cm−2 at a small overpotential of 210 mV vs. RHE in 1 M KOH. The nickel foam assists in the directional growth, revealing more active sites of the NiCo2S4 for electrocatalytic reactions at the electrode–electrolyte interface. Peng et al.472 reported the fabrication of NWs of Ni0.33Co0.67S2 on titanium foil via sulfuration of a NiCo2O4 precursor. The NWs of Ni0.33Co0.67S2 had a high HER activity in 0.5 M H2SO4, with a low overpotential of ∼73, 87, and 118 mV vs. RHE required to achieve HER current densities of 10, 20, and 100 mA cm−2, respectively. These values are smaller than those for NiS2 and CoS2, however due to the dissolution of sulfides in 0.5 M H2SO4 the HER current generated by Ni0.33Co0.67S2 rapidly diminished.
Kong et al.439 prepared iron cobalt sulfides with different compositions where Fe0.43Co0.57S2 demonstrated the best HER activity and long-term stability in acid compared to other sulfides such as Ni0.68Co0.32S2, FeS2, CoS2 and NiS2. Importantly, the HER activity of Ni0.68Co0.32S2 was lower than FeS2, CoS2 and NiS2. Hence, the optimization of chemical composition is essential to achieve more electrochemical active sites. Similarly, a ternary catalyst of FexCo1−xSy was prepared using the ALD method.473 Compositional optimization indicated that Fe0.54Co0.46S0.92 was the best arrangement for enhanced HER activity, and it was then modified to form a Fe0.54Co0.46S0.92/CNTs/CC electrode. The modified electrode exhibited a low HER overpotential of ∼−70 mV at a current density of 10 mA cm−2 in an alkaline solution, which demonstrated the efficiency of the ALD-based structural engineering approach. Yang et al.474 reported the in situ hydrothermal sulfidization of a hierarchical nanoarray of CuCo2S4 on copper foam (CuCo2S4/CF) from a precursor of CuCo2–OH nanowire array. The as-prepared CuCo2S4/CF showed superior HER activity in 1.0 M KOH, with overpotentials of only ∼259 and ∼295 mV to achieve current densities of 60 and 100 mA cm−2 respectively. Furthermore, a high turnover frequency with strong electrochemical durability values of 0.069 and 0.390 mol s−1 O2 at overpotentials of 300 and 400 mV were achieved, respectively.

6.3 Strategies for enhancing HER performance
As shown in Scheme 2, several strategies have beed widely used to advance the mono-metal or bi-metal sulfide systems. They are discussed in detail below.
 Scheme 2  Systematic of approaches to improve the activity of metal sulfide systems. 

6.3.1 Effects of constructing heterostructures. 
Research groups have investigated the advancement of low-cost and highly efficient electrocatalysts for HER.461,475–480 Liu et al.481 reported the combined experimental and density functional theory (DFT) to examine a NiCo2S4@NiFe heterostructure interface for enhanced water-splitting activity. Using the facile hydrothermal method, NiCo2S4 nanotubes, NiFe LDH nanosheets, and NiCo2S4@NiFe LDH heterostructures were prepared on nickel foam, of which the corresponding experimental HER performance exhibited little enhancement, with a small overpotential of ∼200 mV at 10 mA cm−2. The DFT results revealed that the electron-rich region of NiCo2S4 and a hole-rich region of NiFe LDH sheet, initiating the movement of the Fermi energy. As a result, the interface between NiFe LDH and NiCo2S4 has substantial effects on the activity of electrocatalysts. Significantly, a 3D heteromorphic NiCo2S4/Ni3S2 nanosheet network has been made on nickel foam (NiCo2S4/Ni3S2/NF) using a NiCo-layered hydroxide precursor.482 The resultant NiCo2S4/Ni3S2/NF electrode exhibited an excellent electrocatalytic HER performance with a low onset overpotential of ∼15 mV at 10 mV cm−2 and long-term durability in an alkaline solution. In addition, the Tafel slope of NiCo2S4/Ni3S2/NF electrode was ∼105.2 mV dec−1, which was smaller than those of NiCo-LDH/NF (∼142.3 mV dec−1), NiCo2S4/NF (∼147.3 mV dec−1), Ni3S2/NF (∼156.5 mV dec−1), and bare Ni foam (∼196.5 mV dec−1). Such an improved HER activity was accredited to (i) the open active sites provided by the mixed transition metal sulfides (NiCo2S4 and Ni3S2), (ii) the robust interfacial contact at the NiCo2S4/Ni3S2 heterojunction interfaces with the bound H binding, and (iii) the highly porous and conductive substrate of the NF with improved electron transfer.
Owing to the synergistic effect of facile electron transfer between CdS, MoO3 and Ni3S2/NF and the well-designed structure, as shown in Fig. 31(i), an ultrathin CdS layered MoO3/Ni3S2 hierarchical nanoarray on a NF resulted in an excellent low overpotential of ∼30 mV at −10 mA cm−2, which exceeded the existing non-noble electrocatalysts.483 MoS2/Ni3S2 nanorod arrays that were highly-aligned on an a nickel foam (MoS2/Ni3S2/NF) acted as a novel 3D hierarchical electrode.484 Each Ni3S2 nanorod had a MoS2 coated structure, with large surface contact regions that provided outstanding catalytic activity and good stability for the HER. Aided by a decoratively designed architecture, the MoS2/Ni3S2/NF exhibited a catalytic current density of 10, 100, 200, and 300 mA cm−2, it only required 187, 274, 300, and 320 mV overpotentials respectively for HER, better than a benchmark combination. In addition, the 1D MoS2/Ni3S2 nanoarrays were grown on nickel foam by Cao et al.485 The MoS2/Ni3S2 Ni composite had an onset potential ∼13 mV in 1 M KOH, which was similar to a Pt-based electrocatalyst for HER. Moreover, the functional overpotential at 10 mA cm−2 is ∼76 mV for the 3D MoS2/Ni3S2/Ni hybrid composite, which are lower than those of the Ni3S2/Ni nanostructure (∼260 mV) and nickel foam (∼350 mV).
 Fig. 31  (i) (a) SEM images of (a) MoO3/Ni3S2/NF, (inset: Ni3S2/NF not covered by MoO3), (b) CdS/MoO3/Ni3S2/NF and (c) the corresponding elemental mapping of CdS/MoO3/Ni3S2/NF, (d) cathodic polarization curves of the prepared samples. The inset highlights the overpotentials consistent to −10 mA cm−2 (e) estimation of Cdl by plotting the current density variation vs. scan rate to fit a linear regression, (f) Impedance spectra of NF, CdS–NF, Ni3S2/NF, CdS–Ni3S2/NF, MoO3/Ni3S2/NF and CdS–MoO3/Ni3S2/NF at a potential of −0.2 V vs. RHE and (g) long-term durability of CdS–MoO3/Ni3S2/NF at 30 mV vs. RHE. Reproduced from ref. 483 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2018. (ii) (a) XRD patterns of hydrothermally produced and CoMn-S@NiO/CC. SEM images of (b and c) hydrothermal product and (d and e) CoMn–S@NiO/CC. (f) EDS mapping images of Co, Mn, S, and Ni for CoMn–S@NiO/CC. (g) TEM and (h) HRTEM images of CoMn–S@NiO (i) HER activity for CoMn–S@NiO/CC, CoMn–S/CC, NiO/CC, Pt/C, and bare CC in 1.0 M KOH solution with a scan rate of 2 mV s−1, and (j) corresponding Tafel plots. Reproduced from ref. 487 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2016. 
Recently, effort has been made to improve the electrocatalytic activity of MoS2 through creating more active sites and enhancing the charge transfer rate. Chen et al.486 demonstrated the sulfur concentration effect on MoS2, where the metallic phase transition of VO2 showed a significant effect on hydrogen evolution properties of a MoS2/VO2 hybrid heterostructure. Upon the insulator to metal phase transition of VO2, the MoS2/VO2 hybrid exhibited a low onset potential and small Tafel slope of ∼99 mV dec−1 and 85 mV dec−1, respectively. This was accredited to the improved charge transport ability and interfacial strain effect on modifying the electronic structure of MoS2 and increasing the conductivity. In addition, an array of 3D self-supported nickel-oxide-coated cobalt manganese sulfide nanosheets on a carbon cloth (CoMn–S@NiO/CC) was used as an efficient electrocatalysts for HER in alkaline media.487 The performance of CoMn–S@NiO/CC was improved through the HER current densities of ∼100 and 200 mA cm−2 at low overpotentials of ∼232 and 278 mV, respectively in 1.0 M KOH, with long-term stability for hydrogen production; see Fig. 31(ii). Such a high HER activity for CoMn–S@NiO/CC was a result of Co ions, which improves the amorphous nature thereby increasing the electrocatalytic active surface area. Since hierarchical nanostructured materials are of interest for energy storage and harvesting, V2O5@Ni3S2 was prepared by Zhong et al.488 Owing to the structural features and coupling effect of the V2O5@Ni3S2 interface and V–S active bonding between vanadium pentoxide and nickel sulfide the V2O5@Ni3S2 catalyst has an overpotential of 95 mV at 10 mA cm−2 with a low Tafel value of 107.9 mV dec−1 in 1 M KOH.
Decorated metal sulfides are also of interest for energy materials applications. Recently, CdS quantum dot (QD) decorated Ni3S2 flowers were deposited on a plasma-treated nickel foam (PNF i.e. NiO).489 The NiO created during the plasma treatment showed a rich number of active sites, which plays a significant role in alkaline based HER. Specifically, a current density of 10 mA cm−2 was attained for the HER with a ∼121 mV overpotential with Tafel slope of ∼110 mV dec−1, when the working electrode was 1 mM CdS/Ni3S2/PNF catalyst in 1 M KOH. In addition, a WO3·2H2O/WS2 hybrid catalyst490 revealed excellent HER performance with a current density of 100 mA cm−2 at a 152 mV overpotential with a Tafel slope of ∼54 mV dec−1. Recently, by adjusting the amount of WS2, Zhou et al.491 prepared highly active 3D hierarchical CoS2 nanosheets combined with WS2 (CoS2@WS2) via a one-step sulfurization method. It was established that the creation of nano-interfaces of Co–S–W between CoS2 and WS2 was responsible for the outstanding HER activity. As a result, a low overpotential of ∼−97.2 mV at −10 mA cm−2, a small Tafel slope of ∼66.0 mV dec−1, and high electrochemical stability was observed for the CoS2@WS2 hybrid electrocatalyst. Similarly, other heterostructures of WS2–WS3,492 MoOx/Ni3S2,493 MoS2–CoSe2494 also have beed used for HER.


6.3.2 Effects of doping. 
Tuning the catalyst composition by doping single metal or non-metals has been considered as a universal way to advance catalytic activity. This form of doping, including cations and anions, such as metals and/or nitrogen, oxygen, and carbon can alter the electronic structure with small effects on the crystal structure to create abundant active sites, leading to elevated adsorption energy and enhanced electrocatalytic performance.495,496 Therefore, combining structure design and anion and/or metal doping is a favorable strategy to alter the electronic structure and increase the catalytic activity of electrocatalysts.
6.3.2.1 Anion doped metal sulfides. 
Recently, heterostructures of Ni3S2/VS2 with nitrogen doping showed superior electrocatalysis for HER activity. The N-doped Ni3S2/VS2 (N-Ni3S2/VS2) revealed a high electrocatalytic HER performance with a low overpotential of ∼151 mV at 10 mA cm−2, since N-doping improves conductivity and increases the number of catalytic active sites,497 see Fig. 32(i).
