Oxygen doped amorphous CoSx film was prepared by Sultana et al.498 and due to its structural features, during HER an onset potential of 220 mV vs. RHE with a lower Tafel slope of 72 mV dec−1 was attained, moreover it showed long-term stability over 24 h in a neutral pH. The HER activity of the O-CoSx film is comparable to highly crystalline cobalt sulfide materials. In addition, DFT calculations indicated that a reduced S–Co coordination number, as encountered in amorphous materials, leads to an optimum binding energy for hydrogen adsorption (Hads) on the material which facilitates electron transfer kinetics.
 Fig. 32  (i) (a) Fabrication of N-Ni3S2/VS2 (b) HER polarization curves and (c) Tafel plots of Ni3S2/VS2, N-Ni3S2/VS2, N-Ni3S2, Ni3S2, Ni foam and Pt/C (20 wt%) with a scan rate of 5 mV s−1 in 1 M KOH. Reproduced from ref. 497 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018. (ii) (a) O-CoMoS heteronanosheets grown on flexible carbon fiber cloth as bifunctional electrocatalysts have been produced by use of the Anderson-type (NH4)4[CoIIMo6O24H6]·6H2O polyoxometalate as bi-metal precursor (b and c) HER polarization curves of metal sulfide nanosheets arrays. Reproduced from ref. 499 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018. (iii) TEM image of Pt–TiS2 (a), Au–TiS2 (b), Pt–TaS2 (c), Au–TaS2 (d), (e) Polarization curves of different catalysts, and (f) the corresponding Tafel plots. (g) LSV curves of Pt–TiS2 and TiS2 before and after cyclic testing, and (h) Polarization curves of Pt–TaS2 and TaS2 before and after cyclic testing. Reproduced from ref. 502 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2014. 
To achieve efficient conversion, the modification of material composition is important and in this aspect, vertically aligned oxygenated-CoS2–MoS2 (O-CoMoS) hetero-nanosheets were grown on flexible carbon fiber cloth by Hou et al.499 In comparison to different oxygen doped metal sulfides such as O-FeMoS, O-NiMoS, and MoS2 nanosheet arrays, the O-CoMoS hetero-nanosheets array revealed a low overpotential of ∼97 mV to reach a current density of 10 mA cm−2 in an alkaline solution for the HER; see Fig. 32(ii). The Tafel slopes of as-prepared O-CoMoS, O-NiMoS, and O-FeMoS arrays were 70, 80, and 123 mV dec−1, respectively, indicating promising HER reaction kinetics for the O-CoMoS array. This was mainly due to the disordered structure of the O-CoMoS, which exposed the active heterointerfaces, facile charge transport on 2D conductive carbon channels, and abundant active catalytic sites from the synergistic effect of the heterostructures, thereby achieving an enhanced HER performance.
6.3.2.2 Cation doped metal sulfides. 
Due to improved performance, relatively low cost and high stability, the doping of cations attracted attention in the energy conversion process. In this regard, vanadium (V) doped Ni3S2 nanowire arrays on nickel foam were reported by Qu et al.500 It was observed that, at low temperatures, the doping of vanadium (V) can stimulate the formation of Ni3S2 nanowires. V doped Ni3S2 nanowires exhibited excellent electrocatalytic activity, and outperformed pristine Ni3S2 and other Ni3S2-based compounds. The nanowires had a low overpotential of ∼68 mV at 10 mA cm−2, a relatively low Tafel slope of 112 mV dec−1 with long-term stability (∼1000 cycles) and increased faradaic efficiency. The onset potential of V-doped Ni3S2 nanowires was as low as ∼39 mV, which is similar to platinum. From first-principle calculations, V-doping in Ni3S2 enriches the free carrier density near the Fermi level, resulting in improved catalytic active sites. Zhang et al.501 fabricated vertically oriented Fe-doped Ni3S2 nanosheet array on a 3D Ni foam (Fe-Ni3S2/NF). During their experiments it was observed that the metal sites were found to contribute mainly to the HER activity of Fe-Ni3S2/NF; Fe-doping could also improve the water adsorption ability and optimize the hydrogen adsorption energy of Ni3S2, which resulted in the enhancement of HER activity on Fe-Ni3S2/NF in terms of the overpotentials of 47, 142 and 232 mV vs. RHE, at current densities of ∼10, 20 and 100 mA cm−2 respectively, for Fe17.5%-Ni3S2/NF.
The fabrication of single layer nanosheets with high-yields remains a challenge. In this aspect, Zeng et al.502 prepared single-layer Pt–TiS2 and Pt–TaS2 nanosheets using exfoliated TiS2 and TaS2 nanosheets. It is significant that the Pt–TiS2 exhibited a similar current density to the commercial Pt–C catalyst on the basis of equivalent platinum loading levels. In addition, both Pt–TiS2 and Pt–TaS2 catalysts offered an increased electrocatalytic activity in HER compared to pure nanosheets of TiS2 and TaS2, respectively. The calculated Tafel slopes for Pt–C, Pt–TiS2 and TiS2 were ∼34.4, ∼40.6 and ∼102.5 V dec−1, respectively. This indicated that the catalytic reaction of Pt–TiS2 benefits from the grouping of Volmer–Tafel and Volmer–Heyrovsky mechanisms, as shown in Fig. 32(iii). In addition, the Pt–TiS2 and Pt–TaS2 hybrid materials showed long-term stability after 1000 cycles.


6.3.3 HER activity of carbon/graphene based metal sulfide composites. 
Hybridizing electrocatalysts with various highly active materials such as organic compounds of CNT and/or graphene enables uniform growth of nanostructures with large surface areas and greater surface roughness to increase the number of active sites, contribute to electrolyte movement, and solve the aggregation of active sites through an anchoring effect with a substrate, while decreasing the contact resistance and increasing the electrical conductivity with substrates.41,420,503–510 Wang et al.420 developed a highly active and stable catalyst of Co doped FeS2 nanosheets hybridized with carbon nanotubes (Fe1−xCoxS2/CNT hybrid) for HER in acidic solutions. The hybrid of Fe0.9Co0.1S2/CNT showed a low overpotential of ∼0.12 V at 20 mA cm−2, with a lower Tafel slope of ∼46 mV dec−1, and long-term durability over 40 h during HER operation. A DFT study showed that the high HER activity of hybrid Fe0.9Co0.1S2/CNT was due to the strong heteroatom interactions between CNT and Fe0.9Co0.1S2.
In addition, nitrogenated carbon materials with metal sulfides are also of interest for energy materials. In this aspect, Pramoda et al.511 prepared nanosheets of C3N4/MoS2 with nitrogenated rGO (NrGO) for HER activity. The cross-linked C3N4–NrGO (∼−0.36 V with Tafel value of 88 mV dec−1) hybrid revealed an onset potential shift of 60 mV as compared to that of a physical mixture of C3N4–NrGO (−0.42 V with Tafel value of ∼95 mV dec−1). It was observed that covalent cross-linking of 2D materials to produce hetero-assemblies or nanocomposites may have a valuable effect on catalytic activity.
Preparing metal sulfides with carbon materials will also provide enriched electrocatalytic active sites to increase HER performance.512 In this aspect, using slightly oxidized GO as a support, Li et al.41 prepared MoS2/rGO hybrid via a solvothermal method. The prepared hybrid catalyst revealed few-layer MoS2 nanostructure with numerous wide-open edges slanted onto graphene, which exhibited exceptional HER catalytic activity with a low overpotential of ∼100 mV and a Tafel slope of ∼41 mV dec−1 in 0.5 M H2SO4, which is smaller than amorphous MoS2 (∼40 mV dec−1). However, the large number of catalytic edge sites on the MoS2, as well as the excellent electrical coupling to the graphene system, led to a highly active HER catalyst. Covalently attached amorphous MoSx on rGO was prepared by Lee et al.513 Owing to the structural features, this linking of MoSx and rGO led to a highly efficient HER reaction at a low overpotential of ∼−157 mV and small Tafel slope of ∼41 mV dec−1 with 95% versus original activity after ∼1000 cycles. It was observed that the improved HER activity was attributed to the creation of a facile bridge by oxygen atoms between MoSx and GO which played a crucial role in control of planar MoSx growth, and improvement of exposed active sulfur sites. Similarly, Kumar et al.514 observed that at a lower activation energy ∼17–6 kJ mol−1, the MoS2/GO and MoS2/rGO hybrid provides faster HER reactions in an acid medium. Ghosh et al.515 measured a Tafel value of ∼41.7 mV dec−1 with ∼3000 cycles long-term stability for a MoS2–rGO hybrid, via the Volmer–Heyrovsky mechanism. Using the hydrothermal method, Li et al.516 prepared a composite of Cu and MoS2 on reduced graphene oxide (rGO) (Cu–MoS2/rGO) for the HER process. It was observed that the coverage of rGO enhanced the electrical conductivity of the Cu–MoS2/rGO catalyst, and a higher number of exposed edges via the formation of a nanoflower structure of MoS2. As a result, the Cu–MoS2/rGO (12 wt%) catalyst displayed a high HER activity with a current density of ∼83.6 mA cm−2 at −400 mV, which was ∼1.6 times higher than a MoS2/rGO (∼53.7 mA cm−2) catalyst. The Cu and rGO contents not only increase the electrical conductivity of the catalyst, but also promoted catalytic activity via a synergistic effect with MoS2 nanoflowers. Similarly, a hybrid composite of double-decked MoS2 nanosheets and reduced graphene oxide (rGO) sheets dotted with CoS2 nanoparticles (CoS2@MoS2/rGO) was prepared by Guo et al.517 Compared to bare materials such as CoS2/rGO and MoS2/rGO, the CoS2@MoS2/rGO demonstrated a greater HER activity with a lower overpotential of ∼98 mV at 10 mA cm−2 and a small Tafel slope of 37.4 mV dec−1, as well as excellent cyclic stability, and at ∼175 mV it reached 100 mA cm−2. From their DFT analysis it was observed that the hydrogen adsorption free energy of CoS2@MoS2/rGO was close to zero.
In addition, a PVP-assisted hydrothermal technique was adapted to synthesise WS2/rGO hybrids.518 The optimized catalyst of monolayered mWS2–rGO exhibited remarkable HER activity with a current density of ∼10 mA cm−2 at a small overpotential of ∼170 mV. This enhanced HER performance was attributed to the abundant active edges, high surface area, and a conducting link between mono-layered WS2 and rGO. Lately, Chen et al.519 prepared ternary hybrids of reduced graphene oxide (rGO)/tungsten disulfide (WS2)/tungsten trioxide (WO3) for the HER process. The resultant hybrids had a spherical structure made by crumpled graphene and WS2/WO3 nanorods. The crumpled graphene/WS2/WO3 (CGTH) catalyst displayed a superior electrocatalytic activity in terms of small Tafel slope ∼37 mV dec−1 and a low onset potential of 96 mV for a current density of 10 mA cm−2. Compared with the pristine catalysts, this hybrid composite showed improved catalytic activities for the HER. Similarly, as shown in Fig. 33(i), Seo et al.476 combined experimental and computational investigations for electrocatalysts based on a monolayer WS2 nanoplates on carbon from WSx nanoclusters (WSx@OMC) prepared via different sulfidation times. Notably, the electrocatalyst of WSx@OMC synthesized at a short sulfidation time contained large number of WSx nanoclusters, and showed higher HER activity than the catalyst prepared at a long sulfidation time. Owing to these features, the 20% WSx@OMC catalysts showed good HER performance at a current density of ∼10 mA cm−2 in the overpotential range of ∼213 mV with a small Tafel value of ∼74 mV dec−1. The enhanced performance was attributed to the controlled loading in 20% WSx@OMC which exposed a large amount of active sites. In addition, Cu nanodot (ND)-decorated Ni3S2 nanotubes (NTs) on carbon fibers (CFs) (Cu NDs/Ni3S2 NTs–CFs) was tested as an electrocatalyst for HER activity in alkaline media.520 The Cu NDs/Ni3S2 NTs–CFs exhibited a low onset potential of ∼60 mV, and an overpotential of ∼128 mV at a current density of 10 mA cm−2, which was comparable to Ni3S2 NTs–CFs (∼189 mV), Cu NDs–CFs (>300 mV), and CFs (>300 mV). In addition, the Tafel slope of Cu NDs/Ni3S2 NTs–CFs at a low overpotential interval between 0.01 and 0.20 V was only ∼76.2 mV dec−1, which was much smaller than those of pristine Ni3S2 NTs–CFs (∼125.7 mV dec−1), Cu NDs–CFs (∼166.7 mV dec−1), and CFs (∼168.4 mV dec−1).
 Fig. 33  (i) (a) Schematic of growth orientation of MSx (M = W or Mo) nanostructures on amorphous carbon surfaces, (b) LSV curves measured in 0.5 M H2SO4 at a scan rate of 2 mV−1 and (c) corresponding Tafel plots with horizontal error bars. Reproduced from ref. 476 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2018. (ii) (a) Schematic of growth orientation of P–N–O/graphene/WS2, (b) polarization curves below 10 mA cm−2, (c) Tafel plots, inset, the exchange current density, (d) EIS spectra, inset shows the enlarged EIS spectra of P, N, O-graphene film, WS2 nanolayers, WS2@N, O-graphene film, WS2@P, N, O-graphene film, and Pt/C and (e) chronoamperometric response of WS2@P, N, O-graphene film, inset shows the polarization curves before and after 20 h operating. Reproduced from ref. 521 with permission from Wiley-VCH, copyright 2015. 
Likewise, P, N, O doped graphene with 1T-WS2 nanolayers as a heterostructure film was prepared by Duan et al.521 Due to the highly exposed active centers on 2D 1T-WS2 nanolayers, the extended surface, and continuous conductive network of these heterostructure exhibited a small overpotential of ∼125 mV vs. RHE with a current density of ∼10 mA cm−2, which is close to commercial Pt/C under N2 saturated 0.5 M H2SO4. In addition, the strong synergistic effects between the components exhibited the smallest Tafel slope of ∼52.7 mV dec−1 as compared to WS2@N, O-graphene film ∼180.61 mV dec−1, WS2 nanolayers ∼103.2 mV dec−1, and P, N, O-graphene film ∼183.0 mV dec−1, demonstrating the highest HER rate and favorable kinetics of heterostructured WS2@P, N, O-graphene films as shown in Fig. 33(ii). Several other metal sulfide composites and hybrid structures have been reported for HER activity353,522–525 and their enhanced performance is summarized in Table 4.

6.3.4 Effects of edge sites and defects engineering. 
Defects are well-known to strongly interrupt the local density of states in metal sulfide crystal structures and produce extra energy levels among the valence and conduction bands.526,527 Typically, most of the metal sulfide basal surfaces are catalytically passive for HER activity. In order to increase the catalytic activity of the plane, reactive sites can be produced by the introduction of defects. As a result, the electronic properties of the metal sulfide catalysts can be adjusted. In addition, owing to the edge sites, many of the metal sulfides exhibit high electrocatalytic activity. This is thought to make unsaturated sulfur atoms at the edges, which is directed to the amorphous metal sulfide systems.445,528 Moreover, edge sites can be created by increasing the density of structural defects on the metal sulfides.529–531 As a result, several types of defects have been detected in metal sulfides.532–536 Accordingly, a limited number of studies have focused on the impact of defects of metal sulfides on the HER activity. However, it is important to discern the role of defects, since defect controlled manufacturing can provide advanced approaches for improving the density of active sites or enhancing the basal plane conductivity of the metal sulfides.
The design of high-performance electrocatalytic HER reactions requires emergent strategies for defect engineering or oxygen vacancy control and understanding the relationship between dislocations and electrochemical performance. Usually, physical and chemical defects can be produced by O2 plasma or Ar plasma to adjust the electronic properties and provide activity of the inert in-plane S atoms of MS2 (M = Mo or W or V etc.). Among other metal sulfides, MoS2 is a promising and low-cost material for electrochemical hydrogen generation owing to its high activity and long-term stability. However, the efficiency of HER is restricted by the number of active sites in MoS2, for example edges in MoS2 catalysts. In this aspect, Ye et al.537 created defects on a monolayer MoS2via oxygen plasma exposure and hydrogen treatment, which led to dense exposed edges and a substantial enhancement in hydrogen evolution reactions. The corresponding HER performance of MoS2 samples with hydrogen annealing was also evaluated, see Fig. 34(i). In addition, the HER activity of pristine MoS2 with hydrogen annealing temperatures of 400, 500, and 600 °C were tested. When compared to bare MoS2 without annealing, it was observed that an annealing treatment reduced the onset overpotential and increased the current density. It is worth noting that MoS2 annealed under 500 °C displayed the lowest overpotential (∼300 mV) compared to 400 °C (∼470 mV) and 600 °C (∼450 mV). These results revealed that HER performance increases with an increased number of defects since the creation of large amounts of macro- and micro-size triangular holes provided more catalytic active sites for the HER.
 Fig. 34  (i) (a–d) SEM images of monolayer MoS2 with 400, 500, 600, and 700 °C H2 annealing, respectively, showing the appearance of small triangle holes. (e) HER property hydrogen treated MoS2 (f) Tafel plots show hydrogen-annealed MoS2 has lower Tafel slope than MoS2 without any treatment, revealing improved electrochemical activity of MoS2 as a HER catalyst after hydrogen annealing. Reproduced from ref. 537 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018. (ii) (a) LSV curves of Ru/MoS2/CP, PtC/CP, Ru/CP, MoS2/CP, and MoS2 electrodes in 0.5 M H2SO4 (b) compared overpotential values to afford a current density of −10 mA cm−2 under basic and acidic conditions and (c) schematic illustration of interfacial synergy between Ru and MoS2 for enhanced alkaline HER. Reproduced from ref. 539 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2017. (iii) (a) HER activity of NbS2 nanoflakes compared with NbS2 and Pt/C (b) EIS spectrum of Rct for NbS2/rGO when compared to NbS2 nanoflakes alone (partial magnification of EIS spectra is shown in the inset) (c) Tafel plot for the HER performance of NbS2 flakes and NbS2/rGO (d) schematic of hydrogen evolution on NbS2 nanosheets. Reproduced from ref. 543 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2018. 
In addition to plasma treatment, chemical functionalization also plays a significant role in creating defects. In this aspect, Xie et al.442 reported on the impact of defects on MoS2 ultrathin nanosheets for electrocatalytic HER, where the defects were increased using thiourea to convert Mo(VI) to Mo(IV) during the solvothermal method. The highly defective MoS2 structure introduces additional active edge sites on the ultrathin nanosheets, which increased their electrocatalytic performance under acidic environment, thereby achieving a high cathodic current density of ∼13 mA cm−2 at a low overpotential, small Tafel slope of ∼50 mV dec−1 and excellent durability. Furthermore, by exploiting a liquid-ammonia-assisted lithiation method, Yin et al.538 prepared porous metallic 1T phase MoS2 nanosheets with abundant edge sites and a large concentration of S-vacancies. As a consequence, HER activity for a porous 1T-MoS2 revealed a current density of ∼10 mA cm−2 at a low overpotential of ∼154 mV and small Tafel slope of 43 mV dec−1. A first principle study on the defect engineering for assisting the hydrogen evolution has been conducted which revealed that, when a defect is formed, a dangling bond nearer to the Fermi energy is created with more suitable adsorption energies for hydrogen evolution reactions. Liu et al.539 studied the alkaline HER kinetics of Ru coupled defect-rich MoS2 nanosheets, whereby Ru created water dissociation and nearby defect-rich MoS2 allowed promising hydrogen adsorption for recombination into H2. As shown in Fig. 34(ii), the Ru/MoS2 catalyst exhibited a remarkable catalytic activity in terms of low overpotential of ∼−13 mV at 10 mA cm−2 with long-term stability after 12 h or 1000 cycles, which was greater than commercial 20 wt% Pt/C (∼−78 mV).
In addition to MoS2 and WS2, 2D VS2 nanomaterials have emerged as highly efficient and cost-effective electrocatalysts for the HER. As with the extensively studied 2H-phase MoS2 basal plane, both 2H- and 1T-phase VS2 basal planes exhibit higher catalytic activity due to their metallic properties. Recently, Zhang et al.540 predicted a DFT model for the HER activity of 2D VS2 with various intrinsic point defects and it was observed that the V-rich form provides high HER activity. This was performed with the introduction of intrinsic point defects on the VS2 basal planes; as a result they found four types of stable defects in the 2H phase such as Sad, Svac, Vad, and VS and three kinds of stable defects in 1T phase such as Sad, Svac, and Vad. Additionally, it was observed that the development of Svac, Vad, and VS structures in the 2H phase and Vad in the 1T phase can increase the HER activity of basal planes. In order to investigate the activity of the defect rich VS2, Zhang et al.541 prepared defect rich VS2 nanosheets via the solvothermal method. They attained defect rich VS2 nanosheets, in which they also observed an expanded (001) interlayer spacing as high as 1.00 nm, which was expanded by 74% as compared to the pristine VS2 (∼0.575 nm). The interlayer extended VS2 nanosheets exhibited outstanding HER activity, with a small overpotential of ∼43 mV at a current density of 10 mA cm−2, a low Tafel slope of ∼36 mV dec−1, and long-term durability of ∼60 h without any decrease in current. The HER performance of this expanded interlayered VS2 nanosheets was superior to pristine VS2 with a normal interlayer spacing and commercial Pt/C. The excellent electrocatalytic HER activity was accredited to the expanded interlayer space and rich defects.
Notably, there are four types of NbS2 phases available; two stoichiometric and two non-stoichiometric phases including – 2H NbS2 and 3R NbS2 and 2H Nb1+xS2 and 3R Nb1+xS2, respectively.542 Using an Ar plasma treatment, Gopalakrishnan et al.543 prepared a defect rich 3R-NbS2 polymorph structure with semiconducting behavior and abundant catalytic defect sites of nanoflakes of NbS2 for HER activity. However, the defect free NbS2/rGO composite revealed an enhanced HER catalyst in terms of small onset potential, low Tafel value and improved exchange current density, see Fig. 34(iii).
Recently, the creation of defects by a plasma has attracted attention in functionalizing the catalytic inert basal plane of metal sulfides. Li et al.522 prepared a high density of atomic-scale pores in a 2D TaS2 surface to improve the HER activity via an oxygen plasma treatment. As a result, the intrinsic relationship between the HER and edge defects were revealed. The onset potential of HER was reduced from 302, 200, to 225 mV with a rise of exposure period varying from 5, 10 to 15 min, respectively. Similarly, 2D metallic TaS2 was prepared by Huan et al.544 using CVD. Owing to its electrocatalytic active edge sites, vertically oriented 1T-TaS2 nanosheets on nanoporous gold substrate had excellent performance with a high exchange current density of ∼67.61 μA cm−2 with Tafel slopes of 67–82 mV dec−1. In addition, metallic vanadium-doped NiS2 nanosheets displayed outstanding electrocatalytic HER performance with small overpotential of ∼290 mV at 10 mA cm−2 with high durability in 1 M KOH solutions.545 This enhanced performance was attributed to the well-designed atomic defects in V-doped NiS2, which was created by a vanadium displacement defect. Likewise, Ni3S2–rGO nanosheets on nickel foam (Ni3S2–rGO@NF) prepared by one-step hydrothermal process546 showed an enhanced HER performance in terms of an onset potential as low as ∼0 mV, which is comparable to Pt, and only a low overpotential of ∼44 mV is needed to reach ∼10 mA cm−2 with a high stability.546


6.3.5 Annealing temperature effects. 
As the temperature of the semiconducting materials is increased, the valence band electrons gain adequate energy to outflow from the confines of their atoms. As a consequence, at higher temperatures the valence electrons in semiconducting materials are free and the electrical resistivity falls.547,548 However, if the temperature increases, the grain size of the semiconducting material will increase and hence an optimum elevated temperature treatment is essential. By using ultra-high vacuum (UHV), Jaramillo et al.414 prepared MoS2 on an Au(111) surface, which was annealed at 400 °C or 500 °C. The MoS2 samples possessed Tafel slopes between 55 and 60 mV dec−1 irrespective of annealing temperature. In addition, MoS2/SiO2 was prepared by Sobczynski549 from MoS3/SiO2, by varying the annealing temperature. It was revealed that the catalytic activity of MoS2/SiO2 reduced with an increase of calcination temperature.
Wu et al.435 prepared WS2 nanosheets via ball milling, followed by annealing at 600–700 °C. However, inorganic fullerene such as WS2 (IF-WS2) was obtained only above 800 °C. The uneven nanosheets and associated structural features, such as separate NSs with loosely arranged layers, provided highly open edges as active catalytic sites. The WS2 nanosheets had a HER current density of ∼9.66 mA cm−2 with a small overpotential of ∼−150 mV, which was ∼8.6 times higher than commercial MoS2 (∼1.12 mA cm−2). Lukowski et al.550 prepared a metallic 1T-WS2 polymorph from the conversion of as-grown 2H-WS2 by heating the samples in n-butyllithium medium at 80 °C for 48 h; a microwave-assisted higher temperature method was used to accelerate the intercalation procedure. As a result, 1T-WS2 nanosheets revealed improved electrocatalytic HER activity and required only a small overpotential of ∼−75 mV vs. RHE to attain 10 mA cm−2 with a small Tafel value of ∼−70 mV dec−1. This enhanced HER performance was attributed to the controlled nanostructure, structural polymorphs and optimum temperature.
In addition, 3D hybrids of WS2/graphene/Ni foam showed high HER activity after annealing at 450 °C,551 and revealed a low Tafel slope of ∼43 mV dec−1, and a high current density of 10 mA cm−2 at −119 mV vs. RHE and long-term durability. The enhancement was thought to be due to the good electrical contact between WS2 and graphene and porous structures of the nickel support. At 250 °C the annealed WS2 nanoribbons exhibited a decrease in HER activity,475 which could be due to the reduced electrical conductivity after annealing. In contrast, an enhanced electrocatalytic activity was observed for annealed MoS2 (450 °C) and its hybrid with rGO i.e. MoS2/rGO (450 °C).552 Compared to the annealed MoS2, the MoS2/rGO nanocomposite showed higher HER catalytic activity with a lower onset overpotential of ∼28 mV vs. RHE at current density of ∼10 mA cm−2, and a Tafel slope of ∼41.7 mV dec−1. This enhanced performance was attributed to the improved electrocatalytic active sites during annealing of MoS2/rGO.552
Recently, Wu et al.553 reported an annealing effect for N doped NiCo2S4 NWs. It was observed that with an increase of temperature the catalytic performance progressively increased and then achieved a maximum activity at 600 °C for annealed N–NiCo2S4. During H2 evolution testing, the 600 °C annealed N–NiCo2S4 sample exhibited an enhanced HER activity in terms of low overpotential of ∼41 mV vs. RHE at a current density of ∼10 mA cm−2, as shown in Fig. 35(i). Furthermore, it was seen that the HER performance decreased as the annealing temperature was further increased above 600 °C, which may be due to the aggregation of NW into bulk particles. As a result, N–NiCo2S4 annealed at 600 °C had a lower Tafel slope and smaller overpotential than Ni–Co–O, N–NiCoO, NiCo2S4, N–NiCo2S4, and commercial Pt/C in alkaline condition. In addition, the core–shell nanostructures provided facile electron transfer to enhance HER activity. By the solvothermal method,554 uniform Co9S8@MoS2 core–shell heterostructures were prepared and subjected to annealing treatments of 650, 750, and 850 °C for 3 h. As a result, the core–shell Co9S8@MoS2 catalyst annealed at 750 °C revealed HER performance with a low overpotential of ∼143 mV at 10 mA cm−2 and a small Tafel slope of ∼117 mV dec−1 in alkaline environment. This performance was better than samples annealed at lower (650 °C) or higher (850 °C) temperatures. Meanwhile, the poor crystallinity and the bulk size nature of 650 °C and 850 °C annealed core–shell Co9S8@MoS2 samples showed a decrease in the electrocatalytic active sites. Moreover, at 650 °C the enhanced performance of annealed Co9S8@MoS2 catalysts was attributed to the core–shell structure that increased the synergistic effect of Co9S8 and MoS2 and provides catalytically active sites at higher temperature; see Fig. 35(ii).
 Fig. 35  (i) (a) XRD patterns (b and c) TEM images of NiCo2S4 and N-NiCo2S4 nanowire arrays and (d) the HER performance of NiCo2S4 and N-NiCo2S4 nanowire arrays in KOH solution. Reproduced from ref. 553 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2018. (ii) (a) XRD pattern of the as-obtained Co9S8@MoS2 obtained at 750 °C and (b) XRD patterns of the precursor and Co9S8@MoS2 samples at different annealing temperatures, Polarization curves (c) and (d) Tafel plots of prepared samples for HER in 1.0 M KOH. Reproduced from ref. 554 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018. (iii) (a and b) TEM images of Zn0.30Co2.70S4, (c and d) LSV polarization curves and Tafel plot for prepared cobalt based sulfides. Reproduced from ref. 552 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2016. 
Recently, Huang et al.552 prepared hollow polyhedral structured metal sulfides of MxCo3−xS4 (M = Zn, Ni, and Cu) via self-template strategy followed by annealing at 350 °C for 2 h. Remarkably, as shown in Fig. 35(iii), the hollow Zn0.30Co2.70S4 displayed an excellent HER activity compared to other metal sulfides of MxCo3−xS4 (M = Zn, Ni, and Cu) over a wide pH range, with low overpotentials of ∼80, ∼90, and ∼85 mV at 10 mA cm−2 and reached a current density of ∼100 mA cm−2 with low potentials of ∼129, ∼144, and ∼136 mV in 0.5 M H2SO4, 0.1 M phosphate buffer, and 1 M KOH, respectively.


6.3.6 Porosity effects. 
A porous nature and large surface area are key features that regulate the functional properties and performance of catalysts.436,555 Recently, unique 3D open/hollow architectures have shown promise for several energy conversion and storage applications. By a sulfurization method, Miao et al.556 prepared mesoporous FeS2. As a result of the mesoporous nature and more exposed (210) facets, the FeS2 exhibited a remarkable HER performance with a low overpotential of ∼96 mV at ∼10 mA cm−2 and a small Tafel slope of ∼78 mV dec−1 at a pH = 13. Theoretical analysis indicated that the excellent HER catalytic activity of mesoporous FeS2 was attributed to the exposed (210) facets. Similarly, due to additional catalytic active sites as a result of the large surface area ∼35.7 m2 g−1, the porous Ni3S2 nanorods showed an excellent HER activity with a low overpotential of ∼200 mV at a current density of 10 mA cm−2 in alkaline medium.557 Recently, Jiang et al.558 synthesized NiCo2S4 double-shelled ball-in-ball hollow spheres (BHSs) via a simple solvothermal route. Due to their structural features, optimum chemical composition, large surface area and distinct interior voids, the NiCo2S4 BHSs catalyst possessed a small onset overpotential ∼27.9 mV and a low overpotential of ∼89.7 mV at 10 mA cm−2 under alkaline conditions. Similarly, Wang et al.559 prepared 1D porous MS2/MO2 (M = W, Mo) nanorods for the HER. Due to its porous nature, the WS2/WO2 NRs showed an excellent HER activity with an overpotential of −160 mV at a current density of ∼15.0 mA cm−2, which was much larger than that observed for WO2 NRs (ca. 0.38 mA cm−2) and bulk WS2 (ca. 0.36 mA cm−2). At a low overpotential of ∼−200 mV, MoS2/MoO2-6 exhibited the highest cathodic current of ∼15.1 mA cm−2, which was higher than that of bulk MoS2 (0.85 mA cm−2) and MoO2 NRs (0.90 mA cm−2). Meanwhile, the enhanced performance of MS2/MO2 NRs was inherited from the highly porous nature, enhanced mass transport and conductive properties from their metallic dioxides for excellent HER activities and stabilities. In addition, highly porous MoS2/graphene aerogels (GMA) was prepared by Worsley et al.560 The graphene micro assemblies on MoS2 provided a large surface area of ∼700 m2 g−1 and an electrical conductivity of ∼112 S m−1. Due to the additional active sties as a result of the large surface area of the MoS2/graphene aerogel, the material exhibited promising HER performance with low onset potential of ∼100 mV and high current density of ∼100 mA cm−2 at 260 mV.
Recently, nano-shelled heterostructures of amorphous Ni(OH)2 with crystalline CuS mesoporous heterostructures were prepared by Liu et al.561 using a template technique. As shown in Fig. 36, due to the distinctive hollow mesoporous nanoshell features and the heterostructure synergetic effects among crystalline CuS and amorphous Ni(OH)2, the Ni(OH)2@CuS catalyst offered the facile electron transport with abundant active sites for HER. As a result, the Ni(OH)2@CuS catalyst revealed a smaller onset overpotential of ∼42 mV with a current density of ∼10 mA cm−2 at an overpotential of 186 mV. This HER activity was higher than the pristine Ni(OH)2, CuS and their mixtures. Likewise, due to the rich electroactive sites from nanoporous structures and the integration of highly conductive graphene, nanoballs of a porous NiS2/rGO hybrid562 exhibited an excellent HER activity in terms of high cathodic current density of ∼10 mA cm− 2 at a low overpotential of ∼−200 mV with a small Tafel slope of 52 mV dec−1, whereas the pristine porous nanoballs of NiS2 showed a current density of 10 mA cm−2 at an overpotential of ∼317 mV. Likewise, Zhu et al.563 prepared NiCo2S4 porous nanosheets on carbon cloth with a total pore volume of 0.228 mL g−1 and average pore radius of 1.818 nm by an anion exchange method. During HER testing, the porous nanosheets of NiCo2S4/CC exhibited a higher performance compared to nonporous NiCo2S4/NF. This enhanced performance was attributed to the metallic feature of the NiCo2S4 phase, intrinsic high activity and porous nanosheet features, which exposes more active catalytic sites and increases the diffusion of electrolyte.
 Fig. 36  (a) SEM images, (b) TEM images of hollow Ni(OH)2@CuS spheres, (c) histogram of oneset overpotential and (d) η at j = 10 mA cm−2 of various as-prepared samples and (e) polarization curves of Ni(OH)2@CuS in 1 M KOH electrolyte before and after long-term 1000 cycles. Inserted is the time-dependent current density curve under a static overpotential of 90 mV for 30 h. Reproduced from ref. 561 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018. 

6.4 Perspectives of electrochemical HER
The control of morphology is essential to understand the relationships between the structure and performance of metal sulfide materials in HER catalysis applications. For instance, the electrocatalytic catalytic performance of metal sulfide materials toward HER is strongly associated with the density of edge sites, defect concentration level, and its nano-scale morphology. Metal sulfides with a range of nanoscale morphologies have been developed, including truncated triangles, hexagons, flakes, ultrathin sheets, hierarchical, dendritic, and hollow structures. These catalysts also mostly possess highly porous structures, which are beneficial for electrolyte permeation. It is generally recognized that a high surface area can lead to more exposed catalytic active sites, which is favorable in achieving an optimum structure for enhanced HER activity. In addition, creating appropriate defects in materials such as metallic MoS2, WS2 and VS2 can improve HER performance by providing a highly conductive nature.
Other methods of improving performance include changing the composition of bi-metal sulfides, compared with mono-metal sulfides, to create a complex structure that can provide a synergistic coupling and structural effects to enhance the benefits of each metal and sulfide component. The doping of highly active nanoparticles of noble metals on metal sulfides can improve the number of exposed active sites and increase HER performance. Using highly conductive substrates such as carbon fibers or carbon cloth to load the metal sulfides also enhances the electron conduction and provides further electrocatalytic active sites. Notably, the development of metal–carbon bonds increases the catalyst stability to achieve a prolonged lifetime. Moreover, the formation of a heterojunction can enhance HER performance by tuning the nanostructure, electronic levels and providing more exposed active sites. During the electrochemical HER process, a lower onset potential, high cathodic current, greater turnover frequency (TOF), and lower Tafel slope values can be achieved. In addition, the stability of the catalyst can also be improved.
7 Photocatalytic hydrogen generations using metal sulfides
7.1 Mono-metal sulfides

7.1.1 MoS2 and ZnS photocatalysts. 
Typically metal sulfides with a tunable number of layers, high crystallinity, and edge and defect morphologies are crucial for high performance photocatalytic applications. Over recent decades, metal sulfides have been widely investigated as hydrogen evolution photocatalysts.7,36,373,407,458,564 Among them, MoS2 is extensively investigated as a result of its excellent stability, favorable electronic arrangement and light absorption properties. Moreover, bulk MoS2 has an indirect band gap of ∼1.2 eV, which is unsuitable for a photocatalytic response since it lacks oxidation/reduction potentials for initiating a photocatalytic reaction.565 However, 2D nanosheets of MoS2 have a direct band gap of ∼1.96 eV due to a quantum confinement effect, which provides MoS2 nanosheets with suitable band positions for visible-light absorption.436,566 Peng et al.567 prepared 2D single-layers of MoS2 nanosheets, in which well-designed heterojunctions are created between the different phases of semiconducting 2H, metallic 1T, and quasi-metallic 1T′ phases via exfoliated monolayers of MoS2. Remarkably, multi-phases of MoS2 provide optimum photocatalytic HER from the development of in-plane heterojunctions between 1T′ MoS2 and 2H MoS2. Significantly, this not only facilitates charge generation and light absorption by the 2H phase, but provides proficient charge separation at the 1T′/2H interface, and increases HER over the basal sites of 1T′ MoS2. In addition, due to the molecular mimic of MoS2 edge sites, Lei et al.568 attained a photocatalytic H2 evolution rate of ∼335 h−1 with a turnover number of ∼1570 for a thiomolybdate [Mo3S13]2− catalyst.568
Tanagida et al.569 reported that without a redox catalysts, colloidal ZnS was an efficient photocatalyst for hydrogen generation in the existence of electron donors such as MeOH, THF, EtOH, and TEA. Their findings revealed that colloidal ZnS fabricated in situ can assist in the transfer of photoinduced effective charge carriers. It was also reported570,571 that in the absence of sacrificial electron donors, colloidal ZnS experienced photocatalyst degradation upon irradiation of light, where the photocatalyst turned black after a small quantity of H2 evolution. In addition, ZnS in sacrificial SO32− solutions were used for photocatalytic H2 production under UV light irradiation with a quantum yield of ∼90% at 313 nm.571


7.1.2 CoxSy, SnxSy, CdS, NixSy and CuxSy photocatalysts. 
Zheng et al.572 prepared multilayer hierarchical nanostructured cobalt sulfide (CoS-2) composed of nanoplates via a facile solvothermal route. Owing to its structural features, the CoS-2 catalyst exhibited a substantial H2 production rate of 1196 μmol h−1 g−1 under visible light. The enhanced HER rate of CoS-2 was associated with its highly active sites due to its unique nanostructural features, and the synergistic impact among CoS and the mixture of cubic cattierite (CoS2) phase that leads to active electron transfer in catalytic process. Recently, by hydrothermal technique, SnS2 nanoplates were manufactured by Fu et al.573 with exposed (001) facets and a single-crystalline SnS2 over hexagon-shaped SnS2 nanoplates with a regular size of ∼275 nm and thickness of ∼56 nm. The ordered hexagon-SnS2 nanoplates attained a high photocatalytic hydrogen evolution activity of ∼356 μmol h−1 under visible light (λ ≥ 420 nm), which was greatly improved compared to irregular nanoplate-like SnS2. The enhanced performance was attributed to the highly crystalline nature with a smaller amount of defects in the regular hexagon SnS2 nanoplates compared to the irregular SnS2. In addition, the S atoms with more dense (001) exposed facets favoured the development of H bonds with H2O molecules, which can improve the activity. Typically, compared to hexagonal CdS, the cubic CdS photocatalyst displayed a lower H2 evolution performance under visible-light irradiation;574,575 since the hexagonal phase of CdS is stable and cubic CdS is a metastable phase. Hence, most CdS based photocatalysts for hydrogen generation are focused on hexagonal phased CdS, while the cubic CdS with an improved H2-evolution rate has rarely been studied. However, Yu et al.576 reported a high photocatalytic H2-evolution rate of ∼0.36 mmol h−1 for cubic phase CdS which was ∼2.6 times greater than the traditional hexagonal phase CdS i.e. ∼0.14 mmol h−1, as shown in Fig. 37. The resultant cubic CdS showed a small crystal size (∼5 nm) and large surface area (>75.23 m2 g−1) with a high stability and large number of active sites. Toe et al.577 prepared hollow copper sulfide (CuxS) from cuprous oxide (Cu2O) via an ion-exchange synthesis route. During photocatalytic hydrogen generation, the high activity was facilitated by its highly crystalline nature with low copper defects in CuxS; the catalyst also showed an excellent stability.
 Fig. 37  Schematic of facile synthesis of (a) cubic-phase CdS nanocrystals in a Na2S–Na2SO3 system: step (1) is the formation of cubic CdS nanocrystals; step (2) is the preferential adsorption of massive S2−/SO32− on the nanocrystal surface. (b) Traditional precipitation-calcination preparation of hexagonal CdS: step (3) is the formation of cubic CdS nanocrystal aggregates; step (4) is the phase transformation to form traditional h-CdS at 550 °C (c) photocatalytic H2-evolution activity for various samples: (black squares) c-CdS-NC, (red dots c-CdS-180 °C), (blue triangles) c-CdS-AG and (green triangles) traditional h-CdS and (d) cycling runs of the H2-evolution activity of c-CdS-NC photocatalyst. Reproduced from ref. 576 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018. 
Similarly, nanostructures of NiS2 were controlled by a microwave-assisted method.338 NiS2 nanosized cubes, spheres and particles were obtained and were primarily used for photocatalytic hydrogen production. A variety of morphologies of NiS2 nanostructures are beneficial to enhance H2 production. Notably, NiS2 nanospheres showed highly enhanced photocatalytic for H2 evolution up to 3400 μmol throughout 12 h under visible light (λ > 420 nm) with an average H2 generation rate of 283 μmol h−1. Other metal sulfide photocatalytic nanomaterials such as ZrS2,578 Bi2S3,579 In2S3,580 and SnS581,582 also demonstrated good photocatalytic activity. Notably, most of the pristine or pure binary metal sulfides are defect rich and since some have poor light absorption, their physical, chemical and optical properties were modified accordingly. These materials are now discussed in detail in the following sections.

7.2 Bi-metal sulfides

7.2.1 MInxSy (M = Zn, Cd, Cu, Ca, Na) photocatalysts. 
Bi-metal ZnIn2S4 is a semiconductor chalcogenide photocatalyst that has an energy gap consistent to the absorption of visible-light with chemical stability. In addition, ZnIn2S4 is an eco-friendly photocatalyst for photocatalytic H2 generation and thus several morphologies such as nanoparticles, microspheres, nanoribbons and nanotubes have been fabricated and used for photocatalysis.583–588 However, during nanostructure growth, solvent effects are more influential. In this aspect, Shen et al.584 prepared three kinds of microstructures such as flowering-cherry-like microspheres and micro clusters using the solvents of water, methanol and ethylene glycol (EG). Notably, aqueous-mediated ZnIn2S4 exhibited a highly crystalline nature, which resulted in the highest photocatalytic activity of ∼27.3 μmol h−1, for hydrogen production rate under visible-light. Methanol and ethylene glycol mediated ZnIn2S4 exhibited a smaller H2 generation rate of ∼12.4 μmol h−1 and ∼9.1 μmol h−1, respectively.
A hexagonal ZnIn2S4 surface morphology was controlled using trimethylamine to create marigold flowers and nanoplates/nanostrips via a hydrothermal method. Due to the narrow band gap of ∼2.34–2.48 eV the hexagonal ZnIn2S4 photocatalyst was used for H2 production from hydrogen sulfide (H2S). H2S + OH− ↔ HS− + H2O(53) Semiconducting metal sulfide: ZnIn2S4 → h+VB + e−CB(54) Oxidation reaction: 2HS− + 2h+VB → S22− + 2H+(55) Reduction reaction: 2H+ + 2e−CB → H2(56)
These are the basic mechanisms for photocatalytic H2 generation via H2S photodecomposition. Initially, the semiconducting metal sulfide absorbs light and produces electron–hole pairs. Then, the photogenerated charge carriers diffuse into the electrolyte solution, driving the reaction of photodecomposition to yield hydrogen. Further, the valence band hole (h+VB) of ZnIn2S4 photocatalyst powder oxidizes the HS− ion to a disulfide (S22−) ion, releasing a proton from the HS− ion, where the electron of the conduction band electron (e−CB) of the photocatalyst reduces protons to create hydrogen molecules. A HER rate of ∼5287 μmol h−1 was achieved under visible light. The influence of indium concentration on structural morphology and photocatalytic HER activity was examined by Song et al. Of all the varied indium compositions studied, a high photocatalytic HER activity of 363 μmol g−1 h−1 was observed for the ZnIn2.3S4−y photocatalyst, which was credited to its large specific surface area and small crystallite size.589 In addition, Lei et al.256 examined the photocatalytic activity of ZnIn2S4 for H2 evolution. Significantly, the ZnIn2S4 exhibited a HER activity of ∼57 μmol g−1 h−1 and by using a Pt co-catalyst, it was improved to ∼800 μmol g−1 h−1. Microspheres of ZnIn2S4 fabricated by a template free hydrothermal technique exhibited a hydrogen production rate of ∼842 μmol h−1 at pH = 1.585 Likewise, a bio-inspired material of hydrilla templated hexagonal-cubic phase ZnIn2S4 was prepared by Chen et al.590 and an enhanced photocatalytic H2 production rate of ∼14.9 μmol h−1 was achieved. The enhancement in performance was attributed to effective charge separation and transfer through the hexagonal-cubic phase junction and photon energy harvesting nature of hydrilla leave structure. Later, Shen et al.591 prepared a ZnIn2S4 photocatalyst using a cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) assisted hydrothermal method. Notably, the photocatalytic performance of CTAB assisted ZnIn2S4 was enhanced with a large band gap, compared to the ZnIn2S4 made without CTAB, and as the degree of CTAB increases, the d space (006) of ZnIn2S4 increases. The highest quantum yield for a ZnIn2S4 photocatalyst prepared by the 9.6 mol CTAB-assisted hydrothermal method was ∼18.4% at 420 nm with a hydrogen production rate of 112.45 mmol h−1 and 122.5 mmol h−1.592
A highly crystalline CdIn2S4 photocatalyst with a marigold-like morphology and a diameter of 25 nm was prepared by Kale et al.62 where the photocatalyst had quantum yields of ∼16.8% (CdIn2S4 marigold-like morphology) and 17.1% (CdIn2S4 nanotubes) at 500 nm light irradiation for photocatalytic HER. Similarly, a cubic spinel structured CdIn2S4 was prepared by Bhirud et al.593 who investigated the role of surfactant on its surface morphology and photocatalytic H2 generation activity. The marigold flower-like structure was attained without any surfactant. In addition, the surfactant PVP and CTAB was used to fabricate a bipyramid shape CdIn2S4 (length of 0.7–1 μm) and hollow cavity with marigold flower shaped CdIn2S4 respectively. The optical band gap was in the range of 2.12–2.29 eV. Compared to other photocatalysts, due to the structural feature and low band gap, the bipyramid shaped CdIn2S4 showed a good photocatalytic activity of ∼3238 mmol h−1 with good durability.
Ding et al.594 analyzed the photocatalytic HER activity of CaIn2S4, where optical studies indicated that CaIn2S4 had an optical band gap in the range of ∼1.68 to 1.74 eV with a hydrogen production rate of ∼30.92 μmol h−1 g−1. The authors proposed that CaIn2S4 confined In3+ ions in a d10 arrangement could be the reason for the high photocatalytic activity. In addition, Zhang et al.595 prepared a CuInS2 photocatalyst by an in situ template solvothermal method. The CuInS2 products showed complex hierarchical features, composed of interleaving 2D microcrystals and adjacent monodispersity. During synthesis, the CuS hierarchitectures were initially created, and later acted as self-sacrificed templates to produce the CuInS2 photocatalyst. Notably, a high HER rate of ∼59.4 μmol h−1 was attained and this enhanced performance may be due to the monodispersity, high surface area, visible light response and more active sites for photocatalysis reaction. Akihiko et al.596 prepared a spinel metal sulfide of NaInS2 that comprised of anion layers of InS2, and due to its low band gap of ∼2.3 eV the photocatalytic activity for H2 evolution was tested under visible light irradiation. The NaInS2 photocatalyst exhibited a photocatalytic activity ∼28 μmol h−1 even without a co-catalyst; however, this material may undergo photocorrosion.


7.2.2 M1−xCdxS (M = Zn, Mn) photocatalysts. 
Numerous investigations have claimed that the photocatalytic performance of metal sulfide catalysts can be significantly improved by using its bi-metal form.552,597–599 For example, Kaur et al.600 prepared various Zn1−xCdxS (x = 0.1–0.9) materials using a 4,4′-dipyridyldisulfide (DPDS = (C5H4N)2S2) sulfur source. The band gap shrinkage of Zn1−xCdxS was observed to be related to the Cd concentration. The Zn0.7Cd0.3S solid solution exhibited the highest H2 production rate of ∼750 μmol h−1 g−1; see Fig. 38(i). Zhao et al.601 prepared a cubic zinc blende Zn–Cd–S via a hydrothermal technique using L-cystine. Notably, without a co-catalyst, an improved photocatalytic H2 activity was achieved over Zn0.9Cd0.1S with a generation rate of ∼4.4 mmol h−1 g−1, which was higher than CdS. In addition, Zn0.9Cd0.1S exhibited excellent anti-photocorrosion during the photoreduction of H2O into H2. The improvement in performance was credited to the efficient charge transfer of large surface area and alloyed ternary structure. Using a ZIF-8, Chen et al.602 prepared hollow ZnCdS rhombic dodecahedral cages via sulfurization and cation-exchange method, see Fig. 38(ii). The prepared hollow cages of ZnCdS exhibited a mesoporous structure with improved visible-light utilization and separation of charge carriers and transfer compared to the ZnS cages, due to the provision of abundant exposed active sites for hydrogen generation. As a consequence, the hollow Zn0.6Cd0.4S cage catalyst demonstrated the maximum hydrogen generation rate of ∼5.68 mmol h−1 g−1 without any co-catalyst in visible-light conditions. Additionally, these ZnCdS hollow cages displayed long-term stability. Using a hydrothermal method, Ikeue et al.603 prepared Mn1−xCdxS with a high concentration of Mn content (x = 0.1), which was an efficient and stable photocatalyst with the addition of RuOx for H2 production from water under visible light irradiation. As a result, the Mn1−xCdxS showed an eight times higher activity than bare CdS. Moreover, the addition of Mn to the mono-metal sulfides including NiS, ZnS, FeS, CoS, CuS, Ag2S, BaS, and SnS revealed a higher performance than bare materials. These results clearly indicate that the bi-metal structure formation of mono-sulfides with optimum Mn concentration can enhance HER activity.
 Fig. 38  (i) Schematic of (a) band positions of CdS, Zn1−xCdxS and ZnS; (b) visible-light-photocatalytic water splitting by Zn1−xCdxS photocatalyst respectively, (c) H2 evolution activity of solid solutions with time; (d) comparison of H2 production activity of Zn1−xCdxS (ZnS, x = 0.1 (S1), 0.3 (S3), 0.5 (S5), 0.9 (S9), and CdS) samples for the photocatalytic H2 production from 0.25 M Na2S and 0.3 M Na2SO3 mixed aqueous solution under visible light (>395 nm) irradiation. Reproduced from ref. 600 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018. (ii) (a) Schematic of the synthesis process of hollow ZnCdS cages from ZIF 8, photocatalytic hydrogen evolution curves of (b) hollow cages with different metal ratios; (c) individual Zn0.6Cd0.4S, a physical mixture of hollow ZnS and hollow CdS, and hollow Zn0.6Cd0.4S, under visible irradiation from a 300 W Xe lamp (λ > 420 nm, 0.75 M Na2S, and 1.05 M Na2SO3). Inset TEM image of hollow Zn0.6Cd0.4S photocatalyst. Reproduced from ref. 602 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018. 

7.3 Other bi-metal sulfides
Compared to metal oxides, the metal sulfides have initiated as a new class of materials for energy conversion and storage applications due to their low-cost and high electrochemical and photocatalytic activity. They have demonstrated attractive properties such as exceptional redox reversibility, capacitance, optical properties and high conductivity. Recently, Takayama et al.604 prepared a series of sulvanite structured Cu3VS4, Cu3NbS4, and Cu3TaS4 photocatalysts by a solid-state reaction method. The sulvanite structured Cu3MS4 (M = V, Ta, and Nb) contained edge and corner assimilated MS4 and CuS4 tetrahedrals. Notably, the Cu3VS4 photocatalyst reacted to near-IR light, up to ∼800 nm. These results indicated that photocatalytic H2 evolution progressed by band gap excitation of the photocatalyst and all materials exhibited an excellent photocatalytic HER, as shown in Fig. 39. In addition, without any co-catalyst, Zn0.957Cu0.043S exhibited a high rate of photocatalytic hydrogen evolution with a quantum yield of 3.7% under visible light in an aqueous Na2SO3 solution.605 Kale et al.606 prepared nanoprisms, nanohexagons, and nanowires of cadmium lanthanum sulfide (CdLa2S4) through a facile hydrothermal method and used them for HER activity. Due to the narrow band gap, the nanoprism shaped photocatalyst achieved a good optical response to visible light; with a high H2 production rate of ∼2552 μmol h−1.
 Fig. 39  Band structures of Cu3TaS4, Cu3NbS4, Cu3VS4, ZnIn2S4, ZnGa0.5In1.5S4, MnGaInS4, and (ZnS)0.9–(CuCl)0.1 photocatalysts. Reproduced from ref. 604 with permission from Chemical Society of Japan, copyright 2017. 
7.4 Doped metal sulfide photocatalysts

7.4.1 Cation doping. 
For solar water splitting, many of the semiconducting metal sulfides are restricted by their lack of optical absorption over the entire solar spectrum. Some materials possess a wide band, and some have photocorrosion properties which limit long-term durability.607–610 To date, metal sulfide semiconductors have been widely used as active materials and combined with materials such as Au, Pd, Pt, and Ni271,611–617 for efficient charge separation and band gap reduction, which are beneficial for high photocatalytic activity. For instance, with plasmonic Au/Ag doping on monolayer MoS2, a localized phase transition was observed,618 and a plasmonic enhanced photocatalytic H2 evolution rate of ∼1.35 μmol h−1 was observed under 690 nm laser illumination (25 mW). Likewise, as shown in Fig. 40(i), Raza et al.619 prepared Pd doped 2H-WS2 nanosheets by a sonic wave-assisted nucleation method without any reductants. It was reported that, different sizes and densities of Pd NPs on 2H-WS2 nanosheets can control the photocatalytic performance of WS2/PdNPs. Notably, the increased size of Pd nanoparticles on WS2 enhanced the photocatalytic activity through Suzuki reactions.
 Fig. 40  (i) (a–c) TEM images of 2H-WS2/PdNPs prepared at ratios of (a) 1, (b) 10, and (c) 30. (d) Possible mechanism responsible for photocatalytic Suzuki cross-coupling reactions over 2H-WS2/PdNPs, effects of (e) solvents and (f) bases on the photocatalytic activity of 2H-WS2/PdNPs. Reproduced from ref. 619 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2017. (ii) (a) Hydrogen evolution rates of CNSs and Au–CNSs photocatalysts, (b) recycling test for the hydrogen evolution performance of 0.5% Au–CNSs, (c) schematic of the proposed charge carrier transfer and hydrogen evolution mechanism in Au–CNSs hybrid photocatalysts. Reproduced from ref. 625 with permission from Wiley-VCH, copyright 2016. 
Significantly, the doping of metal ions into a semiconducting oxide photocatalysts has been widely examined, but not for metal sulfide photocatalysts. A ZnS photocatalyst with a wide band gap (3.6 eV) has a high conduction band and displays high photocatalytic activity for H2 evolution without any co-catalysts such as Pt or Ru in the existence of a sacrificial reagent. However, an outstanding photocatalytic activity was observed after ZnS was doped with low-cost metal cations such as Cu, Ni, and Pb.620–623 Similarly, band gap shrinkage was observed for Ni doped ZnS photocatalysts.571 These metal cation-doped ZnS materials exhibited higher photocatalytic HER from aqueous solutions with electron donors of S2− and/or SO32−. Zhang et al.624 synthesized nanoparticles of Au decorated ZnS photocatalyst and after the decoration of 5 nm sized Au nanoparticles on the ZnS surface, the band gap was narrowed. The band gap reduction and surface plasmonic resonance (SPR) of Au nanoparticles enabled 4 wt% of Au loaded on ZnS to display the best HER rate of ∼3306 μmol h−1 g−1 under visible light irradiation. Kuang et al.625 stated that, owing to the SPR effect, Au QDs doped CdS nanospheres exhibited the highest photocatalytic activity for H2 generation of ∼601.2 μmol h−1 g−1 under visible light, as shown in Fig. 40(ii). The enhanced activity may also be attributed to the high adhesion of the rough surface of cadmium sulfide nanospheres and interface with Au QDs, which facilitates charge separation, confines the charge recombination, and accelerates the separation of photoinduced electron–hole pairs.
The addition of a Pt co-catalyst can enhance the electroactive sites and facilitate a high H2 evolution rate. Shang et al.597 prepared microspheres of ZnIn2S2via a polyol-mediated hot injection method and by the use of co-catalyst of Pt (1 wt% loading), the ZnIn2S4 exhibited a photocatalytic H2 production rate of ∼612 mmol h−1 g−1 which was approximately four times greater than pristine ZnIn2S4 photocatalysts (∼151.8 μmol h−1 g−1). Chai et al.585 reported that Pt loaded ZnIn2S2 prepared using a template-free approach enhanced the photocatalytic activity due to increased active sites and large surface area. Depending on the cation and their combination with the ZnIn2S4 photocatalyst, the photocatalytic performance was greatly enhanced. For instance, the combination of cubic quantum dot and hexagonal microsphere ZnIn2S4 exhibited a photocatalytic HER rate of ∼114.2 μmol h−1.284 A ZnIn2S4 with different compositions of Cu (0 wt% to 2 wt%) as a dopant was introduced by Shen et al.626 At the optimum concentration, the energy band positions and optical properties of 0.5 wt% of Cu doped ZnIn2S4 showed the highest photocatalytic H2 evolution of ∼151.5 μmol h−1 under visible light. Likewise, Jing et al.627 prepared Ni2+ doped ZnIn2S4 microsphere structures with flower-like nanopetals. The optimum composition of a 0.3 wt% Ni doped ZnIn2S4 exhibited a high HER rate. However, it was observed that the use of a higher Ni2+ doping level could not lead to doping into the ZnIn2S4 host lattice and simply attached at ZnIn2S4 surface, leading to a reduced photoactivity. It was thought that Ni2+ entering the ZnIn2S4 host lattice, instead of the surface, had the strongest impact on photocatalytic activity. Tian et al.628 doped rare earth dopants such as La3+, Ce3+, Gd3+, Er3+, and Y3+ with a ZnIn2S4 photocatalyst. As a consequence, the 2.0 wt% rare earth ions of Y, Gd, Er, Ce, and La doping exhibited improved HER efficiency in terms of ∼46%, ∼53%, ∼61%, ∼69%, and ∼106%, respectively. This enhanced performance was accredited to the increased surface area and total pore volume, and the doping introduced rich defects to ZnIn2S4. Similarly, Shen et al.629 prepared a series of Ca, Sr and Ba doped ZnIn2S4 materials by a microwave-assisted hydrothermal method. The Ca-doped ZnIn2S4 displayed a superior photocatalytic performance of ∼550 μmol h−1 g−1, which was higher than pure and other metals doped ZnIn2S4 due to a suppressed charge carrier recombination via band-to-band transition in dopant of Ca in ZnIn2S4. Later, the same group630 doped Cr, Mn, Fe and Co into the host lattices of ZnIn2S4 to reduce the energy gap. Due to their narrow band gap, the Mn-doped ZnIn2S4 revealed enhanced photocatalytic activity. Conversely, the lowered activity of Cr, Fe, and Co doped ZnIn2S4 was accredited to the dopant-related impurity energy levels or they may act as centers of non-radiative recombination for photoexcited charge carriers of electrons and holes. Recently, Bai et al.631 prepared Ce3+-doped nanosheets of Zn0.8Cd0.2S–ethylenediamine (En) by solvothermal technique. The role of Ce3+ doping concentration on the photocatalytic H2 evolution was observed under visible light irradiation. As a result, the doping of Ce3+ in the form of uniformly distributed Ce2S3 on Zn0.8Cd0.2S–En hybrid nanosheets revealed a narrow band gap. Moreover, under the visible light irradiation, a maximum H2 production rate of ∼229.2 μmol h−1 was measured for 0.1 wt% Ce3+-doped Zn0.8Cd0.2S–En nanosheets, which was about ∼1.4 times higher than the pristine material. Similarly, Cu and Ni doped Cd05Zn0.5S nanoplates were fabricated by Hao et al.632 and due to the synergetic effect between Co, Ni and Cd05Zn0.5S nanoplates, the maximum H2 evolution rate of ∼58.33 mmol h−1 g−1 was attained, which was ∼3.5 times higher than pure Cd0.5Zn0.5S nanoplates. Ding et al.633 developed a bi-metallic Au–Pt with CaIn2S4, and due to the SPR effect this photocatalyst showed a greater photocatalytic hydrogen generation activity of ∼107.6 μmol h−1 g−1, which was ∼18.3, 2.37 and 2.4 times higher than pure CaIn2S4, Au–CaIn2S4, and Pt–CaIn2S4 photocatalysts. This enhanced H2 production could be ascribed to the low recombination of the photogenerated charges and surface plasmon resonance effect of Au nanoparticles, as shown in Fig. 41(i).
 Fig. 41  (i) (a) Possible mechanism of the charge separation and transportation for Au0.5−xPtx/CIS composites under visible and SPR irradiation and (b) photocatalytic hydrogen production activity of CaIn2S4, Au0.5/CIS, Pt0.5/CIS and Au0.3Pt0.2/CIS composites under 515 ≥ λ ≥ 485 nm irradiation. Reproduced from ref. 633 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2016. (ii) (a) Energy levels and variations in the Fermi levels of CdS and CdS-P (b) photocatalytic H2 evolution rates over various samples under an LED and (c) photocatalytic H2 evolution rate over 5% CoP/CdS-P, 5% CoP/CdS-P + HC (3 mg), and 5% CoP/CdS-P + PFDL (50 μL). Reproduced from ref. 634 with permission from Wiley-VCH, copyright 2018. 


7.4.2 Anion doping. 
It is recognized that the Fermi level of metal sulfides is elevated to the level of S vacancies after P doping, which enables the S vacancies to become improved electron trap centers.634 This is considered to extend the lifetime of photogenerated electrons. Recently, without any electron sacrificial agents, a phosphorus (P)-doped CdS with rich S vacancies (CdS-P) was shown to act as an effective photocatalytic material for photocatalytic hydrogen evolution under visible light irradiation.634 The pristine CdS did not show any dominant activity, while CdS-P had a moderate H2 production rate of ∼76 μmol h g−1. The photogenerated charge carriers in CdS-P with a long lifetime due to suppressed electron–hole pair recombination for surface active sites for the photocatalytic redox reaction see Fig. 41(ii). Moreover, when a small amount of co-catalyst of CoP was loaded onto the CdS-P photocatalysts, the photocatalytic H2 evolution reaction rate was enhanced significantly, particularly for 5 wt% of CoP on CdS-P exhibited a maximum HER rate of ∼231 μmol h g−1. This enhancement was associated with the separation of facile charge carrier between CdS-P and CoP. Moreover, sulfur vacancies can promote the energy conversion process. In this aspect, Ye et al.635 prepared P doped ZnxCd1−xS with rich S vacancies (ZnxCd1−xS-P). It was found that interstitial doping of P on ZnxCd1−xS with sulfur vacancies could extend the life time of charge carriers and increase the separation and generation of photogenerated electrons and holes, thus resulting in a maximum H2 production rate of ∼419 μmol h−1 g−1, which was ∼72 and 7.5 times greater than those of ZnS-P (∼5.8 μmol h−1 g−1) and CdS-P (∼56 μmol h−1 g−1) photocatalysts, respectively.

7.5 Heterostructured metal sulfides
Evolving high-quality nanocrystals of metal sulfide photocatalysts with precisely controlled optical and chemical properties is of interest for fundamental studies and practical energy conversion applications.271,611,636,637 Despite the excellent chemical stabilities, some of the metal sulfides could exhibit poor optical properties for renewable energy conversion applications. Typically, the optical properties of metal sulfides are determined by the intrinsic band gap energy. The ability to fine-tune the band gap is worthwhile for emerging metal sulfides with novel optical properties and photocatalytic applications. In this regard, recent studies are focusing on heterostructures and/or hybrid structures.271 Mostly, the heterostructures or hybrids of semiconducting materials are formed through the use of one or more semiconducting compounds on the host material, where the size confines the activities of the charge carriers for quantum confinement. This leads to the development of a set of discrete energy levels at which the charge carriers can exist.638–646 Examples of such heterostructures are now discussed.

7.5.1 MoS2 based heterostructures. 
The bulk nature and reduced inter-domain electron transport of MoS2 makes it important for photocatalytic oxidation/reduction reactions. Thus, effort has been devoted to improving the conductivity of MoS2. In order to activate the material to achieve a photocatalytic response it is essential to modify the optical properties of MoS2 through heterostructure formation.647–652 Compared to the commonly used precious metal co-catalysts (such as Pt, Pd and Au), layered transition metal sulfides such as MoS2 and WS2 show p-type active species, which are favorable for the creation of p–n-junction heterostructured photocatalysts with CdS (n-type) semiconductor for the separation of photoinduced electrons and holes, leading to efficient photocatalytic hydrogen generation.653,654 Recently, Liu et al.655 prepared unique hollow microbox structured CdS/MoS2 heterostructures using a hydrothermal method. Under visible-light, the CdS/MoS2 microboxes exhibited a higher photocatalytic H2 production rate of ∼1.36 mmol h−1 g−1 compared to pristine microboxes of CdS (∼0.04 mmol h−1 g−1). In addition, at 420 nm an apparent quantum yield of ∼14.5% was attained, as shown in Fig. 42(i). Their investigation revealed that MoS2 nanosheets can act as an electron acceptor and co-catalyst for the enrichment of electron transfer and photogenerated charge carrier separation from CdS to further encourage the surface H2 generation kinetics. In addition, the hierarchical CdS/MoS2 microboxes provide additional features such as distinct interior voids, low density and shell porosity to enhance the photocatalytic activity. Yang et al.656 used nanowires of unstable CdS as a core and multilayered MoS2 as a shell to form a CdS@MoS2 core–shell structures. The MoS2 not only acts as a shielding shell to preserve the CdS but also offers reactive sites and builds a type I junction, thereby increasing HER activity and long-term durability. As a result, an optimum H2 production rate of ∼26.14 mmol h−1 g−1 was attained, which was ∼54 times and ∼2 times higher than pristine CdS and 1 wt% Pt/CdS, respectively. Similarly, using a hydrothermal method, CdS–Au/MoS2 hierarchical core/shell heterostructures were prepared,657 see Fig. 42(ii). The addition of Au nanoparticles on CdS nanorods led to the creation of a Schottky junction and a strong SPR effect. Moreover, the few layered MoS2 nanosheets acted as a hierarchical protecting shell around the CdS nanorods, to reduce photocorrosion. A CdS–Au/MoS2 retains a large number of edge sites in the MoS2 layers which are energetic active sites for photocatalytic hydrogen evolution reaction and the heterostructure displayed outstanding photocatalytic H2 evolution which was seven times higher than pristine CdS nanorods. Zong et al.658 confirmed that the activity of MoS2 can be increased by mixing with CdS. The MoS2/CdS material displayed a greater rate of HER than Pt/CdS. It was proposed that structural combination among CdS and MoS2 due to S2− anions of each substances resulted in improved photocatalytic activity. As a result, the H2 evolution rate of CdS was increased by ∼36 times after loading of ∼0.2 wt% of MoS2, and the photocatalytic activity of MoS2/CdS was greater than the CdS photocatalysts loaded with noble metals, such as Pt, Ru, Rh, Pd, and Au. Tang et al.659 investigated a microwave-deposited MoS3 on CdSe-seeded CdS nanorod photoabsorbers. They indicated that MoS3 was as active as Pt for HER, but the photoactivity decreased as MoS3 was condensed to MoS2 over time.
 Fig. 42  (i) (a) Schematic of H2 production mechanisms on CdS/MoS2 microboxes (b) timeline of photocatalytic H2 evolution (c) hydrogen production rate of CdS and CdS/MoS2 composite catalysts with different MoS2 loading (d) repeated time courses of photocatalytic H2 evolution on CM-33 sample and (e) SEM image of CM-33 after cycling tests. Reproduced from ref. 655 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018. (ii) (a) TEM and (b) XRD pattern of CdS-5Au/MoS2 CSHNSs, (c) photocatalytic cycling experiments for CdS-5Au/MoS2 CSHNSs, each cycle of 4 h; and (d) transient photocurrent density curves for CdS NRs and CdS-5Au/MoS2 CSHNSs. Reproduced from ref. 657 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018. (iii) (a) Schematic of the synthesis of Ag2S@MoS2 NWs (b) TEM image of Ag2S@MoS2 NWs (50 wt% of MoS2) (c) optical absorption spectra and (d) photocatalytic performances of prepared samples. Schematic of the photocatalytic mechanism in (e) Ag2S@MoS2 NWs (20 wt%) and (f) Ag2S@MoS2 NWs (80 wt%); (g) schematic illustrating the different interfacial charge transfer abilities between Ag2S@MoS2NWs and pre-Ag2S@MoS2 NWs. Reproduced from ref. 660 with permission from Wiley-VCH, copyright 2018. 
The formation of Ag2S@MoS2 core–shell nanowires via the addition of different amounts of MoS2 with Ag2S was proposed by Bai et al.660 It was observed that the maximum photocatalytic activity was achieved by an optimum MoS2 loading level. Notably, a lower amount of MoS2 not only decreased the interfacial contact for inadequate electron–hole separation but also reduced the amount of catalytic active sites for photocatalytic H2 production; whereas a higher amount of MoS2 preserved the light-protecting effect of Ag2S and increased the distance of electron transfer to the catalytic active sites for H2 production, thereby decreasing performance. As a result, as shown in Fig. 42(iii), Ag2S@MoS2 NWs with a moderate amount of MoS2 (50 wt%) loading displayed the maximum H2 production rate of ∼141.6 mmol h−1 g−1. Recently, it was observed that Ni3S2/MoS2 nanostructures favor a wide range of optical absorption from 300 to 800 nm.661 As a consequence, the heterostructure of Ni3S2/MoS2 revealed a remarkable rate of H2 evolution (∼540.75 μmol g−1 h−1), which was ∼5.7 and ∼3.9 times higher than pristine Ni3S2 and MoS2, respectively. Similarly, Ta3N5 nanosheets and MoS2 (5.2 wt%) based MoS2/Ta3N5 heterostructures exhibited a H2 generation rate of 119.4 μmol h−1 g−1, which was higher than Pt/Ta3N5 nanosheets.662 In addition, WS2, MoS2, and SnS loaded onto TiO2 and CdS have also been examined for H2 evolution, where TiO2 and CdS both act as catalysts and light absorbers.663–666 The combination of metal sulfides with TiO2 based semiconductor photocatalysts is effective since TiO2 bears significant absorption of wavelength shorter than ∼400 nm, while the optical absorption of metal sulfides can be adjusted by the number of layers and by the optimum composition ratio to expand absorption of visible and near infra-red (NIR) wavelengths. For instance, MoS2 nanosheets on TiO2 nanobelts exhibited enriched visible light absorption compared to bare TiO2 nanobelts.667 Finally, TiO2 nanobelts displayed a high H2 evolution rate of ∼1.6 mmol h−1 g−1 when combined with 50 wt% of MoS2. It is thought that the similar energy levels of MoS2 and TiO2 allows facile charge transfer and prevents charge carrier recombination. Thus, considerable effort has been dedicated to improving the performance of MoS2 by heterostructure establishment and these include CdSe/MoS2,668 MoS2/AlN(GaN)669 and WS2–MoS2–CdS.654 These materials exhibited an enhanced H2 production rate, facile charge transport properties and long-term durability. Using the hydrothermal method, Ma et al.670 prepared monolayers of 3 nm sized MoS2 quantum dot confined polyimide (MQDs/PI) photocatalyst and a strong quantum confinement was observed for this photocatalyst in terms of a blue shift in the absorption band edge. Owing to the enhancement in interfacial electronic interaction between MoS2 quantum dot (MQDs) and PI revealed a high charge transfer rate of MQDs/PI. As a consequence, the MQDs/PI with a large number of active sites exhibited a ∼360% enhancement in H2 production compared to high cost Pt/PI.


7.5.2 WS2 based heterostructures. 
Increasing the activities of metal sulfide photocatalysts is a challenge in H2 generation. Hence the optimum concentration of materials are essential to form the most appropriate heterostructures; in this aspect a mass ratio of 1.6:1 was used to fabricate the WS2–CdS heterostructure by He et al.671 As a result, CdS nanorods with thin WS2 nanosheets attached were obtained. Due to the improved photogenerated charge carrier separation as a result of heterojunction formation, this heterostructure exhibited an excellent H2 generation rate of ∼1222 μmol h−1 g−1 for 20 mg catalyst under visible light. Similarly, the concentration effect of WS2 on CdS was investigated by Zong et al.672 using an impregnation–sulfidation method, whereas, the CdS/WS2 heterostructure junction formation was identified by XPS analysis. Under an optimal amount of WS2 (i.e. 1 wt%) with CdS, the rate of photocatalytic activity was increased by ∼28 times compared to pristine CdS. In addition, combining three compounds to form a strong heterojunction is of interest. As an example, Reddy et al.654 prepared a stable noble-metal-free photocatalyst of CdS/WS2–MoS2. Under visible light irradiation, the resultant CdS/WS2–MoS2 catalyst achieved high photocatalytic performance with a H2 production rate of ∼209.79 mmol g−1 h−1, which was higher than the values of other bi-junction heterostructures such as CdS/MoS2 (∼123.31 mmol h−1 g−1), CdS/WS2 (∼169.82 mmol h−1 g−1) and the CdS/Pt catalyst (∼34.98 mmol h−1 g−1). The optimized CdS/WS2–MoS2 catalyst provided an apparent quantum yield of ∼51.4% at λ = 425 nm irradiation. Their investigations showed that the heterostructured ultrathin nanosheets of WS2–MoS2 are an excellent co-catalyst to increase the solar-driven photocatalytic hydrogen generation rate by supporting photoinduced charge carrier transfer and controlling recombination of electron–hole pair and consequent reduction of protons to produce H2. Mahler et al.436 used the direct production of 2H and the 1T monolayers of colloidal WS2 by exfoliation. As a result, 1T-WS2 with TiO2 exhibited a higher rate of H2 production (2570 μmol h−1 g−1) than 2H-WS2.


7.5.3 CdS based heterostructures. 
Photocatalytic activity is a surface phenomenon, and materials with similar chemical compositions, but different exposed facets, can display a distinct performance due to their different electronic structures and atomic arrangements. The active exposure of catalytic planes can increase the catalytic activity. Notably, the metal disulfides of MoS2 or WS2 nanosheets with thickness from 4 to 10 nm on (0001) surface of wurtzite CdS nanocrystals were prepared by Chen et al.673 Both catalysts of WS2/CdS and MoS2/CdS showed increased edge sites, which led to the highest photocatalytic H2 evolution rate of ∼1984 and ∼1472 μmol h−1 g−1 respectively, which was ∼16 and 12 times higher than bare CdS (119 μmol h−1 g−1).
Significantly, in most studies to date the incorporation of a co-catalyst on the photocatalyst surface has been shown to have a significant role on catalytic activity, since the co-catalyst can afford excellent electron transfer rate, facile photogenerated charge carrier separation and high adsorption to visible light features to further encourage the surface H2 generation kinetics. Hence, the preparation of photocatalyst heterojunctions using amorphous cobalt phosphide (CoPx) with cadmium sulfide nanorods (CdS NRs) has attracted interest,674 which led to an exceptionally high photocatalytic H2 production rate under visible light with ∼500 μmol h−1 mg−1 and an apparent quantum yield (AQY) of ∼35% at λ = 450 nm. In addition, without any sacrificial agents, a Ni2P@CdS catalyst can promote charge separation and recombination of photogenerated carriers to achieve a high photocatalytic activity under visible light irradiation.675 A heterostructured photocatalyst of nickel phosphide with CdS (10Ni2P@CdS) exhibited an excellent hydrogen evolution activity of ∼251.4 μmol of H2 in 180 min with a high AQY of ∼3.89% at 430 nm. In addition, the photocatalytic activity and stability of 10Ni2P@CdS was higher than a heterostructured photocatalyst of Pt with CdS (1Pt@CdS).
Recently, in order to prepare nickel sulfide that covered CdS heterostructures, Qin et al.676 reported a one-step hydrothermal approach to fabricate a catalyst of NiSx/CdS with enhanced activity for visible light-driven HER; this was due to the dispersion of distinct nanoparticles and close contact between the two materials. As shown in Fig. 43, a novel heterostructure consisting of β-NiS film with CdS nanoflowers (NiS/CdS NFs) was prepared by Zhang et al.677 The manufactured NiS/CdS NFs had a well-formed and integrated structure with robust surface adhesion between the nanoflowers of semiconducting CdS and the co-catalyst NiS thin film, to provide a high photogenerated electron transfer rate and high adsorption to visible light features. Subsequently, the heterostructure had an efficient photocatalytic H2 production rate of 30.1 mmol h−1 g−1, and improved structural and chemical stability in 20 vol% lactic acid/aqueous solution under visible light, which was greater than other CdS–NiS composite catalysts. Later, it was observed that a maximum AQY of ∼93% was achievable for a Pt–PbS/CdS photocatalysts based H2 generation system in the presence of sacrificial reagents under visible-light.614 This higher activity was achieved with a loading level as low as 0.30 wt% of Pt and 0.13 wt% of PdS as a co-catalyst on CdS.
