In addition, the photocatalytic H2 production by CdS, Pt/CdS, Pd/CdS, Pt–Pd/CdS catalysts were also studied. The bare CdS exhibited a low activity for H2 production (∼0.023 mmol h−1), and the photoactivity was improved by loading precious metals such as Pd and Pt as co-catalysts, while the performance was amplified after Pt and Pd were co-loaded. When PdS was used as a co-catalyst, the photocatalytic performance was even greater than Pt/CdS, reaching an activity ∼280 times higher than bare CdS. In addition, the photocatalytic activity was enhanced by ∼380 times when Pt/PdS were co-loaded on the CdS catalyst, and the photocatalytic H2 generation reached ∼8.77 mmol h−1.
 Fig. 43  (a and b) Microscopic images of heterostructured NiS/CdS nanoflowers, (c) quantum efficiency (QE) for hydrogen evolution over different catalyst concentrations in 100 mL of aqueous solution containing 20 vol% lactic acid over the optimal NiS/CdS NFs HSNC photocatalyst under monochromatic light (λ = 420 ± 5 nm) provided by a 300 W Xe lamp with a 420 nm band-pass filter. (d) Hydrogen evolution and QE over the optimal NiS/CdS NFs HSNC photocatalyst with time, in which the experiments were carried out under the same light irradiation condition as that in (c). During the photocatalytic reaction, 20 mg of the optimal NiS/CdS NFs photocatalyst was dispersed in a 100 mL of aqueous solution containing 20 vol% lactic acid; and the reaction cell was kept at 25 °C by cooling water. Reproduced from ref. 677 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018. 
Similarly, due to its heterostructure nature, an optimized NiCo2S4/CdS composite exhibited a high H2 production rate of ∼137 μmol h−1 when illuminated by visible light, which was ∼17 times higher than the pristine CdS material.678 In addition, Wang et al.679 prepared a CdS/NiTiO3/CoS heterojunction to increase visible light H2 production using a semiconducting CdS. Due to the wide spectrum response of the CdS/NiTiO3/CoS nanocomposite, the development of a CdS/NiTiO3 heterojunction, and the decoration of CoS as a co-catalyst, a superior photocatalytic performance was achieved compared to bare samples. As a result, a maximum HER activity of ∼2381 μmol was attained under visible to NIR irradiation, which was ∼22 times higher than the bare CdS. Furthermore, photo-corrosion was inhibited for this CdS/NiTiO3/CoS heterostructure.
Hou et al.680 prepared 20 nm sized CdS decorated CdLa2S4 microspheres (CdS/CdLa2S4) by a two-step hydrothermal process where highly crystalline CdS with a hexagonal structure was attained in the CdS/CdLa2S4. The structural features resulted in an intimate multi-point contact between the CdS and CdLa2S4, which delivered an improvement in charge separation of the CdS/CdLa2S4 heterostructure. A CdLa2S4 with 3 wt% CdS including 0.4 wt% of Pt provided a H2 generation rate of ∼2.25 mmol h−1 with an AQY of ∼54% under illumination by 420 nm light. This rate of HER was ∼9 times higher than that for CdLa2S4, which was thought to be due to the existence of 20 nm sized CdS nanocrystals that can modify the energy levels of the conduction and valence bands in the combined semiconductor system. Zou et al.681 prepared CdS/Sr2(Nb17/18Zn1/18)2O1−δ heterostructured composites by varying the CdS content, among them 30% CdS/Sr2(Nb17/18Zn1/18)2O1−δ (30CSNZO) exhibited the highest H2 production activity of ∼1669.1 μmol h−1 g−1, which was higher than the pristine material.

7.6 Other heterostructured metal sulfides
To date, several metal sulfides have been used to create heterostructures.682–685 However an ideal practical catalyst for H2 production is yet to emerge. Thus, it is essential to develop Earth abundant, environmental-friendly, cost-effective and highly efficient noble-metal-free H2 evolution photocatalysts with the aim of delivering cost-competitive hydrogen fuel. In this regard, Qin et al.686 observed that the formation of a heterojunction using a NiS2 co-catalyst resulted in a remarkable improvement in photocatalytic H2 production compared to a bare semiconducting photocatalyst under visible light. The NiS/Ni3S4 catalyst was fabricated via a hydrothermal method. As a result, the NiS/Ni3S4 retained the maximum adsorbed protons, which led to enhanced photocatalytic properties and the catalyst exhibited an AQY of ∼56.5% at 420 nm, demonstrating the intrinsic property for well-organized H2 generation. Similarly, owing to the enhanced photogenerated electron and hole separation and the activation effect of NiS2 on CdLa2S4, the optimized 2 wt% NiS2/CdLa2S4 exhibited a high H2 evolution rate of ∼2.5 mmol h−1 g−1, which was ∼3 times higher than a bare CdLa2S4 photocatalyst.687
A chalcopyrite-type structured copper gallium sulfide (CuGa3S5) with a band gap of ∼2.4 eV was fabricated by Tabata et al.688 A 5.0 wt% NiS/CuGa3S5 catalyst was also prepared by modification of the bare sample. Even without a co-catalyst, under visible light irradiation the 5.0 wt% NiS/CuGa3S5 exhibited an improved photocatalytic activity which was ∼3 times higher than Rh/CuGa3S5. Their examination of electrochemical and photocatalytic reactions suggested that the NiS dispersed in the reactant solution received electrons from the excited state of the host CuGa3S5 catalyst to reduce H2O into H2, whereas NiS and CuGa3S5 nanoparticles collided with each other in the reactant aqueous solution. Moreover, co-catalysts of CuS689 and NiS690 were introduced to modify the photocatalytic activity of TiO2, and demonstrated an improvement. A cobalt sulfide quantum dot (CoSx QD) modified TiO2 photocatalyst was prepared by Yu et al.691via a precipitation technique. Quantum dots of CoSx can act as an active co-catalyst, which improves photo-generated electron transport and also act as active catalytic sites for water splitting reaction, thereby increasing the photocatalytic H2 generation activity of TiO2 by the apparent separation of the e−/h+ pairs. As a consequence, a 5% CoSx with TiO2 exhibited a H2 production rate of ∼838 μmol h−1 g−1 in the presence of sacrificial reagent (ethanol), which was ∼35 times higher than bare TiO2.
Heterostructures of hollow materials with a well-organized light harvesting component with tunable interior constituents offer benefits for constructing an efficient energy conversion system. In this aspect, using hollow Co(OH)2 as a template, hollow Co9S8 cubes with CdS quantum dots (QDs) was prepared by Qiu et al.692 By utilizing multiple light reflections as it entered the cubic structure, the hollow CdS/Co9S8 cubes exhibited superior photocatalytic activity with a HER rate over ∼134 and 9.1 times higher than pristine hollow Co9S8 and CdS QDs respectively, see Fig. 44. This structure also offers complete light-harvesting and charge separation.
 Fig. 44  (a) SEM and (b) TEM images of hollow CdS/Co9S8, (c) time course of hydrogen evolution under irradiation of a 300 W Xe lamp (AM1.5 air mass filter) over different samples with pure CdS and Co9S8 and (d) illustration of multiple reflections within the CdS/Co9S8 hollow structure and the photoexcited charge-carrier distribution. Reproduced from ref. 692 with permission from Wiley-VCH, copyright 2017. 
Recently, the conversion of H2S to useable hydrogen has become an active research area. Using a one step solvothermal method, a highly active In2S3/CuS heterostructure showed a remarkable visible light absorption with an edge of absorption at ∼785 nm, which is beneficial to harvest the complete light spectrum.693 As a consequence, the In2S3/CuS composite exhibited an excellent photocatalytic activity under visible light and long-term durability for the reduction of H2S in terms of maximum H2 production rate of ∼14950 μmol g−1 h−1 without any precious metal co-catalysts, which was ∼404 and ∼87 times larger than bare Cu9S5 and β-In2S3 respectively. In addition, the In2S3/CuS heterostructure had an AQY of ∼9.3% at 420 nm. This excellent activity was influenced by the chemical structure, crystalline phase, optical nature and nanosheet-like structure of the In2S3/CuS heterostructure, which improved electron transfer between the interfaces of β-In2S3 and CuS, and enhanced the photogenerated electron–hole pair separation. Similarly, a Pt-loaded ZnS–CuInS2–AgInS2 photocatalyst provided photocatalytic activity for H2 in the presence of SO32− and S2− sacrificial reagents under irradiation of visible light (λ ≥ 420 nm).694 Notably, the energy band structure could be controlled by a change in composition. Hence, the ZnS–CuInS2–AgInS2 photocatalyst displayed absorption bands that were longer in wavelength than ZnS–CuInS2 and ZnS–AgInS2 solid solutions, which is possibly due to interactions between the Cu 3d and Ag 4d orbitals. Moreover, the Ru-loaded Cu0.25Ag0.25In0.5ZnS2 heterostructure, with the small band gap of ∼1.77 eV, generated H2 gas at a rate of ∼7.9 m−2 h−1. Similarly, due to its high optical absorption due to its heterostructure nature, CuS/TiO2 revealed a maximum photocatalytic H2 evolution rate of ∼1262 μmol h−1 g−1, which was ∼9.7 and 9.3 times higher than the bare TiO2 nanospindles and CuS nanoflakes under visible light.695 In addition, the core–shell CuInS2/ZnS QDs had a H2 production rate higher than bare CuInS2 and ZnS QDs.696
Similarly, Kang et al.697 attained a maximum H2 generation rate of ∼559 μmol g−1 h−1 for a solvothermally manufactured ZnS(en)0.5–CdS using 80 wt% of CdS. In addition, due to the efficient charge transfer response, the PdS-loaded ZnS(en)0.5–CdS displayed a good H2 production rate of ∼2600 μmol h−1 g−1, which was greater than PdS-loaded CdS and Cd0.6Zn0.4S catalysts.698 Du et al.699 attained a high performance of H2 generation using a metallic 1T-LixMoS2/Cd0.5Zn0.5S photocatalyst. The obtained heterostructure demonstrated excellent photocatalytic H2 generation from aqueous solution containing Na2S and Na2SO3 under visible light. During fabrication, the insertion of Li+ ions led to the transition of bulk MoS2 from a semiconductor trigonal 2H phase to a metallic 1T phase. The discrete work functionalities of 1T-LixMoS2 promoted the separation of photoexcited charge carriers when used as a co-catalyst with Cd0.5Zn0.5S. As a result, (1.0 wt%) 1T-LixMoS2/Cd0.5Zn0.5S junctions displayed a high HER rate of ∼769.9 μmol h−1, which was ∼3.5 and ∼2 times higher than bare Cd0.5Zn0.5S and (1.0 wt%) 2H-MoS2/Cd0.5Zn0.5S catalysts, respectively. Their outcomes demonstrated that Li+-intercalated MoS2 nanosheets provide high conductivity and exposed active sites to enhance performance. Nguyen700 reported a scalable fabrication method for MoS2/ZnxCd1−xS photocatalysts at various concentrations for H2 production. The best catalyst of Zn0.2Cd0.8S/MoS2 3% exhibited ∼210 times higher H2 production rate in visible light compared to bare Zn0.2Cd0.8S; this was a remarkable photocatalytic improvement in a heterojunction system.
By loading Pt with a wide band ZnS and narrow band gap CuInS2, Tsuji et al.701 prepared a photocatalyst of (CuIn)xZn2(1−x)S2 with a band gap of 2.3 eV. Due to its optimum band gap, this photocatalyst exhibited the highest H2 evolution activity of ∼1.5 L h−1 m−2 and the AQY was ∼12.5% at 420 nm. Their DFT models indicated that the visible-light response may result from the influence of Cu 3d and S 3p orbitals on the valence band and that of orbitals of In 5s5p and Zn 4s4p to the conduction band, respectively. The role of these orbitals to the energy bands exaggerated the photocatalytic and photophysical properties. By similar strategies, several research groups have achieved a high performance by forming heterojunctions of metal sulfides. For instance, Li et al.702 prepared ZnS-coated ZnIn2S4 photocatalyst, which exposed photocatalytic HER in the existence of glucose, and a production rate of ∼25 μmol h−1 g−1 was observed at a pH = 12. By using a two-step process, Wei et al.703 produced ZnIn2S4/NiS and used it as a photocatalyst. Their investigations indicated that NiS acts as an energetic co-catalyst, which aids in directional passage of photo-excited carriers, and provides catalytic active sites for H2 evolution. Interestingly, a heterojunction between NiS and ZnIn2S4 achieved a H2 rate of ∼104.7 μmol h−1, which was ∼1.35 times higher than Pt/ZnIn2S4 (∼77.8 μmol h−1). Mei et al.704 manufactured In2S3/ZnIn2S4 through an ion exchange method and the photocatalytic performance was higher than the bare samples. In addition, Ni(OH)2 was prepared as a co-catalyst with microspheres of ZnIn2S4 which exhibited a reasonable photocatalytic H2 evolution of ∼8.35 mmol g−1 h−1, which was ∼18 times larger than pristine ZnIn2S4. The assistance of Ni(OH)2 improved interfacial charge transfer and suppressed the electron–hole pair recombination.705
7.7 Graphene and/or carbon based metal sulfide photocatalysts
Graphene and/or carbon based metal sulfide photocatalysts with heterostructured compounds have attracted attention in photocatalytic H2 evolution research. A recent investigation by Bernardi et al.706 observed that 2D materials can absorb larger amounts of visible light compared to conventional photovoltaic semiconductors such as silicon or GaAs. Meng et al.616 reported high photocatalytic activity for a MoS2/rGO composite in which MoS2 was the light-harvesting material. In contrast, the MoS2/rGO composite had an enhanced H2 evolution rate of 7.4 μmol h−1 g−1, which was further increased to 24.8 μmol h−1 g−1 when the composite of rGO was doped with nitrogen. In addition, a VS4/rGO nanocomposite was synthesized by Guo et al.707 using 5 wt% rGO. During photocatalytic testing, a maximum H2 production rate of ∼0.7 mmol L−1 was observed. Ye et al.708 prepared a sheet-on-sheet morphology of rGO/ZnIn2S4 nanocomposites via a solvothermal method. The rGO reduction during composite formation enhanced the conductive nature of the composites, notably, 1.0 wt% rGO/ZnIn2S4 nanocomposite revealed an enhanced photoactivity of ∼245.1 μmol in 6 h, which was ∼4.3 times higher than the bare ZnIn2S4. This enhanced activity was attributed to the band gap of ZnIn2S4 and the more electrocatalytic active sites, good electron acceptor and mediator as well as the co-catalyst properties of the rGO.
The impact of 0D carbon quantum dots, 1D carbon nanotubes, and 2D reduced graphene oxide with ZnIn2S4 and their use for the H2 evolution reaction was analyzed by Xia et al.709 Compared to the other catalysts, rGO/ZnIn2S4 had a higher photocatalytic H2 production rate of ∼2640.8 mmol g−1 h−1. The enhanced activity of rGO/ZnIn2S4 was accredited to a high surface area provided by graphene which offered more active sites and larger area for transfer of charge-carriers. Mahadadalkar et al.710 prepared graphene with a CdIn2S4 composite, which exhibited improved photocatalytic performance of ∼4495 μmol h−1. The existence of rGO in the metal sulfide structures improved effective hole–electron separation and conductivity, which leads to reduced charge combination in the composite system. Similarly, Zhou et al.711 prepared ZnIn2S4/rGO nanosheets through a precise solvothermal method and due to the presence of rGO, the surface area of ZnIn2S4/rGO (∼92 m2 g−1) was doubled compared to the bare ZnIn2S4. As a result, 0.05 g of ZnIn2S4/rGO exhibited a maximum H2 generation rate of ∼81.6 mmol h−1 which was reduced to ∼40.3 mmol h−1 for 0.1 g of ZnIn2S4/rGO catalyst. Similarly, microspheres of a flower-like rGO/ZnIn2S4 photocatalyst were prepared using a microwave-assisted method,712 which provided a high photocatalytic H2 production rate of ∼132.3 μmol h−1, which was higher than 1.0 wt%-Pt/ZnIn2S4. Kale et al.713 achieved a photocatalytic H2 production rate of ∼6365 μmol h−1 with 0.2 g of catalyst of 3D ZnIn2S4/rGO heterostructure. As shown in Fig. 45(i), Zhang et al.714 obtained a HER rate of ∼1824 μmol h−1 g−1 with the AQY of ∼23.4% at 420 nm, in which the rGO content was ∼0.25 wt% in rGO–Zn0.8Cd0.2S photocatalyst.
 Fig. 45  (i) (a) Schematic of charge transfer and separation in the rGO–Zn0.8Cd0.2S system, (b and c) TEM images of rGO–Zn0.8Cd0.2S photocatalysts and (d) transient photocurrent responses of the GS0 (a), GS0 loaded with 1 wt% Pt (b), and GS0.25 (c) photocatalytic H2 generation performances of prepared photocatalysts in 0.1 M Na2S + 0.02 M Na2SO3 mixed aqueous solution under solar irradiation. Reproduced from ref. 714 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2012. (ii) (a) Stability study of photocatalytic H2 production activity and (b) transient photocurrent responses of the g-C3N4, CaIn2S4, and 30% CaIn2S4/g-C3N4 heterojunction nanocomposite. Reproduced from ref. 718 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2015. 
Jia et al.617 prepared a series of nitrogen-doped graphene (N-graphene)/CdS nanocomposites by varying the N-graphene ratio. The optimum quantity of N-graphene was ∼2 wt%, at which the N-graphene/CdS catalyst reached the highest H2 generation of ∼210 μmol h−1, while ∼99 μmol h−1 for graphene/CdS and ∼95 μmol h−1 for GO/CdS was observed. Chai et al.715 fabricated a MWCNT/ZnIn2S4 by a hydrothermal method. Notably, 3 wt% of MWCNT on ZnIn2S4 had an improved H2 production rate of ∼684 mmol h−1. The addition of the optimal amount of MWCNT increased the charge transfer and provided higher charge separation efficiency, which was higher than materials with a lower and higher amount of 3 wt% of MWCNT. By a similar strategy, Xia et al.716 attempted to enhance the photocatalytic properties by employing CNT as a hybrid material with CaIn2S4. Benefitting from the stability and conductive nature of CNT, the composite material showed an improved crystalline nature and large surface area so that the CaIn2S4 exhibited a photocatalytic H2 generation rate of ∼23.4 μmol h−1 g−1.
Pramoda et al.511 prepared a C3N4–MoS2 nanocomposite via a solution technique, where C3N4 was covalently bonded to MoS2 or N-rGO nanosheets. Due to these chemical features, the high photocatalytic HER activity of ∼12778 μmol h−1 g−1 and a turnover frequency of 2.35 h−1 were attained for the C3N4–MoS2 nanocomposite. This enhanced performance was attributed to the effective charge-transfer between the components as well as larger numbers of cross-linked layers. To date, a polymeric photocatalyst based on graphitic carbon nitride (g-C3N4), has received attention because of its good chemical and thermal stability, and response to visible-light. By using the sacrificial reagents during photocatalytic H2 production, the CdS/g-C3N4 core/shell nanowires exhibited a high rate of ∼4152 μmol h−1 g−1, where the composite had 2 wt% of g-C3N4.717 The synergic effect between CdS and g-C3N4, which can efficiently facilitate charge separation and transfer the corrosive holes from CdS to durable C3N4, was thought to be responsible for improving photocatalytic performance and photostability. Jiang et al.718 achieved ∼102 μmol g−1 h−1 for CaIn2S4/g-C3N4 under visible light, which was ∼3 times higher than the bare CaIn2S4, see Fig. 45(ii). The improved photocatalytic activity of the CaIn2S4/g-C3N4 heterojunction was due to improved charge separation efficiency.
However, there remains a challenge to improve both the number of active sites and charge separation of metal sulfide semiconductors to create highly efficient and long-term durable photocatalytic HER. Shen et al.719 attempted to solve this issue, where acetylene black (AB) acts as the electron bridge that connects the nanosheets of g-C3N4 (CN) and CuS. The ternary CN–0.5%AB–2%CuS catalyst exhibited a H2 production rate of ∼348 μmol h−1 g−1, which was ∼348 and 1.5 times of those for pristine CN (∼1 μmol h−1 g−1) and CN–1%Pt (∼230 μmol h−1 g−1), respectively; the catalysts also had long-term durability. Notably, the AB nanoparticles act as oriented electron-transfer channels that improve separation and collection of photoexcited carriers and CuS stability, while the more active sites provided by the CuS increased the surface H2 evolution reaction. In addition, acetylene black and CuS increased the light absorption of the composite.

7.7.1 Double metal sulfide heterostructures with carbon or graphene. 
The use of hybridization with carbon materials and metal sulfide heterostructures are beneficial, whereby one constituent provides light absorption for the generation of an electrochemical potential, and the other provides more catalytic active sites for hydrogen production, and also enhances catalysts conductivity, e.g. using reduced graphene oxide (rGO) or carbon materials such as CNT.720–725 For example, Xiang et al.722 examined the photocatalytic activity of TiO2/MoS2/graphene composites. It was observed that rGO is beneficial for easing photogenerated electrons transport from TiO2 to MoS2 and for improving charge-separation properties, which resulted in a high H2 evolution rate of ∼165.3 μmol h−1 and the AQY was ∼9.7% at 365 nm, whereas the loading of the MoS2/graphene co-catalyst was 0.5 wt% and the content of graphene was 5.0 wt%. In addition, a ternary CdS/MoS2/graphene combination catalyst exhibited an improved photocatalytic activity for H2 generation compared with CdS, CdS–MoS2, and CdS–graphene materials.565,726
A ternary hierarchical composite of Cd0.5Zn0.5S–g-C3N4–MoS2 was developed by Gogoi et al.727 Due to band gap alignment and effective charge separation, the Cd0.5Zn0.5S–g-C3N4–MoS2 material had an excellent photocatalytic performance of ∼68 μmol h−1 for 0.02 g catalyst. As a result of improved optical absorption, the NiS-MoS2/CNTs/CN catalyst showed a H2 generation rate of ∼309.9 μmol h−1 g−1 with long-term durability.728 The optimal loading of layered WS2/graphene hybrid (WG; amount ∼4.2 wt%) acted as a co-catalyst in the CdS/WS2/graphene composites, and resulted in a visible light photocatalytic H2 generation rate of ∼1842 μmol h−1 g−1 with AQY of ∼21.2% at 420 nm.729 A 2.0 wt% of MoS2/graphene co-catalyst used a MoS2/g-CdS composite and achieved the highest photocatalytic H2 evolution activity of ∼1.8 mmol h−1 in a lactic acid solution equivalent to an AQY of 28.1% at ∼420 nm, which was not only greater than the sacrificial solutions of Na2S–Na2SO3 of ∼1.2 mmol h−1 but considerably larger than Pt/CdS in a solution of lactic acid.565 Similarly, Ha et al.730 prepared a Cu2ZnSnS4/MoS2–rGO heterostructure, in which a high density of nanoscale surface interaction between Cu2ZnSnS4 (CZTS) and MoS2/rGO hybrid was attained through in situ growth of CZTS nanoparticles on the surface of MoS2/rGO. As a result of the structural interface, see Fig. 46, a 90 wt% CZTS on 10 wt% MoS2/rGO showed an excellent photocatalytic activity of H2 generation in terms of ∼52 μmol g−1 h−1, which was higher than pristine Cu2ZnSnS4, Pt/Cu2ZnSnS4, and Au/Cu2ZnSnS4. Zhu et al.731 investigated the effect of La and rGO content in ZnIn2S4 respectively. A photocatalytic H2 production rate of 1.0RGO/1.0La–ZnIn2S4 of ∼2255 μmol g−1 h−1 was achieved. Zhang et al.732 prepared a ternary ZnxCd1−xS-based material with NiS and reduced graphene oxide (rGO). In this system, rGO acted as an electron collector and transporter and provided active reduction sites for H2 production. In addition, the metal sulfide of NiS served as an active oxidation site to collect photogenerated holes due to the creation of nanoscale p–n junctions between NiS and ZnxCd1−xS nanoparticles. As a consequence, the NiS–rGO–ZnxCd1−xS photocatalyst reached a maximum H2 production rate of ∼375.7 μmol h−1, which surpassed the rate gained for Zn0.5Cd0.5S/rGO and NiS alone by ∼1.8 and 1.6 times, respectively. The technology of heterostructure formation with carbon materials has been widely used to modify a variety of UV-light-active photocatalysts.112,724,733
 Fig. 46  (a) TEM and (b, c) HR-TEM images of CZTS/MoS2–rGO composites, (d and e) Illustration and energy diagram of interfacial charge transfer and photocatalytic redox reaction in CZTS/MoS2–rGO composites and (f) comparison of photocatalytic H2 evolution from various CZTS/MoS2–rGO composites with different ratios of MoS2–rGO co-catalyst. Reproduced from ref. 730 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2017. 

7.8 Porosity effects
CdS attached to WO3/WS2 nanorods (NRs) have been fabricated by Wang et al.734via a two-step process. It was observed that the surface area was increased by ∼1.8 times due to the addition of 40 wt% CdS on WO3/WS2. The heterostructure is also a key factor for the photocatalytic performance. As a result, the optimized CdS/WO3/WS2 NRs nanocomposite exhibited ∼11.4 times enhancement in photocurrent and it was six times better than WO3/WS2 nanorods. As shown in Fig. 47(i), Yang et al.735 prepared porous ZnS/Ag2S nanostructures with different pore dimensions. During photocatalytic testing, the Ag2S nanocrystals acted as hole scavengers, as a result, without any co-catalysts, the porous nanosheets displayed a high photocatalytic H2 evolution rate of ∼104.9 μmol h−1 g−1. Benefitting from a two step aqueous process, Pt loaded nanoporous CdS nanostructures, including nanosheets and hollow nanorods were prepared by Bao et al.373 The nanoporous CdS nanosheets exhibited sizes up to ∼60 nm and an average thickness of ∼9 nm; where the hollow nanorods of CdS revealed ∼30 nm lengths with ∼7–14 nm outer diameters. Moreover, the achieved CdS nanostructures exhibited ∼3 nm diameter nanopores and a high BET surface area of ∼112.8 m−1 g−1. As a consequence, a high photocatalytic H2 yield of ∼4.1 mmol h−1, corresponding to the highest AQY of ∼60.34% measured at 420 nm. This improvement was due to effective charge separation, rapid photogenerated carrier transport, and a rapid photocatalytic reaction at the CdS/electrolyte interface due to the large surface area, porous nature and optimized chemical composition.
 Fig. 47  (i) TEM and HR-TEM images of (a and b) ZnS, (c–e) ZnS:Ag2S-2 nanostructure, and (f and g) ZnS:Ag2S-8 nanostructure, (h and i) photocatalytic H2 evolution of ZnS (curve a), ZnS:Ag2S-1 (curve b), ZnS:Ag2S-2 (curve c), and ZnS:Ag2S-8 (curve d) porous nanostructures. (b) Stability of ZnS:Ag2S-2. Light source: 300 W xenon lamp. Reproduced from ref. 735 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2014. (ii) (a and b) TEM images of porous CuS/ZnS photocatalyst, (c) nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms and the corresponding pore size distribution curves of ZnS(en)0.5 precursor, CZ0, and CZ2 and (d) comparison of the visible light photocatalytic H2-production activity of CuS/ZnS porous nanosheets and ZnS samples under visible light (λ ≥ 420 nm) with the mixed aqueous solution containing 0.35 M Na2S and 0.25 M Na2SO3 as sacrificial solution; light source: 350 W xenon arc lamp. Reproduced from ref. 737 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2011. 
Recently, using an polyol-mediated hot-injection synthesis technique, Shang et al.597 prepared a variety of photocatalysts such as spherical flower-like ZnIn2S4 superstructure with a diameter of ∼500 nm composed of 2–3 nm thick ultrathin mesoporous nanosheets, and other metal sulfide nanostructured photocatalysts of quantum dots of CdS, nanorods of Bi2S3, In2S3 hierarchical flower and SnS spheres. Owing to the outstanding specific surface area, the 1 wt% Pt loaded ZnIn2S4 hierarchical superstructure catalyst exhibited proficient visible-light-driven photocatalytic H2 generation (612 μmol h−1 g−1) compared with flower-like ZnIn2S4 microspheres synthesized via a conventional hydrothermal technique. This enhanced performance was attributed to the larger surface area (128 m2 g−1), which may provide more photocatalytic active sites and adsorb a large number of photons and reactants. To examine the effect of doping of La3+ and porosity on the photocatalytic performance of the ZnIn2S4 photocatalyst, a series of La3+-doped porous ZnIn2S4 were prepared by Tian et al.736 The results indicated that La existed in the form of La2O3 on the ZnIn2S4 petals surface. A higher amount of La decreased the crystallite size of ZnIn2S4 and stimulates ZnIn2S4 micro-sphere of flower-like morphology, which also increased the surface area and pore volume, and create rich defects in ZnIn2S4. As a result, the photocatalytic HER efficiency was increased by ∼142% after adding 1.0 wt% La on ZnIn2S4. Without using a noble metal co-catalyst, the bare CuS/ZnS porous nanosheets attained a high photocatalytic H2 generation rate of ∼4147 μmol h−1 g−1 at 2 mol% of CuS loading and an AQY of 20% at 420 nm,737 as shown in Fig. 47(ii). This enhanced activity was due to the photoinduced interfacial charge transfer from the catalyst valence band of ZnS to CuS, which results in the reduction of incomplete CuS to Cu2S and thus improves the H2 generation reaction. The porous structure also facilitated the transport of reactants and products over the internal space due to the organized porous network assisting in using back reflected light due to the increased surface area and light scattering inside the porous structure.
Bai et al.738 reported on microspheres of ZnIn2S4 with a high surface area of ∼165.4 m2 g−1 made by a cetylpyridinium bromide (CPBr) aided hydrothermal process. The concentration of CPBr and pH significantly affected the electrocatalytic reactions and ZnIn2S4 formed at pH = 2 had a higher photocatalytic activity of ∼1544.8 μmol h−1 g−1, which was credited to its flower-like microsphere surface morphology with a pore assembly which supported electron and hole transfer. A mesoporous structure and a chain-like surface morphology with large surface area and high pore volume of monoclinic CaIn2S4 was produced via a high-temperature sulfurization method.739 It was then modified with 0.5 wt% Pt nanoparticles, and due to this structural feature a high HER rate of ∼30.2 μmol h−1 under visible light with good stability was realized in Na2S/Na2SO3 aqueous solution. Due to the large surface area (∼47 m2 g−1) of the PdS/ZnIn2S4/CdIn2S4 photocatalysts, Yu et al.740 achieved ∼780 μmol h−1.
7.9 Effect of defects
Among several photocatalysts, ZnS is one of the most commonly studied photocatalysts for H2 generation; however its wide band gap limits its activity to the UV region. Hence, Feng et al.741 proposed experimental and DFT analysis to facilitate ZnS to improve performance for H2 evolution using defect engineering and phase junction construction. Initially, it was suggested that the controlled vacancies of sulfur in a wurtzite structure phase of ZnS could provide photosensitization components for facile charge creation under visible light conditions and active sites for effective electron use. The synergetic sphalerite–wurtzite (S–W) structure phase junctions control the photoinduced charge separation and charge-transfer kinetics, while the ZnS with optimized phase junctions and extreme sulfur vacancies offered considerable photocatalytic activity. Hence, both sulfur vacancies and an S–W junction for ZnS (temperature 200 °C) led to a high photocatalytic H2 generation of ∼18 μmol h−1 g−1. Later, the same group742 prepared an organic–inorganic hybrid semiconductor of D-ZnS(en)0.5 with abundant defect states through grain boundary engineering, see Fig. 48(i). Due to the enriched defect state excitation and elevated electrical properties, D-ZnS(en)0.5 revealed an enhanced visible-light-driven photocatalytic hydrogen evolution rate of ∼242.6 μmol h−1 g−1, which was ∼22 times greater than that of defect free ZnS(en)0.5 (∼11.0 μmol h−1 g−1). Likewise, Hao et al.743 introduced zinc vacancies (VZn) in ZnS through the addition of sodium sulfide as a sulfur source. The rich zinc vacancies on ZnS were observed by fluorescence spectroscopy and XPS analysis. Notably, the Zn vacancies could increase the valence band (VB) position that deteriorates the oxidative nature of the holes to shield ZnS-vacancies in ZnS from photo-corrosion. As a result, zinc-deficient ZnS (ZnS-2.5) with the most appropriate level of Zn vacancies showed higher photocatalytic performance for H2 production, of ∼337.71 ± 3.72 μmol h−1 g−1 under visible-light; high photostability was also observed.
 Fig. 48  (i) TEM image (a), HR-TEM image (b) and (c) SAED pattern of defected ZnS(en)0.5 (named as D-ZnS(en)0.5), (d) structural model of the disordered structure in D-ZnS(en)0.5 and (e) photocatalytic H2 evolution over defect free ZnS(en)0.5 and Defected-ZnS(en)0.5 under visible light irradiation (λ > 420 nm) (e). Reproduced from ref. 742 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2017. (ii) TEM (a) and HR-TEM (b) images of 20 wt% DRM-C sample (defect-rich O-integrated 1T-MoS2 nanosheets: DRM), (c) photocatalytic H2 evolution rates for DRM, CdS, Pt/CdS, and DRM-C and DFM-C containing different amounts of co-catalyst (10, 15, 20, 25, 30 wt%) under visible light irradiation, (d) wavelength-dependent AQY and hydrogen evolution from water by 20 wt% DRM-C and 20 wt% DFM-C, (e) cyclic H2 production on 20 wt% DRM-C and 20 wt% DFM-C, and (f) photocatalytic H2 production catalyzed by 20 wt% DRM-C under a prolonged visible light irradiation of 30 h. Conditions: 2 mg CdS, 20 mL aqueous solution with 2 mL lactic acid and 500 W metal halide lamp with an optical cutoff filter (λ > 420 nm). Reproduced from ref. 744 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018. 
Recently, Zhang et al.744 prepared defect-rich O-integrated 1T-MoS2 nanosheets (DRM), and defect free 1T-MoS2 nanosheets (DFM) by a facile hydrothermal process through the addition of high and small amount of thiourea precursor, respectively. Their hybrids were also prepared by DRM or DFM with CdS nanorods (indicated as DRM-C and DFM-C), as shown in Fig. 48(ii). A comparative investigation on DRM and DFM validated that the DRM co-catalyst displayed rich defects in the basal planes and edges of MoS2, and increased density of electro-active sites for the photocatalytic HER reaction. As a consequence, the elevated 20 wt% DRM-C revealed a visible-light driven photocatalytic H2 generation rate of ∼132.4 mmol h−1 g−1, greater than that of 20 wt% DFM-C (∼102.1 mmol h−1 g−1), bare CdS (∼20.0 mmol h−1 g−1) and Pt/CdS (∼89.0 mmol h−1 g−1). In addition, Zhang et al.745 prepared defect rich CdS@MoS2 through a one-step growth of oxygen fused defect-rich ultrathin MoS2 nanosheets on the CdS surfaces with irregular openings. As a result, the CdS@MoS2 exhibited maximum H2 generation rate of ∼17203 μmol h−1 g−1 and an AQY of ∼24.2% at 420 nm using sacrificial agents of Na2S/Na2SO3, which was ∼47.3 and ∼14.7 times greater than pristine CdS (∼363.8 μmol h−1 g−1) and 3 wt% Pt/CdS (∼1173.2 μmol h−1 g−1), respectively. The enhanced H2 production was attributed to the formation of adjacent heterojunctions between the CdS and MoS2, which improve the charge transfer capacity and hinder electron–hole pair recombination. Moreover, the defects in the MoS2 provide extra active sites for sulfur atoms on the exposed edge sites, and oxygen incorporation minimizes the energy barrier for facile H2 evolution reaction and increases the electrical conductivity of MoS2.
7.10 Processing temperature effects
A temperature effect, such as calcination or annealing temperature, is a common method to execute structural reformation in bulk materials and reduce structural defects in materials. Appropriate annealing was shown to be effective in increasing the performance of several photocatalysts. Jing et al.575 investigated the photocatalytic HER activity of high temperature (200–500 °C) treated CdS photocatalyst, which resulted in the growth of a highly stable and highly active CdS photocatalyst. In addition, during photocatalytic testing, CdS treated at 400 °C was found to be highly stable against both photo-corrosion and oxidation in air. As a consequence, the CdS-400 °C photocatalyst revealed an AQY and conversion efficiency of ∼24.1% and ∼6.35%, respectively. A broad range annealing temperature of 150–500 °C revealed either cubic, a combination of cubic and hexagonal, or hexagonal CdS nanocrystals.746 In particular, the cubic CdS was stable at ∼500 °C for 0.5 h, and after 1 h annealing, it was transformed to hexagonal CdS. An appropriate temperature effect to achieve a mixture of cubic and hexagonal CdS showed a higher photocatalytic activity compared to bare cubic or hexagonal CdS. Similarly, Wang et al.747 attained an improved conductivity for CdS after calcination of 400 °C for several hours. The enhanced conductivity and close interface between Cd and CdS resulted in an improved photocatalytic activity. After calcination the defects on the CdS system also increased, which also influenced photocatalytic performance.748 Xing et al.749 prepared Cd1−xZnxS (x = 0–0.92) photocatalysts via a co-precipitation method followed by calcination at 723 K in N2 atmosphere. The photocatalyst band gaps were ∼2.20–3.12 eV (x = 0–0.92), which was almost linear with composition of x. Notably, the conduction band position of the solid solution was shifted to a more negative potential compared to pristine CdS. Owing to this band gap reduction, the Cd0.62Zn0.16S photocatalyst showed the highest AQY of ∼2.17% (under UV light) and ∼0.60% (under visible light). It was thought that the size of the crystallite, the (1 0 1) plane and energy gap of Cd1−xZnxS photocatalysts had a strong impact on the H2 production efficiency. Chen et al.750 reported that, MoSx/CdS (PT) photocatalyst performance increases progressively with an increase of calcination temperature and a clear surface morphology change was detected at ∼773 K. Recently, Tian et al.751 examined the synthesis of ZnIn2S4via a hydrothermal method. As the temperature was steadily increased, there was an improvement in total pore volume, pore diameter, and surface area which regulated the photocatalytic activity. As result, a H2 production rate of ∼248.9 mmol g−1 h−1 was achieved for ZnIn2S4 prepared at 160 °C.
7.11 Perspectives on photocatalytic hydrogen generation
Several metal sulfides, such as MoS2 and WS2, have exhibited excellent activities as a co-catalyst for the modification of several photocatalysts, which reduces the activation energy, Tafel slope and overpotential for photocatalytic H2 evolution process. The functional properties of these metal sulfides depend on their crystalline nature, their amount, and layer arrangement. In addition, their appropriate band gap, eco-friendly nature and low-cost have made several metal sulfide nanomaterials potential candidates for environmentally sustainable photo-responsive materials, in particular for photocatalytic hydrogen generation applications. These materials are summarized in Table 5. However, most mono-metal sulfides and bi-metal sulfides have poor performance for photocatalytic H2 generation, since many of the metal sulfide materials suffer from photodegradation, which limits their long-term durability. In addition, to enhance the performance without structural or sulfur deficiencies, it is essential to use sacrificial agents to improve performance. However, creating heterojunctions or doping with anions or cations will expose more active sites, prevent agglomeration and amalgamation, and achieve electronic structure modification of the nanoparticles. As a result, charge carrier separation and electron transport can be enhanced, leading to the improved photocatalytic activity. In addition, heterojunctions can reduce the required activation energy and overpotential for photocatalytic H2 generation. Based on the above results, metal sulfide photocatalysts have shown to be future candidates to replace Earth's rare and high cost precious metal catalysts such as Pt and Ru for the production of innovative photocatalysts with improved photoactivity and long-term robustness.
