8. Photoelectrochemical (PEC) water splitting by utilizing metal sulfides
8.1 PEC photoanodes
Photoanode materials are responsible for oxygen evolution reactions (OER) and they must be a semiconducting material, have an energy gap that can harvest wide range of solar spectrum, and act as an efficient charge collection with high charge carrier mobility. Moreover, the constituents need to be stable in an aqueous environment, be low-cost, and eco-friendly.424,426,752 This section will focus on semiconductors of metal sulfides that have been studied as a photoanode in PEC solar water splitting. It is known that the PEC performance of a photoanode material is affected by its structure and morphology, including crystal structure, particle size, electronic nature, surface morphology, and defects in both surface and bulk substances.1,425,753–755 Therefore, enhancement in performance can be achieved by optimising these features. For instance, semiconductors such as several metal sulfides, e.g. CdS,756–759 Bi2S3,760,761 PbS,762–764 SnS,765–772 Sb2S,773 Ag3CuS2774 and CuInS2,775–777 which absorb visible light, can act as sensitizers that are capable of transferring electrons to the wide band gap semiconductors such as ZnO, SnO2 or TiO2, and thereby increase photoelectrochemical water splitting performance.

8.1.1 CdS based photoanodes. 
Due to the quantum confinement effect, nanosized CdS was chosen as a prototype system for several PEC systems.564,778 For instance, Sun et al.779 attained a PEC efficiency of ∼4.15% for a CdS–QDs sensitized TiO2 nanotube array photoelectrode. In addition, Lee et al.419 proposed that the alignment of Fermi-levels between CdS and CdSe could create optimum band-edge levels in the TiO2/CdS/CdSe electrode, which is beneficial for solar water splitting performance. As a result, from Fig. 49(i), the TiO2/CdS/CdSe photoanode revealed a saturated photocurrent of ∼14.9 mA cm−2, which was ∼3 times higher than the bare TiO2/CdS and TiO2/CdSe electrode. Similarly, Chi et al.780 observed the effect of decreased charge combination on CdS–QDs into a TiO2 mesoporous film, which exhibited a high efficiency of ∼3.7% under visible light.
 Fig. 49  (i) HR-TEM images showing a mesoporous structure of the TiO2/CdS/CdSe electrode (a) and the arrangement of CdS and CdSe around a TiO2 crystallite (b), current density versus potential for various photoelectrodes measured in under illumination of AM 1.5 light (with UV cutoff) at 100 mW cm−2 (c) and (d) efficiency of prepared samples. Reproduced from ref. 419 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2010. (ii) TEM (a) and HR-TEM (b) images of CdS/Zn1−xCdxS/ZnS css nanorods, J–V profile over CdS and CdS/Zn1−xCdxS/ZnS nanorod array photoanodes (c) and J–t of CdS and CdS/Zn1−xCdxS/ZnS nanorod array photoanodes measured at −0.5 V vs. SCE under AM 1.5G light (100 mW cm−2) with five on/off cycles (d). Reproduced from ref. 788 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2016. 
Due to the 1D CdS nanostructure, it is perfectly aligned with wide band gap materials such as TiO2,756,781 ZnO,782–784 CuGaSe2,785 and ZnFe2O4;783 these have been combined into CdS nanostructures to build heterojunctions for well-organized solar energy conversion systems. Notably, Wang et al.786 prepared CdS/CdSe core–shell nanorod arrays through deposition of CdSe on hydrothermally produced CdS nanorods, which resulted in CdS nanorod arrays encapsulated by a uniform CdSe layer. This core–shell feature exhibited a higher optical absorbance and exposed absorption edges on the films. In addition, it was confirmed that the CdS/CdSe was also shaped as an effect of the Fermi level alignment, thus a maximum photocurrent of ∼5.3 mA cm−2 was attained for the CdS/CdSe core–shell photoanode, which was ∼4 and ∼11 times of the bare CdS and CdSe photoanodes, respectively. Recently, Zhang et al.787 fabricated a thin film of core/shell nanorods of CdS/ZnS dense arrays via aerosol-assisted CVD (AACVD) method. The photoanode of CdS/ZnS arrays had higher visible light driven PEC activity with a photocurrent density of ∼7.8 mA cm−2 (0 V vs. SCE), which was ∼4 times higher than the bare CdS. Later, the same group788 attained ∼14.0 mA cm−2 (0 V, vs. SCE) for core/shell/shell nanorod (css-NR) of CdS/Zn1−xCdxS/ZnS photoanode, see Fig. 49(ii), which was ∼8 times higher than a pristine CdS nanorod array. The enhanced PEC performance was mainly attributed to ∼12 nm sized ultrathin Zn1−xCdxS layers on the interface of CdS/ZnS core/shell nanorods, and the formation of a heterojunction. Previous investigations have shown that CdS could be embedded onto planar surface-functionalized semiconducting materials to form large surface area heterostructured photoanodes for efficient photoactive response in a redox electrolyte.789–792 The spectroscopic and PEC experiments indicate that quantum size confinement, and hence the controlled absorption edge of CdS, which revealed efficient photosensitization of the CdS/TiO2 photoelectrode.793
Similarly, a CuInS2 nanocrystal film was formed between the TiO2 photoelectrode and CdS to suppress charge recombination in CuInS2-sensitized TiO2 photoelectrodes.794 A heterostructured Au NPs/CdS/LaBTC MOFs photoanode showed long-term stability.795 However, several researchers indicated that the thin film thickness,419,796 temperature and lattice parameter791,797 of the metal sulfides shell limited the function of the tunneling junction layer. In addition, a few materials also exhibited a low efficiency, which is often the effect of charge recombination between the electrolyte and photoelectrodes.798–800


8.1.2 Ag2S, ZnS and SnS based photoanodes. 
The large absorption coefficient of Ag2S (Eg = 0.9 to 1.05 eV) makes it an important material for PEC water splitting. The PEC activity of a CdS/Ag2S/TiO2/ITO photoelectrode801 indicated that the Ag2S nanocrystals coating could not only improve the optical absorption of CdS/TiO2/ITO photoelectrode, but also efficiently reduce charge recombination between the TiO2 and electrolyte, which could increase the overall efficiency of CdS-sensitized TiO2/ITO photoelectrode. Similarly, Yu et al.802 prepared p–n heterostructures of Cu2ZnSnS4–Ag2S photoanode for PEC analysis. As shown in Fig. 50(i), the photoanode displayed the highest photocurrent density of ∼0.58 mA cm−2 under visible light irradiation, which was ∼10 times greater than that of the bare CZTS (∼0.06 mA cm−2). However, a decrease in photocurrent was observed by further increasing the Ag2S amount.
 Fig. 50  (i) (a) Schematic of the energy band diagram of the heterostructures and the possible model of electron transfer, and (b) schematic of water splitting reaction in the photoelectrochemical cell. Reproduced from ref. 802 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2015. (ii) (a) PEC performance of zinc blende and wurtzite structured ZnS photoelectrodes. Reproduced from ref. 806 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2016. (iii) (a) Schematics of DOS and the potential energy diagrams of pristine ZnS and N-doped ZnS according to the results of valence-band XPS analysis and (b) photocurrent–potential curves of pristine ZnS-coated and N-doped ZnS-coated FTO glass photoelectrodes in 0.5 M Na2SO4 under UV-vis light illumination. Reproduced from ref. 810 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2015. 
As discussed in previous sections (see Sections 4 and 7.4), bulk ZnS is only responsible for operation in the UV region. In order to make it active under visible light to drive PEC water splitting, Kurnia et al.803 used defect engineering to fabricate a ZnS nanostructure, and hence the electronic states were altered to enable substantial optical absorption of light at energies lower than the band gap energy of bulk ZnS. As a result, they attained the band gap of ∼2.4 eV for defected ZnS thin films, whereas the PEC photocurrent density reached ∼1.5 mA cm−2 under visible-light condition (λ ≥ 435 nm). In addition, for a ZnS photocatalysts it has been estimated from first-principles analyses that the wurtzite phase would have a greater reducing ability than the blende phase of ZnS due to the developed energy of the conduction band.804,805 Therefore, the ZnS phase has a significant impact on PEC performance. Later, the same group806 achieved a high photocurrent density for wurtzite ZnS with high incident photon-to-electron conversion efficiency (IPCE) of 4.3%, see Fig. 50(ii). In addition, the adjustment of the pulsed laser deposition synthesis conditions led to defect states in the ZnS thin films, which resulted in enhanced visible-light absorption, facile charge separation, and a photocurrent of ∼1.6 mA cm−2 was obtained.803
ZnS modification has been found to play an important role in determining the photocatalytic efficiency. The surface defects could act as a charge carrier trap centers as well as adsorption sites where the photogenerated charge carriers can be transported to the adsorbed species, therefore inhibiting the recombination of electron–hole pairs and effective charge separation. Consequently, numerous studies have explored the light absorption performance of a ZnS photoanode. For example, doping ZnS with metal ions such as Mn, Cd, Ni, and Cu has improved the performance under visible light irradiation.807–809 Recently, Huang et al.807 used a chemical bath deposition (CBD) method to prepare Ni-doped ZnS thin films. After annealing at 500 °C, the Ni-doped ZnS thin films exhibited a photocurrent density of ∼0.65 mA cm−2 at 0.25 V vs. Ag/AgCl in 0.5 M K2SO4 electrolyte solution, which was higher than the undoped ZnS films (∼0.25 mA cm−2). As shown in Fig. 50(iii), under 0.5 M Na2SO4 electrolyte solution, the N-doped ZnS showed an excellent stability with a high photocurrent density of ∼1.6 mA cm−2 at 1.0 V vs. Ag/AgCl, while the bare ZnS reached ∼1.0 mA cm−2 at the same potential.810 Owing to the ZnO/ZnS/Au heterostructure nature, the photoelectrode reached a maximum IPCE efficiency of 0.21%, which was ∼3.5 times higher than pristine ZnO.811 Notably, the modification of the Au NPs on ZnO/ZnS encouraged effective surface charge passivation and surface plasmon resonance effects, and an increased photoelectrochemical performance was attained in both UV and visible light conditions. In addition, the interlayer of ZnS is preferred for the transport of photogenerated carriers under UV light and hot electrons under visible light, initiating their injection into ZnO; this instantaneously inhibited the electron–hole recombination at the interface of the photoanode–electrolyte to improve PEC performance.
The band gap of SnS is appropriate to use the complete solar spectrum. However, compared to other metal sulfides, the SnS efficiency is restricted by charge carrier recombination at the grain boundaries and sulfur vacancies.812 To improve the photoanode efficiency, SnS doped with Sb,813 Ag,814,815 Cu816 and In817 for solar water splitting applications have been investigated. Both In- and Sb-doped SnS have been prepared via electrochemical deposition by Seal et al.818 Due to the reduced band gap, the Sb doped SnS revealed better PEC performance than In-doped SnS.


8.1.3 WS2, MoS2 and Bi2S3 based photoanodes. 
Both the valence band and conduction band positions of WS2 are higher than MoS2.819 Thus, the hybridization of WS2 and TiO2 can lead to the development of a type-II heterostructure, so that electron–hole separation activity is expected to be superior to MoS2–TiO2. Based on this structural influence, nanosheets of WS2 can provide a synergetic coupling effect with TiO2 and the WS2–TiO2 heterostructures exhibited higher optical response than bare TiO2. Zhang et al.820 prepared a Au/MoS2/ZnO NRs/FTO photoelectrode, which consisted of vertically aligned few layer MoS2 with ZnO nanorod arrays, where the Au was deposited for a surface plasmon resonance (SPR) effect. In 1.0 M Na2SO4, the photoanode exhibited improved photo activity compared to bare samples under UV light and red light illumination. These results provide insights in understanding the electronic and optoelectronic properties of vertically aligned MoS2 heterostructures. Liu et al.821 observed that a MoS2/CdS film with a ratio of 0.14% displayed the maximum photocurrent density of ∼28 mA cm−2 and the increased IPCE of ∼28% at 420 nm at 0 V vs. Ag/AgCl. The enhanced performance of the MoS2/CdS heterojunction electrode was ascribed to the visible light absorption enhanced by MoS2 edge sites and the creation of a p–n junction among CdS and MoS2, which facilitates the effective photogenerated carrier separation via the internal electrostatic field in the heterojunction region. Jiang et al.822 investigated the impact of MoS2 and CdS in the formation of heterostructure of In2S3/CdS/MoS2 and In2S3/MoS2/CdS. From Fig. 51(i), their results revealed that transient photocurrent responses of both the photocatalysts of In2S3/CdS/MoS2 and In2S3/MoS2/CdS were quite different, which suggests that the structural arrangements and deposition sites have a significant effect on charge migration and photocatalytic activity. Recently, Liu et al.823 prepared pure CdTe QDs, Bi2S3 NRs, and CdTe–Bi2S3 heterojunctions by a hydrothermal method. During visible light driven PEC water splitting, the photocurrent of bare CdTe QDs and Bi2S3 NRs were ∼70 nA and 450 nA, respectively. After the combination of CdTe QDs with Bi2S3 NRs, the CdTe–Bi2S3 heterojunction displayed photocurrent of ∼2 and ∼13 times higher than the Bi2S3 NRs and CdTe QDs. Their experimental results suggested that the CdTe–Bi2S3 heterojunction could increase the photocurrent due to the reduction of the photoexcited electron–hole pairs in the CdTe–Bi2S3 composite.
 Fig. 51  (i) (a) Transient photocurrent responses of the pure CdS nanorods, and MoS2/CdS, In2S3/CdS, In2S3/CdS/MoS2 and In2S3/MoS2/CdS heterostructures, and (b) photocurrent–potential curves of the pure CdS nanorods, MoS2/CdS, In2S3/CdS, In2S3/CdS/MoS2 and In2S3/MoS2/CdS heterostructures. Reproduced from ref. 822 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2015. (ii) (a) The schematic illustration of the photocurrent device setup consisting of the FeS2–TiO2 coating on the FTO substrate, a Ag/AgCl reference, and a Pt wire counter electrode in the alkaline electrolyte (pH = 13.5) with SO32−/S2O32− as a sacrificial agent and (b) the current–time (i–t) characteristics of the FeS2–TiO2 heterostructure and pure TiO2 on FTO under the on/off cycles of the NIR laser (808 nm) illumination at a constant bias of 0.1 V. Reproduced from ref. 831 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2018. 
Similarly, a WO3/Bi2S3 nanorods composite824 prepared with a seed layer revealed a superior photocurrent density of ∼5.95 mA cm−2 at 0.9 V vs. RHE, which was ∼35 and ∼1.4 times greater than bare WO3 and WO3/Bi2S3 heterostructure without a seed layer, respectively. Moreover, a higher IPCE of ∼68.8% and photoconversion efficiency ∼1.70% were attained. The improvement was due to the better light absorption, effective charge transport, and high carrier density. Similarly, a Bi2S3 nanobelt/WO3 nanoplate array exhibited an excellent photocurrent density of ∼8.91 mA cm−2 at −0.1 V vs. Ag/AgCl in the 0.1 M Na2SO3 and 0.1 M Na2S.825 The outstanding PEC activity of the photoanode was credited to the well-organized charge transfer and improved surface areas in the photoelectrode. In addition, due to the lower interface defects, the Bi2S3/WO3 photoelectrode exhibited a photocurrent density of ∼8.0 mA cm−2 at −0.1 V vs. Ag/AgCl under visible light irradiation.826 Taking advantage of the large surface area and longer direct electron path of the Bi2S3 NS/TiO2 NR structure, Wan et al.827 attained the maximum efficiency of ∼2.23%, which was ∼9 times higher than bare TiO2 (0.25%).


8.1.4 Other mono-metal based photoanodes. 
FeS2 (EBG = 0.95 eV) has been used in a photoelectrochemical cell to demonstrate 2.8% solar to electrical energy efficiency.828 The potential of pyrite (FeS2) thin films as a photoanode for PEC water splitting promoted by visible light absorption has been widely investigated. Barawi et al.829 fabricated n-type FeS2 on titanium substrates and during testing the flat band potential was −0.75 ± 0.05 V vs. Ag/AgCl, which resulted the high efficiency of ∼8% for H2 generation. Kmentova et al.830 stated that, after the sulfidation of iron oxide, the pyrite iron disulfide had a lower photocurrent density of ∼4 μA cm−2 at 600 mV vs. Ag/AgCl compared to the metal oxide of iron. This was due to the fast photogenerated charge recombination nature of Fe2S. Due to the heterostructure formed, Kuo et al.831 attained a maximum photocatalytic H2 production rate of ∼331 μmol h−1 g−1 for a FeS2–TiO2 photoanode, as shown in Fig. 51(ii), which was higher than FeS2 nanocrystals (NCs) and TiO2 nanoparticles, since the FeS2 NCs in the FeS2–TiO2 heterostructures was acting as a photosensitizer.
Ehsan et al.832 reported the fabrication of β-In2S3 on FTO substrates by the AACVD method at temperatures of 300, 350 and 400 °C using a precursor of [In(S2CNRR′)3]·n(py) complexes. The β-In2S3 films made at 400 and 350 °C from the precursors exhibited high photocurrent densities of ∼1.25 and 0.65 mA cm−2 at 0.23 V vs. Ag/AgCl respectively. The enhanced performance was attributed to the surface morphologies of uniform structures and band gap shrinkage, which reduced photogenerated electron–hole recombination. Li et al.833 prepared a nanosheet array (NSA) based photoanode of an In2S3/ZnO heterojunction through ALD of ZnO over In2S3 NSAs, where the photoanode showed an increased light absorbance over a long wavelength range of 250–850 nm. The photoanode showed improved PEC performance with a photocurrent density of ∼1.642 mA cm−2 (at 1.5 V vs. RHE) and an IPCE of ∼27.64% at 380 nm (at 1.23 V vs. RHE), which was 70 and 116 times higher than bare ZnO and In2S3, respectively. The enhanced performance was attributed to type-II band alignment of the In2S3/ZnO heterojunction, which facilitated effective separation and collection of photogenerated charge carriers, specifically with the aid of positive bias potential, and increased PEC activity. Likewise, Li et al.834 reported that the PEC activity of ZnO/ZnS core/shell nanoarray was lower than 0.5 mA cm−2. After the addition of a CuS shell instead of ZnS, the ZnO/CuS showed an improved photocurrent density. Moreover, the combination of both sulfides formed a ZnO/CuInS2 core/shell nanoarray which exhibited the highest photocurrent density of ∼16.9 mA cm−2 at 0.29 V vs. SCE. Due to the broader light absorption and the enhanced electrical conductivity provided by the WO3/rGO/Sb2S3 heterostructure,835 the photoanode of WO3/rGO/Sb2S3 exhibited a high photocurrent density of ∼1.20 mA cm−2 at 1.23 V vs. RHE under AM 1.5G illumination, which was higher than WO3/Sb2S3 (0.27 mA cm−2) and WO3 (0.05 mA cm−2) photoelectrodes.


8.1.5 Bi-metal sulfides based photoanodes. 
Architectural control of a nanomaterial can have profound effects on PEC efficiency. Wang et al.836 prepared 1D/2D architectures of CdS nanowire (NW)/CdIn2S4 nanosheet (NS) and due to the type II band alignment and multidimensional nature of the heterostructure, as shown in Fig. 52(i), a high photocurrent density of 2.85 mA cm−2 at 0 V vs. RHE was attained without any co-catalyst. Cheng et al.837 prepared AgInS2via a sulfurization technique, in which the compounds were coated on an FTO substrate, during fabrication the molar ratio of Ag and In was varied. As a result, the highest photocurrent density ∼2.7 mA cm−2 at +0.5 V vs. Ag/AgCl electrode was obtained for AgInS2 photoelectrode, whereas the molar ratio of Ag/In was 0.92/0.90. It was stated that, for the Ag-rich condition, the Ag atoms may inhabit the indium sites, which results in p-type conductivity. An orthorhombic photoelectrode of Ag8SnS6 achieved a maximum PEC performance ∼2.5 mA cm−2 in sea water at 0.7 V vs. RHE,838 see Fig. 52(ii).
 Fig. 52  (i) Solvent-mediated, surface reaction-driven growth route for synthesis of CdS nanowire (NW)/CdIn2S4 nanosheet (NS) 1D/2D architectures, (b) TEM image and (c) PEC water splitting performance of CdS nanowire (NW)/CdIn2S4 nanosheet. Reproduced from ref. 836 with permission from Springer, copyright 2017. (II) (a) Current density–applied voltage plots in the range of 0–0.9 V vs. RHE for samples Ag8SnS6 as (A), (C), and (E), and (b) IPCE curves of samples (A) and (C) under monochromatic light irradiation with applied voltage of 0 V vs. Ag/AgCl reference electrode in Na2S (0.35 M) + K2SO3 (0.25 M) solution. Reproduced from ref. 838 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2016. 
A Cu2SnS3 coated ZnO/FTO array was formed using electrodeposition839 which had a maximum photocurrent density of 0.46 mA cm−2 at 0.5 V vs. SCE in 0.1 M Na2SO4 electrolyte under light irradiation, which was ∼2.86 and 23 times higher than the pure Cu2SnS3 and ZnO, respectively. Liu et al.840 prepared ZnxBi2S3+x/WO3 photoelectrodes on a FTO substrate, which showed that QDs of ZnxBi2S3+x uniformly covered the entire surface of the WO3 plates, thereby forming a ZnxBi2S3+x/WO3 core/shell structure. Due to the structural and core–shell features, the ZnxBi2S3+x/WO3 photoelectrode displayed an excellent PEC activity, see Fig. 53(i), in terms of ∼7.0 mA cm−2 at −0.1 V vs. Ag/AgCl under visible light condition and good photo-stability. Similarly, due to the sensitization nature of 3–5 nm sized CuInS2 QDs on a TiO2 photoanode, Li et al.841 attained a maximum photocurrent density of ∼1.92 mA cm−2 at 0.23 V vs. RHE, whereas the energy conversion efficiency of ∼1.9% was under simulated solar illumination. Later, the same group842 attained a maximum photocurrent density of 16 mA cm−2 and energy conversion efficiencies of ∼13% at 0.34 V vs. RHE for 3.5–4.5 nm sized CuInS2 QDs on TiO2 photoanode. This enhanced performance was attributed to the heterojunction formation and sensitization of QDs on TiO2.
 Fig. 53  (i) (a) Photocurrent density and (b) photocurrent–time plot of the photoelectrodes bismuth sulfide and tungsten oxide heterostructures. Reproduced from ref. 840 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2015. (ii) (a) The PEC performance of the hydrothermally synthesized bare 1D R-TNR's, 2D ZIS NS's/1D R-TNR's photoelectrodes (SC, MC, OC and LC): LSPV (a – backside illumination BSI; b- front side illumination FSI) and TPR (c – BSI; d – FSI) at an applied potential of −0.5 V vs. Ag/AgCl under solar simulator (100 mW cm−2). Reproduced from ref. 844 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018. 
Moreover, Li et al.843 fabricated a ZnIn2S4 thin film on ITO via a spray pyrolysis method. Due to the reduced band gap, the photoanode revealed a photocurrent density of ∼490 μA cm−2 at 0.8 V vs. SCE in 0.1 M Na2SO3 electrolyte, with excellent stability. A hydrothermally prepared heterostructure photoelectrode of 2D ZnIn2S4 nanosheets/1D rutile TiO2 nanorods arrays (2D ZIS NS's/1D R-TNR's) allowed it to be used in a PEC water splitting reaction.844 This heterostructure offered lower resistance, a direct electron transport period and a reduced amount of recombination under back side illumination (BSI) and improved electron hole separation and transport in the 2D ZIS NS's/1D R-TNR's heterostructure. However, the photoanode demonstrated a poor PEC performance via front side illumination (FSI), since the surface of the 2D ZIS NS's block light at the surface. As a result, as shown in Fig. 53(ii), the improved ZIS/1D TNR showed a photocurrent density of ∼1.6 mA cm−2 for back side illumination over front side illumination (∼0.793 mA cm−2). Mahadik et al.266 prepared a ternary CdS/ZnIn2S4/TiO2 hetero-architecture, whereas the photoanode was consisted of TiO2 nanorods, nanograins of CdS, and ZnIn2S4 nanosheets. Owing to this structural feature, the photoanode showed excellent optical absorption, large surface area and high photogenerated charge carrier transport. As a result, the CdS/ZnIn2S4/TiO2 photoanode had an enhanced photocurrent density of ∼1.4 mA cm−2 at AM1.5, which was ∼3.7 and 2.3 times higher than that of pristine ZnIn2S4/TiO2 and CdS/TiO2 respectively.
Recently, Yuan et al.845 prepared ZnIn2S4/graphene composites using a low temperature wet chemical method. During the fabrication, the amount of graphene was set at 1 wt%, 3 wt%, 5 wt%, and 10 wt%, which revealed a modification on the electronic interactions between ZnIn2S4 and graphene. Due to the large surface area, separation of charge efficiency and prolonged life-time of photogenerated charge carriers, the composite of ZnIn2S4/graphene (∼3 wt%) showed an excellent visible light driven PEC water splitting photocurrent density of ∼4 mA at 0.63 V vs. Ag/AgCl in 0.2 M Na2SO4 (pH 6.8) aqueous solution.


8.1.6 Effect of processing temperature. 
Pawar et al.846 synthesized MoBi2S5 nanoflowers via a facile microwave method. After thermal treatment, the morphology was optimized and the photocurrent density was increased by ∼80% after annealing at 473 K, compared to the un-annealed sample. This was attributed to the band gap shrinkage from 2.0 to 1.75 eV and the increased crystallinity of MoBi2S5 nanoflowers after thermal treatment. Similarly, DeAngelis et al.847 synthesized a Sb2S3 photoanode by thermal evaporation onto FTO substrates. The samples were subjected to temperature treatments from 250 °C to 400 °C. As a result, the 300 °C treated sample exhibited an improved PEC photocurrent density of ∼600 μA cm−2 at 1.23 V vs. RHE in 0.1 M Na2SO4 under 1 sun illumination, although photocorrosion was detected in this investigation. Similarly, the high optical absorption coefficient (α = 104 cm−1), a band gap shrinkage of ∼1.35 eV and the crystalline nature due to the temperature of sulfidation increased the PEC performance of a VS4 photoanode.848 Later, a temperature of 400 °C for annealed PbS QDNCs embedded on TiO2 nanotubes as a photoanode revealed the maximum photocurrent density of ∼3 mA cm−2 under visible light irradiation.849 It was revealed that decorating PbS with TiO2 nanotubes enhanced the photoelectrochemical and photocatalytic activity. In addition, the decreased charge transfer resistance and the high optical absorption features made a 24-fold increase in the photocurrent density compared to pristine TiO2 nanotubes, see Fig. 54.
 Fig. 54  (a) Photoelectrochemical (PEC) water splitting mechanism of PbS/TNT and PEC responses of PbS deposited TNTs shown are (b) J–t characteristics at multiple on–off cycles and (c) J–V characteristics [PEC conditions: CE = Pt; RE = Ag/AgCl; electrolyte = 0.1 N Na2S]. Reproduced from ref. 849 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2015. 

8.2 PEC photocathodes
For efficient solar water splitting, semiconducting constituents with high visible-light harvesting features and electrons and/or holes with enhanced mobility for charge separation are essential.850–852 Water splitting photocathodes are generally p-type semiconductors and must generate the required cathodic current to reduce water to hydrogen and must have high stability in aqueous environments. Optimal photocathode materials need to have the conduction band edge potential to be more negative than the hydrogen (H2) redox potential.853 Many earlier studies on electrochemical photocathodes focused on p-type silicon and III–V semiconductors.854–857 Recently, p-type semiconductors, including metal sulfides, have received significant attention. In this section, we review the variety of photocathodes materials studied to date, which include mono-metallic, bi-metallic sulfides, their heterostructures and the effects of temperature, defects and large surface area.

8.2.1 MoS2 based photocathodes. 
The manufacture of MoS2 with monolayer or flake-like morphologies that can improve catalytically active edge sites is of great importance. In order to enhance PEC performance, several research groups858–860 have integrated MoS2 catalysts on p-type silicon (p-Si; band gap ∼1.1 eV) by a variety of methods for photocathode applications, and at the same time they also observed an enhanced photochemical activity. For example, using simple drop-casting method, Ding et al.861 prepared 1T-MoS2/Si photocathodes and used it for PEC testing under an acid environment. A current density of ∼9.2 mA cm−2 at 0 V vs. RHE was obtained for 1T-MoS2/Si photocathodes, which was ∼4 times higher than the 2H-MoS2/Si photocathode (∼2.3 mA cm−2). This enhanced performance was accredited to the reduced charge-transfer resistances across the interfaces of the semiconductor/catalyst and catalyst/electrolyte. In addition, a 3D MoS2 on a TiO2-coated p-type Si photocathode was prepared by Andoshe et al.862 The 3D MoS2/TiO2/p-Si photocathode exhibited a low onset potential of ∼0.35 V with a current density of ∼37 mA cm−2. As shown in Fig. 55(i), the passivation by MoS2 layers on TiO2/p-Si photocathode shifted the onset potential anodically by ∼0.76 V, as compared to the pristine TiO2/p-Si photocathode's onset potential. In addition, the passivation of the TiO2/p-Si with MoS2 layers provided long-term stable operation for ∼181 h without degradation. The obtained high performance in the 3D MoS2/TiO2/p-Si photocathode was credited to the larger number of exposed edge sites and grain boundaries. Later, Fan et al.863 prepared a MoS2 catalyst with ∼2 nm Al2O3 sheltered n+p-Si, where the Al2O3 acted as a passive and protective layer of the silicon surface; this layer prevented direct contact between the n+p-Si surface and the adjoining electrolyte. As a result, a low onset potential of ∼0.4 V vs. RHE and ∼35.6 mA cm−2 photocurrent density under Xe lamp (100 mA cm−2) illumination with a long-term durability of ∼120 h during continuous water splitting was attained. In addition, ultrathin MoS2 nanoflakes on Ag-core Si-shell nanospheres (Ag@Si NSs) were fabricated via CVD.864 Owing to the large surface area and high curvature of the Ag@Si NSs, the as-grown MoS2 nanoflakes had more active sites. Hence, a photocurrent density of ∼33.3 mA cm−2 at −0.4 V vs. RHE was obtained. Similarly, Tan et al.865 prepared photocathodes of amorphous MoSx (a-MoSx) on a TiO2/Sb2Se3 through over electro-deposition. They showed that the dissimilar chemical states of a-MoSx enriched photoelectrochemically active sites during fabrication, see Fig. 55(ii). Chen et al.866 tested the wide pH range operational range of Si/a-CoMoSx, that benefitted from the bi-metallic nature; this photoanode achieved a photocurrent density of ∼17.5 mA cm−2 at 0 V vs. RHE.
 Fig. 55  (i) The PEC performance of the working electrodes, MoS2/TiO2/p-Si, TiO2/p-Si, and bare Si photocathode, (a) LSV of MoS2/TiO2/p-Si and TiO2/p-Si. (b) onset potential of layered and 3D MoS2/TiO2/p-Si at −1 mA cm−2 and (c) hydrogen adsorption free energy of MoS2 basal plane, Mo edge calculated using DFT. Reproduced from ref. 862 with permission from Wiley-VCH, copyright 2018. (ii) PEC performance of amorphous (Al) and crystalline (Cl) MoS2/TiO2/Sb2Se3 and (a) X-ray photoelectron spectra of Mo 3d and S 2s regions, before and after activation of (b) Cl-MoSx and (c) Al-MoSx. The three doublets of Mo 3d are Mo(VI), Mo(V), and Mo(IV) states, represented by blue-, green-, and red-colored peaks, respectively. Each of the Mo 3d5/2 peak positions are denoted by dotted vertical lines, showing that only the Mo(V) and Mo(IV) 3d5/2 peaks of Cl-MoSx were shifted to a lower level after activation (arrows). The three peaks of S 2s are S0, S22−, and S2− states, represented by purple-, dark blue-, and orange-colored peaks, respectively. Reproduced from ref. 865 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2015. 


8.2.2 CdS based photocathodes. 
Due to the direct band gap of ∼2.4 eV, CdS is one of the most extensively studied metal sulfide material for solar water splitting applications, where its band edge positions are suitable for water splitting to produce oxygen and hydrogen.419,867 Despite these benefits, the performance of CdS is limited due to the photo-generated electrons and holes, which cannot be strongly separated and transferred.868,869 In addition, CdS undergoes photocorrosion in aqueous media, which limits its long-term durability.870,871 To overcome these challenges, efforts have been made to increase the stability and efficiency of CdS photoelectrodes, which includes the use of 1D and 2D CdS and/or heterostructure formation of nanostructures since they offer substantial gains such as increased carrier collection efficiency and increased incident light absorption.872,873 However, a recent study showed that the low hole transfer rate was the efficiency-limiting factor in a CdS-based heterostructure.874 For instance, as shown in Fig. 56(i) a relatively high photocurrent of ∼12.08 mA cm−2 at 0 V vs. RHE was attained for PEC cell using Pt/TiO2/CdS/CBTSSe (x ≈ 3) photocathode.875 To avoid Cu2S oxidation and phase changes during PEC testing, deposition of CdS has been used.876 As a result a Cu2S/CdS/TiO2/RuOx photocathode exhibited a photocurrent density of ∼7.0 mA cm−2 at −0.3 V vs. RHE and a low overpotential of ∼−0.48 V vs. RHE. Septina et al.877 reported that a buffer layer of (70 to 80 nm thick) CdS between the CuO and TiO2 increases the PEC photocurrent with the least dark current over a wide-range of applied potentials. The CuO/CdS/TiO2/Pt photocathode reached a maximum photocurrent density of ∼1.68 mA cm−2 at 0 V vs. RHE, as shown in Fig. 56(ii). Similarly, Yokoyama et al.878 stated that CdS modification of a Cu(In,Ga)Se2 photocathode improved the photocurrent and onset potential, and the CdS deposited on Cu(In,Ga)Se2 created a p–n junction which was beneficial for enhancing charge separation for improved photocurrent and onset potential. By developing a p–n junction between CdS, CuGa3Se5 and modified (Ag,Cu) GaSe2 (ACGSe), Zhang et al.879 achieved a high cathodic photocurrent density of ∼8.79 mA cm−2 at 0 V vs. RHE, and an onset potential of ∼0.62 VRHE over ∼20 days of uninterrupted hydrogen evolution. A CdS-coated p-CuGeSe2 exhibited a photocurrent density of over ∼10 mA cm−2vs. RHE and the efficiency was 0.83% at 0.2 V vs. RHE. Furthermore, this photoelectrode provided ∼10 days of continuous water splitting activity without any degradation.785 Yang et al.880 attained a high photocurrent density of ∼13 mA cm−2 at −0.2 V vs. RHE, where the photocathode was consisted of Pt/ALD-TiO2/CdS/CZTS and the improved photocathode achieved a relatively stable H2 production.
 Fig. 56  (i) (a) Estimated band structure of the Pt/TiO2/CdS/CBTSSe (x ≈ 3) photocathode and Pt anode, (b) LSV of Pt/TiO2/CdS/CBTSSe (x ≈ 3), annealed Pt/CdS/CBTSSe (x ≈ 3), and unannealed Pt/CdS/CBTSSe (x ≈ 3) structures in 0.5 M KH2PO4/0.5 M Na2SO4, with a scan rate of 10 mV s−1 from anodic to cathodic potential. Dashed line: LSV in light; solid line: LSV with light chopped on and off every 50 mV. Reproduced from ref. 875 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018. (ii) (a) Structure of the photocathode with the role of each of the layers, (b) current density–potential curves of CuO/TiO2 (with Pt) and CuO/CdS/TiO2 with and without Pt-catalyst measured in 1 M phosphate buffer (pH 7) under chopped illumination from simulated 1 sun illumination, and (c) cross-sectional SEM images of the CuO/CdS/TiO2 photocathode. Reproduced from ref. 877 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2017. 


8.2.3 ZnS based photocathodes. 
Due to its production of transportable photo excited electron–hole pairs and high conduction band potential with fast electron transfer,881,882 ZnS is a promising candidate for solar water splitting. Additionally, it has a high number of active sites for solar water splitting without the need for a classical charge transfer co-catalyst, such as Pt or RuO2. However, due to its wide band gap (∼3.6 eV), the photocatalytic activity is limited, and pure ZnS is only active under UV irradiation.883,884 Hence, several reports have attempted to improve the light absorption of a ZnS photocathode.776,873,885,886 Tuning of the manufacturing conditions can lead to defect states in ZnS thin films, which can also increase visible-light absorption, separation of charge, and photocurrent.885 Therefore, many studies have focused on the surface morphology and optical properties of ZnS based heterojunctions. Notably, sulfur-containing CIGS (Cu(In,Ga)(Se,S)2) with a ZnS surface layer has improved the photocurrent and stability of the CIGS photocathode. As a result, Chae et al.887 attained a high photocurrent density of ∼24 mA cm−2 at −0.3 V vs. RHE for CIGS/ZnS/Pt photocathode. Goto et al.888 fabricated a photocathode of (ZnSe)0.85(CuIn0.7Ga0.3Se2)0.15 and it was modified with CdS/ZnS overlayers and a Pt catalyst to produce high photocurrent densities of ∼4.3 and ∼2.7 mA cm−2 at 0 and 0.3 V vs. RHE, respectively, under visible light irradiation. Modification using thin CdS and ZnS layers raises the cathodic photocurrent with the formation of a p–n junction.


8.2.4 Other metal sulfide photocathodes. 
The valence band of SnS comprises of hybrid orbitals of Sn-5s and S-3p, signifies that SnS can work as a p-type photocathode semiconductor. SnS is a naturally abundant material with a bandgap of ∼1.2–1.5 eV,889–891 which is favorable for efficient use of the solar spectrum. The SnS efficiency is thought to be limited by charge carrier recombination at the grain boundaries and sulfur vacancies.818 Based on DFT approaches, Shiga et al.24 predicted that SnS can be useful as a photocathode for solar H2 production in a sacrificial agent and environment. In addition, Xu et al.892 prepared ReS2 on conductive carbon cloth by a hydrothermal method. It was observed that ReS2@CFC exhibited a high cathodic photocurrent of 10 mA cm−2 at a low onset potential of 8 mV vs. RHE; see Fig. 57. This enhanced performance was due to active edge sites, optimized electronic band structure and lower Gibbs free energy (ΔGH) of hydrogen adsorption of ReS2/CFC.
 Fig. 57  (a) Schematic energy level diagram and carrier dynamics illuminating photo-induced hot electron injection and Fermi level ascent to boost HER, (b) LSV polarization curves and (c) their corresponding Tafel plots of CFC and ReS2@CFC in dark and light fields, with the iR-corrected data. Reproduced from ref. 892 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018. 
Tuning the chemical composition of Cd with ZnS is also of interest for ZnS based systems, which delivers a prospect for improving PEC performance. For instance, the energy gap and solar water splitting efficiency of CdxZn1−xS (x = 0.3) reached ∼1.93 eV and −3.00 mA cm−2, respectively.893 Moreover, chalcopyrite structures have recently revealed remarkable photocathode performance for PEC water splitting due to their good photo-response.894 Copper indium disulfide (CuInS2) is one of the potential candidates to be used in thin film photocathode technology due to its high absorption coefficient (∼105 cm−1) and direct bandgap (∼1.5 eV) in the wide range of the visible spectrum.895,896 In addition, it is eco-friendly and a variety of research groups have fabricated CuInS2 photocathodes.897,898 CuInS2 films modified with a thin layer of CdS and TiO2 were formed by Zhao et al.897 where the use of a thin layer improved the photocurrent and onset potential. In another report,891 after coating of Pt catalyst, CdS, aluminum-doped zinc oxide (AZO), and TiO2 over layers on CuInS2 the as-prepared hybrid photocathode revealed a photocurrent density of ∼3.5 mA cm−2.
Copper gallium disulfide (CuGaS2) has been used as a photocathode material to a lower extent than CuInS2, since it has a direct wide band gap of ∼2.4 eV.899 The conduction band of CuGaS2 is located at a more negative potential than semiconducting CuInS2, which makes it as a promising photocathode for PEC water splitting.900,901 Recent investigations proposed that doping of a CuGaS2 film with a co-catalyst such as Ti and Zn can alter the crystalline structure and surface morphology of a CuGaS2 photocathode.902 In addition, Mathews et al.903 prepared a Cu2SnS3 photoelectrode on an FTO substrate using a sulfurization method. Owing to the high optical absorption of the cathode, a high photocurrent density of ∼0.3 mA cm−2 at −1.0 V vs. SCE was achieved in the 0.1 M NaCl aqueous electrolyte. Wang et al.904 attained a maximum current density of ∼6 mA cm−2 for Pt/CdS/Cu2ZnSnS4/Mo-mesh at a voltage of −0.35 V vs. RHE in a 0.1 M Na2SO4 solution (pH = 9.5). This enhanced performance was attributed to the symmetry of the metal sulfides. Similarly, Kato et al.905 fabricated (CuGa)0.8Zn0.4S2 and Ru (1.0 wt%) loaded (CuGa)0.8Zn0.4S2 electrodes and the effect of calcination on PEC water splitting was evaluated. In the presence of sacrificial reagents, a Ru (1.0 wt%)-loaded (CuGa)0.8Zn0.4S2 sample exhibited a higher photocurrent than the bare materials. The high performance metal sulfide based photoelectrode materials and their performances are summarized in Table 6.
