8.3 Perspectives on photoelectrochemical water splitting
Metal sulfide semiconductors have long been the topic of research for electronic and optical applications. Recently, metal sulfide materials have been used as a photoelectrode material for solar water splitting as photoelectrodes for hydrogen (H2) and oxygen (O2) generation from water. Compared to electrochemical HER and photocatalytic HER, the use of PEC water splitting has received less research attention and there is potential for improvement. Edge sites, defect engineering and temperature effects can also improve the final device and materials performance for PEC applications. One challenge for the range of low-band gap photocathodes with a conduction band sufficiently high for PEC based H2 evolution (such as Cd and, CuGaS2) is that they are readily self-oxidized and S2− and SO32− are normally used as a sacrificial redox mixture in PEC experiments. However, a variety of methods such as electrodeposition, CVD or sulfurization have been used to enhance the performance of metal sulfide photocathodes; for example MoS2.866 However, their stability must be improved and therefore the creation of composites using graphene, carbon and other conductive materials is of interest to improve the performance and life-time of metal sulfide based PEC devices.
9. Stability of metal sulfides
One of the major difficulties encountered with advanced electrocatalysts or photocatalysts is achieving rational stability without compromising catalytic activity.906 Notably, catalyst with ultra-stability is crucial to construct and achieve efficient water splitting for a prolonged lifetime. As described earlier (see Section 7.4), a number of strategies such as heterostructure formations, doping, carbon/graphene composites formation, temperature effects, edge and defect engineering and porosity have been shown to improve the performance and stability of earth-abundant metal sulfide materials. These research efforts are aimed at improving the water splitting performance of the electro- or photo-catalyst. However, the aqueous medium of water splitting plays a crucial role in H2 or O2 production. Thus, it is challenging to find suitable electrocatalysts and/or photocatalysts that can split water in the same pH range. Using modeling,906 volcano curves have been acquired when plotting HER activity versus hydrogen binding energy (HBE) on various metal sulfides and their composites in an acid41,302,420,907 as well as in a base28,908,909 and proposing that an optimal HBE exists via Sabatier's principle.
While most of the recently advanced HER catalysts work well in acidic electrolytes, only a small number can operate in neutral and basic media. Almost all of the best photocatalysts, particularly those prepared using earth-abundant elements, can work well only in neutral or basic electrolytes, see Tables 4–6. In addition, the fabrication method of catalysts also plays a significant role in the catalytic activity and stability since it defines the chemical composition, structure, surface morphology and size of the catalysts,910–913 which directly influence the stability and performance through the interactions formed between the radicals of reactant (H2O, H2 and CO).
9.1 Electrocatalytic hydrogen generation
Recent studies on the HER activity of electrocatalysts at different pH values suggest that the pH of electrolytes strongly affects the stability of the catalyst. Large numbers of nanostructured mono-metal sulfides, such as WS2,446 CoS2,449 FeS2438 and MoS2138 have been evaluated for HER applications in a variety of media. Recently, Lee et al.914 reported that carboxyl group-functionalized MoS2 (i.e. f(COOH)–MoS2) exhibited a highly stable activity compared to n-MoS2 in a phosphate buffer with the pH adjusted to 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, and 7 by a H2SO4 solution; this was mainly due to functional groups alleviating the defect sites of MoS2. As a result, it remains in its original structure after 1000 cycles of HER, since the COOH sites are surrounded by neighboring layers or defects and provide structural stability to the system. Benefitting from the nanoflake-like structure of FeS2, Giovanni et al.915 attained high HER in neutral water at room temperature with excellent catalytic stability exceeding six days of electrolysis. However, the stability of the mono-metal sulfide catalysts was further enhanced through the formation of bi-metal sulfides, heterostructures, doping or composite formation. For instance, Au decorated MoS2,916 monolayer MoS2 decorated Cu1.75S–Au nanocrystals,917 core–shell MoO3–MoS2 nanowires,918 NiS on Ni foam,557,919 graphene920 or carbon fiber paper921,922 have been fabricated by several methods including hydrothermal methods or physical vapor deposition to enhance HER activity; as a result various low overpotentials and reduced Tafel slopes have been attained with substantial stability. Bonde et al.302 observed the instability of CoSx, MoS2 and WS2 in H2SO4, whereas the HER activity competes with cathodic desorption or dissolution of CoSx. Simultaneously, the cobalt (Co) promoted WS2 and MoS2 revealed higher stability than bare CoSx, MoS2 and WS2 samples. In addition, the HER activity and long-term stability of the carbon supported MoS2 is comparable to that of Au(111) supported MoS2.41 A graphene coated 3D nickel foam was also used as a compact support for loading MoSx catalysts for HER.512 In addition, to serve as a conductive scaffold, the sheets of graphene grown on nickel foams also provide strong protection and efficiently increase their stability in an acidic environment.512 Compared with MoSx, CoSx catalysts are highly active, but less stable. Staszak-Jirkovsky et al.923 prepared a highly stable catalyst by linking the highly active CoSx with the higher stability of MoSx units to prepare robust CoMoSx, which could effectively catalyze HER in both alkaline and acidic environments with long-term stability.
9.2 Photocatalytic hydrogen generation
While water is an ideal source of hydrogen (H2), hydrogen sulfide (H2S) is a potential alternate H2 source. Principally, the cleavage of H2S is easier than H2O, requiring a ΔG of ∼39.4 kJ mol−1 which is less than 284.7 kJ mol−1 necessary for water splitting.924,925 In addition, the H2 production rate from the H2S-containing base solution was similar to the rate from photocatalytic H2 production from water containing sacrificial reagents/donors (sodium sulfide with sodium sulfate; Na2S + Na2SO3) at the same concentration.576,822,926 For example, CdS based semiconducting photocatalysts are well known to be active in H2 production from water. Remarkably, the CdS photocatalyst mixed with other semiconducting materials such as ZnS,798,927 MoS2,928 TiO2929 and SrTiO3930 will exhibit high photocatalytic activity. Unfortunately, they undergo severe photocorrosion under light irradiation unless there exist hole scavengers in the electrolyte solution, such as sacrificial reagents based on sulfides (Sn2−) and sulfites (SO32−). When a sacrificial reagent (H2S or Na2S + Na2SO3) is dissolved into an alkaline solution, sulfide ions are formed in situ and play a key role in H2 production.418 Significantly, during photocatalytic testing, surface reactions take place in the presence of the mixed HS−/SO32− sulfide electron donor systems, whereas sulfite (SO32−) and bisulfide (HS−) are oxidized to SO42− and polysulfide ions, Sn2−, such as S42− and S52−, which impart a yellow color to the aqueous electrolyte suspension at high pH value.571,926 However, the polysulfide ions can also effectively suppresses the photoreduction of protons. In addition, the creation of yellow coloured polysulfide ions can be inhibited by a reaction with sulfite ions to give HS− and thiosulfate (S2O32−). Similarly, excess sulfide present in the solution stabilizes the metal sulfide photocatalyst surface by removing surface defects due to photocorrosion.747 By exploiting the stabilizing properties of these ions, researchers have achieved an efficient H2 production with long-term stability under light condition.254,349,564,931
In this aspect, recently, Simon et al.932 proposed that employing a hydroxyl anion/radical redox couple to strongly relay the hole from the Ni-decorated CdS semiconductor to the scavenger leads to an increase in the H2 generation rate with long-term durability; see Fig. 58(a). Under the laser (wavelength ∼447 nm) illumination, the AQY and the H2 production rate was ∼53% and ∼63 mmol g−1 h−1, respectively. Whereas, the aqueous electrolyte contained 1 M KCl and 0.1 M Na2SO3, the pH was adjusted using NaOH. Although the H2 production decreased to ∼20% after 80 h, it remained stable for up to ∼220 h; see Fig. 58(c and d). Similarly, Brahimi et al.933 prepared n-type Bi2S3/TiO2via a solvent thermal process. The heterostructured Bi2S3/TiO2 exhibited a higher photocatalytic activity and long-term stability of ∼70 min than Bi2S3. However, in an S2O32− electrolyte the n-type Bi2S3/TiO2 photocatalyst at pH ∼ 13.7 exhibited a H2 production rate of ∼2.9 μmol mg−1 h−1 which decreased gradually to zero near pH ∼ 7. Notably, at pH ∼ 7, the back reduction of end products of S4O62− and S22− reduced the H2 evolution rate.
 Fig. 58  (a) Schematic electrochemical potentials of water oxidation and reduction, hydroxyl anion and ethanol oxidation follow Nernstian behaviour (slope −59 mV per pH), but the dependence of CdS conduction and valence bands on pH is only −33 mV per pH. Orange dots related to obtained conduction band minima of CdS. The green circle marks the region where valence band of EVB(CdS) is more positive than E°(OH/−OH); the violet arrows represent the chance of a two-step oxidation pathway reaction at high pH versus a single-step reaction at lower pH. (b) Comparison of H2 generation rates over 6 h displaying strong enhancement at high pH, (c) hydrogen evolution versus time under 477 nm laser light illumination for pH 12.0, 14.0 and 14.7, and (d) long-term hydrogen evolution versus illumination time with hole scavenger of 10% ethanol. The scale bars designate the apparent quantum yield (AQY) at 447 nm, be around 20 h of illumination (width of the bars represent the interval time). Reproduced from ref. 932 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2014. 
9.3 Photoelectrochemical water splitting
The long-term stability of the semiconducting photoelectrode under light illumination is one of the important points in the prospect of photoelectrochemical water splitting reaction. Recently, several metal sulfide photoelectrodes were reported as a visible light active photoelectrode material for HER and/or OER reactions. However, metal sulfides such as CdS,934 ZnS,697 CdIn2S4,62 NiS2,935 CuS,693 and FeS2829 suffer from photocorrosion during the photocatalytic reaction where metal sulfide can be oxidized by photo-generated holes, and thus reduce performance. In order to improve the efficiency as well as the stability, co-catalysts of noble metals, non-precious metals, metal oxides, metal sulfides, carbon materials or polymers are necessary. In addition, to stabilize the metal sulfide photoelectrode, sacrificial electron donors have been employed (see Section 9.2). For example, Seger et al.936 prepared electrodeposited MoS2 on the titanium-protected n+p silicon electrode, this photocathode system showed improved performance compared to a bare silicon electrode in an acidic electrolyte, with a long-term stability of 1 h continuous operation. A MoS2 layered silicon photoelectrode showed no photocurrent loss in performance after 100 h of continuous operation under 0.5 M H2SO4 environments.937 In a phosphate solution (pH 4.25),866 the silicon coated amorphous cobalt molybdenum sulfide (Si/a-CoMoSx) photoelectrode showed an excellent HER activity with a photocurrent density of ∼17.5 mA cm−2vs. RHE. However, a small amount of current degradation about ∼1.5 mA was observed after 3 h operation. Likewise, Guio et al.938 prepared a Cu2O coated amorphous MoSx (MoS2+x–CV–Cu2O) by ALD and during PEC testing the MoS2+x–CV–Cu2O photocathode, exhibited photocurrents of ∼−4.5 mA cm−2 for 10 h; −2.0 mA cm−2 for 10 h and the photocurrent gradually decreased to below −1.0 mA cm−2 after 7 h at pH values of 4.0, 9.0 and 10 respectively. Remarkably, at pH ∼ 1.0, a sudden decrease in photocurrent was observed and this was attributed to the dissolution of catalysts by the acid environment. In addition, under neutral conditions, CdS/Pt-modified CIGS photocathodes exhibited a lower one set potential of 0.38 V vs. RHE with a long-term stability of 3 h continuous operation.939 Yang et al.940 prepared a photoanode of PbS, CdS and ZnS attached TiO2 by a sequential deposition method. It was observed that after the 4 h of light illumination, the PEC photocurrent density of the bare TiO2/PbS electrode gradually decreased from ∼72.3 mA cm−2 to ∼56.4 mA cm−2, with improved photostability attained for the TiO2/PbS/CdS (∼171 mA cm−2) and TiO2/PbS/CdS/ZnS (∼182.4 mA cm−2) under 4 h of light illumination in an electrolyte of Na2S with Na2SO3.
9.4 Hydrogen generation and stability analyses in sea-water
Hydrogen production from direct electrolysis of sea-water with robust electrocatalysts or photocatalysts is one of the great interests in green energy research. However, during sea-water electrolysis, high amounts of chlorine and hypochlorite are evolved at the anode through the oxidation of chlorine (Cl−; also called chlorine evolution reaction ClER) under alkaline and acidic environments,941,942 instead of oxygen evolution by the oxidation of water.34,943–945 However, the Pourbaix diagrams of electrochemical oxygen evolution reaction (OER) from various pH values exhibited that the OER activity is thermodynamically favored.941,943 For instance, during PEC testing the anodic current density of Ag8SnS6 reduced from 1.61 to around 0.9 mA cm−2vs. RHE and from 2.1 to around 0.1 mA cm−2vs. RHE in aqueous 0.25 M K2SO3 + 0.35 M Na2S and 0.5 M NaCl solutions, respectively, during a 1000 s stability investigation.838 Later, the same group attained 800 s PEC water splitting stability of Cu–Sn–S photoanodes in 1 M NaCl aqueous solution under light condition.946 However, the water splitting reaction under sea-water is immature for metal sulfide systems.
Recently, Li et al.947 observed the effect of a NaCl electrolyte on the photocatalytic H2 evolution over Pt/Cd0.5Zn0.5S using the electron donor of glucose under visible light illumination. It was observed that, the H2 evolution rate linearly increased with increased NaCl concentration in 0.050 mol L−1 glucose with 0.10 mol L−1 NaOH solution. Notably, at <1.0 mol L−1 of NaCl concentration, the HER activity increased rapidly, while if the NaCl concentration was >1.0 mol L−1, the HER activity increased slowly. As a result, the H2 production rate at 3.0 mol L−1 NaCl was ∼1.77 times higher than 0 mol L−1 NaCl concentration in 0.050 mol L−1 glucose with 0.10 mol L−1 NaOH solution signifying that NaCl can improve the activity. In addition, this enhanced performance of Pt/Cd0.5Zn0.5S in 3.0 mol L−1 NaCl salt water was ∼77% higher than pure water with a long-term stability of 3 h. It was observed that, after the H2 production reaction, Na+ can be also adsorbed on the surface of Cd0.5Zn0.5S, see Fig. 59(a–c). Recently, Guo et al.79 prepared MoS2@TiO2 heterostructures via combined anodization, PVD, and CVD processes for photocatalytic H2 generation. They investigated the effect of NaCl salt on photocatalytic H2 generation activity of MoS2@TiO2 with natural sea-water (pH ∼ 8.4) under light irradiation. The H2 generation activity from seawater decreased compared to that of clean water, see Fig. 59(d), which was ascribed in terms of lower isoelectric point of MoS2 (<7) and adherence of hydroxyl radicals, metal ions, sulfides oxidation and dissolution could take place in the seawater with a weak alkaline background. In addition, Jiang et al.948 attained 50 h stability for Ni–S film in salt water at −0.959 V vs. SHE and this performance was lower than pure water with a wide range of pH values (0 to 14).
 Fig. 59  Schematic of (a) direct and (b) indirect adsorptions of Na+ ions and the glucose at the Zn and Cd sites on Cd0.5Zn0.5S surface during hydrogen production from salt water, (c) photocatalytic hydrogen generation over Pt/Cd0.5Zn0.5S from different concentration saltwater contain glucose (0.050 mol L−1) and NaOH (0.1 mol L−1). Where the reaction condition is: 100 mL NaCl electrolyte solution, 0.10 g 0.50 wt% Pt/Cd0.5Zn0.5S, 3 h light irradiation. Reproduced from ref. 947 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2011. (d) Compared H2 evolution rate over pristine TiO2 and MoS2@TiO2 photoelectrodes in seawater and pure water under simulated solar light. Reproduced from ref. 79 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2018. 
9.5 Perspectives on stability of metal sulfides
To date, there have been a range of strategies implemented to control corrosion, dissolution and increase the stability of metal sulfide catalysts for photocatalysts; these include (i) the use of sacrificial agents (such as H2S, Na2S with Na2SO3) in an electrolyte to act as a hole scavenging agent and consume holes that contribute to photocorrosion, (ii) covering, decorating or loading a passive metal on the semiconductor surface, which acts as a co-catalyst to improve the rate of oxygen production or hydrogen evolution process, (iii) modification or control of the semiconductor surface with a metal oxide, carbon based material or a polymer to isolate the semiconductor from the electrolyte, (iv) using nanostructuring methods to modify the electrode surface. These modification strategies can lead to an improved catalytic and photocatalytic performance of the metal sulfide materials with long-term durability in neutral, acidic and alkaline media. However, splitting of sea-water is often inhibited by the existence of chemical salts and impurities, which fouls the metal sulfide systems and increases the required bias voltage, reduces efficiency and limits long-term stability.
10. Applications and beyond electrocatalytic, photocatalytic HER and PEC water splitting
In renewable energy technologies, particularly, electrocatalytic and photocatalytic energy conversions have attracted increasing attention due to the need to reduce reliance on fossil fuels. Impressively, metal sulfides with various combinations have also been used in several energy conversion systems such as ORR/OER for fuel cells, photocatalytic dye degradation and CO2 reduction. These are summarized in Scheme 3 and described in this section.
 Scheme 3  Overall technological applications of metal sulfide materials. 
10.1 Electrocatalysts for ORR/OER
Bi-functional electrocatalytic reactions such as ORR and OER are considered as two main energy related reactions in clean and sustainable energy system,949–952 including but not limited to, regenerative fuel cells949,953,954 and rechargeable metal–air batteries.955,956 Thus the advancement of efficient bi-functional oxygen catalysts toward both ORR and OER has become a topic of interest in energy conversion and storage research. However, the performance of ORR and OER catalysts is impaired by their slow kinetics which therefore requires a high overpotential to initiate these electrochemical reactions.957–959 Notably, Pt-based electrocatalysts are the best ORR catalysts,960–962 but they suffer from the formation of Pt oxides on the catalyst surface at high overpotentials which deter the catalytic ability of Pt for OER.503,963 Similarly, the highly active OER catalysts are also less active for ORR and vice versa. Notably, the elevated bi-functional ORR/OER catalyst should have the identical intermediate oxygen binding energies close to the theoretical equilibrium potential.
Ultrathin 2D metal sulfide nanostructures such as MoS2, WS2, and Co9S8 have shown interesting physical and chemical properties in energy conversion and storage research owing to their high surface to volume ratio, small ion and electron diffusion paths, large amount of surface exposed atoms, increased electronic conductivity and large catalyst–electrolyte interface.964–970 Such factors can increase the electrocatalytic activity of metal sulfides for surface-dependent electrochemical reactions. However, pure metal sulfide catalysts with different polymorphs continue to exhibit poor catalytic activity and require improvement. Conversely, the electrochemical performance of metal sulfide catalysts is highly dependent on surface morphology and structure with active crystallographic facets. It is known that the metal sulfides for ORR/OER catalysis possess reduced stability and weak oxygen sorption on the surface of metal sulfides, which in turn reduces the electrocatalytic activity.909,971,972 Therefore, significant effort has been dedicated to new synthetic methods involving mixing or using highly conducting support materials; e.g. carbon, ion or anion. Doping with an ion or anion or carbon and its derivatives, with high surface area, greater electronic conductivity, good mechanical properties, and outstanding chemical stability, are identified as providing improved conductive support for electrocatalysts.377,973,974 As a result, a number of reports have delivered exciting promise for ORR/OER performance enhancement.975–980
Recently, Hong et al.981 prepared hybrid structure of three different metal sulfides with N–S doped carbon matrix (i.e. MS@G/NSC, where M: Fe, Co, Ni). Remarkably, compared with NSC, MS@G/NSC (M: Co, Ni) and commercial Pt/C catalyst, owing to the hybrid feature, high conductivity and highly exposed active area, the FeS@G/NSC catalyst showed an excellent ORR activity in terms of lesser half-wave potential (E1/2) of ∼0.88 V vs. RHE and Tafel value of ∼30.9 mV dec−1, which indicating the influence of the synergistic effect between FeS and G/NSC. Likewise, owing to the high surface area, annealing effect and rich active sites, the composite of N, O and S-doped carbon encapsulated Co9S8 (Co9S8@TDC-900) catalyst showed an excellent electrocatalytic activity towards both the ORR (E1/2 ∼ 0.78 V vs. RHE) and OER (the overpotential of ∼0.330 V vs. RHE at 5.45 mA cm−2) with outstanding long-term stability in 0.1 M KOH solution.982 Recently, Yang et al.983 prepared multi-metal sulfide of CoNiFe-S by an anion-exchange method. Due to the mesoporous nature, the CoNiFe–S exhibited superior ORR and OER catalytic activity, providing E1/2 of ∼0.78 V vs. RHE for ORR; and a low overpotential of ∼199 mV vs. RHE at ∼10 mA cm−2 for OER in 1 M KOH solution. By using two step hydrothermal method, colloidal NiSx coupled with the S-rGO (NiSx/S-rGO) was prepared by Sumboja et al.984 Owing to the rich sulfur nature, high conductivity and the large surface area of the composite, the NiSx/S-rGO catalyst exhibited an excellent bifunctional electrocatalytic activity in terms of a high ORR onset potential (Eonset) of ∼0.91 V vs. RHE and an excellent OER activity by low overpotential of ∼301 mV vs. RHE with a small Tafel slope of ∼41 mV dec−1 and high stability over ∼20 h in 0.1 M KOH solution. Besides, it was also perceived that, hydrothermally prepared S-doped rGO supported CoIn2S4 (CoIn2S4/S-rGO) nanocomposite could serve as a novel and potential electrocatalytic material for efficiently catalyzing the ORR and OER.985 Also, Jiang et al.986 prepared NiCo2S4via a solvothermal method and during electrocatalyst testing it exhibited a good OER with a low onset potential of 1.45 V and low Tafel slope of ∼64 mV dec−1. The enriched OER performance of NiCo2S4 initiated from its short diffusion path and fast transport for active species and a long-term stability for ∼30 h were observed. Similarly, under an O2 saturated alkaline environment (0.1 M KOH), a NiCo2S4/3D graphene foam yielded an outstanding ORR performance with a four-electron oxygen reduction transfer in the potential range of −0.45 to 0.65 V.987 In addition, in order to enhance electrocatalytic activity, NiCo2S4 was mixed with nitrogen and sulfur-functionalized multiple graphene aerogel (N,S-MGA) to construct NiCo2S4/N,S-MGA composites.988 A high cathodic current peak about ∼−0.407 V signified outstanding activity towards ORR due to the presence of heteroatoms of N and S in the architecture, which supported the O2 adsorption over the catalyst.
However, the OER performance of several metal sulfides is restricted by their slow kinetics which leads to the need for a high overpotential (above 1.23 V vs. RHE) to initiate the OER reaction. This is a complex, multi-step chemical reaction comprising the transfer of four electrons, which explains the slow kinetics of the reaction. An additional challenge related with the OER is that at a high overpotential the metal sulfide catalyst is often oxidized; i.e. an intense binding of oxygen to the metal surface. Hence, developing metal sulfides based OER catalysts with superior catalytic activity and long-term stability will be an important step towards producing hydrogen gas at a commercial level.
10.2 Photocatalytic dye degradation
Recently, environmental contamination caused by organic chemicals such as synthetic dyes in water has received increasing attention because of its potential hazard to public health4,989–991 and metal sulfide semiconductors have a potential role in the photocatalytic degradation of organic compounds. The progress of practical applications is limited by the band-gap of the photocatalysts; however some metal sulfides have a narrow band gap which can feasibly oxidize and reduce organic dyes for an efficient photocatalytic dye degradation system.935 A large number of reports have indicated the excellent adsorption capacity of metal sulfides, doped metal sulfides, and heterostructures for organic dye removal.992–998 For instance, owing to the electronic properties and catalytic edge sites of monolayer MoS2 it has drawn much attention as a promising photocatalyst in photodegradation of dyes.999–1001 Bi-dopants of anion N and cation Co with MoS2 for photocatalytic dye degradation was studied via DFT.931 The CuS nanostructure was used to degrade the Methylene Blue (MB) dye at 663 nm. From the degradation process it was perceived that the kinetics is relatively quicker for the cationic dye molecules such as MB, rhodamine B (RhB) and malachinte green (MG), rather neutral methyl red (MR) and anionic methyl orange (MO). Additionally, if the catalyst concentration is increased the photoreaction becomes more rapid.992 A Cu1.94S–rGO composite exhibited higher removal efficiencies of ∼90.8% and ∼79.4% for cationic dye crystal violet and anionic dye methyl orange, respectively, where the minimum additive of 0.55 mM hydrogen peroxide was used.1002 Bhar et al.1003 reported that the degradation of Rose Bengal (RB) using mesoporous FeS2 nanoparticles. The high surface area of ∼49.53 m2 g−1 improved the adsorption of dye over a catalyst which in turn enhanced the photocatalytic degradation reaction. Pourahmad et al.1004 reported the photocatalytic activity of both bare Ag2S and Ag2S attached to a mesoporous silica material MCM-41 against MB. As a result, the Ag2S nanoparticles incorporated in MCM-41 resulted in an average surface area of ∼700 m2 g−1, which efficiently enhanced the degradation efficiency to ∼94%.
Later, Luo et al.1005 prepared CdS and Ni-doped CdS hollow spheres via a simple template-free one-pot method. The hollow sphere shaped photocatalysts exhibited a higher photocatalytic activity for the degradation of RhB under visible light irradiation, and 1.2 mol% of Ni-doped CdS hollow spheres were found to be highly efficient for organic pollutants RhB and phenol removal, which also provided improved stability. The unique hollow spheres feature may have favored the collection of exiting light and the doping of Ni2+ may assist in the generation of charge carriers and delay their recombination rate by acting as temporary trapping active sites of photoinduced electrons. Mondal et al.1006 prepared SnS2 nanoflakes with a highly porous nature. Due to the high porosity and large surface area, the SnS2 nanoflakes efficiently removed the Cr(IV) ions under visible light exposure. Similarly, a high rate of degradation of 250 mg L−1 of aqueous Cr(VI) was achieved within 90 min by Zhong et al.125 using carbon nanofiber/tin(IV) sulfide (CNF@SnS2) core/sheath fibers. The enhanced photocatalytic degradation activity of CNF@SnS2 was attributed to its low band gap of 2.34 eV. In addition, CNF@SnS2 displayed good photocatalytic stability and cyclic stability.
10.3 Photocatalytic CO2 reduction
Photocatalytic reduction of CO2 into value added products such as hydrocarbon fuels via solar light is promising as a new alternative for a sustainable energy resource and reduces global warming.1007–1009 To date, a wealth of metal sulfide materials, as well as their hybrids and heterostructures, have been active photocatalysts for solar CO2 reduction with positive achievements. However, most of the examined metal oxide materials such as TiO2,1010,1011 ZnO,1012 are only active to UV light which limits their utilization for solar energy. Hence, these photocatalysts frequently exhibit low efficiency of CO2 photoreduction. In principle, the photogenerated charge carrier separation and the subsequent transfer of electrons into catalytically active sites are the important step for determining solar CO2 reduction efficiency. Therefore, it is a challenge to suppress the recombination of electron–hole pairs, but the narrow band gap of metal sulfides1013,1014 can potentially facilitate an enhanced CO2 reduction rate through co-catalysts, heterostructures934,1015 and doping.1016,1017 In this regard, Chen et al.1018 prepared Bi2S3–ZnIn2S4 nanocomposites via a hydrothermal method, where the amount of Bi2S3 was set at 1 wt%, 2 wt%, and 3 wt%. Due to the thin nanosheet features of the materials, a narrow band gap and good optical absorption properties was achieved compared to the bare ZnIn2S4 nanosheets, and composites of 2 wt%Bi2S3–ZnIn2S4 nanosheets exhibited a high photocatalytic conversion efficiency of CO2 into methyl formate by methanol solution under 365 nm. Similarly, Jiao et al.1019 prepared defective one-unit-cell layers of ZnIn2S4 where the rich zinc vacancies on one-unit-cell ZnIn2S4 layers exhibited a carbon monoxide formation rate of ∼33.2 μmol g−1 h−1, which was ∼3.6 times greater than that of defectless (poor zinc vacancies) one-unit-cell ZnIn2S4 layers and a long-term stability of ∼24 h. The enhanced performance was attributed to the defects in ZnIn2S4 layers, which provide more active sites for enhanced photocatalytic activity. Wang et al.68 achieved a high photocatalytic CO generation rate of ∼3075 μmol h−1 g−1 for sandwich-like ZnIn2S4–In2O3 hierarchical tubular heterostructures. This enhanced performance was attributed to the 1D open architectures of ZnIn2S4 with double heterojunction shells and 2D ultrathin nanosheets features, which provide a high surface area for CO2 adsorption, highly exposed active sites as well as facilitating the separation and transfer of photogenerated charge carriers. In addition, due to the heterostructure formation using boron carbon nitride (BCN) with CdS nanoparticles, Zhou et al.67 attained a high CO production rate of ∼250 μmol h−1 g−1, which was approximately ten times higher than bare BCN material using the reducing agent of triethanolamine (TEOA).
11. Conclusions and perspectives
11.1 Overall conclusions
To date, several hundreds of mono- or bi-metal sulfides have been prepared via both top- and bottom-up approaches. Notably, CVD, pulsed laser, sputtering, and plasma approaches display advantages when fabricating metal sulfides in monolayer form. The approaches of hydrothermal, solvothermal, precipitation, sulfidation, and template methods are leading processing methods in attaining a range of nano- and micro-structured metal sulfide materials. In addition, lithography, spray pyrolysis, electrodeposition and electrospinning are advantageous for fabricating metal sulfide electrodes. However, the application of combustion synthesis for the formation of metal sulfides is yet to be fully explored. By these controlled fabrication techniques, rational tuning of the chemical, physical, optical and nano- and micro-structural properties of metal sulfide nanocrystals have been achieved. As a result of controlled preparation, the prepared metal sulfides and their composites or hybrids have been examined for electrocatalytic and photocatalytic water splitting applications. Importantly, MoS2, WS2, VS2, and their composites or hybrids have focused on HER activity. Due to their monolayer nature, MoS2 and WS2 with highly exposed active sites have attracted attention. However, there are a number of other potential materials such as Fe2S, SnS, CoS, Re2S, and Ta2S that remain to be fully explored. CdMnS, ZnMnS, MoWS, NiWS and their composites remain immature in terms of examination of properties and are worthy of future study.
Compared with metal oxides, the band gaps and energy levels of metal sulfides are more suitable for solar energy harvesting applications. Owing to their structural and electronic similarities to MoS2, WS2 has received intensive investigation as photoelectrochemical catalysts. However, most of the metal sulfides use a co-catalyst for photocatalytic hydrogen generation and most of the mono-metal sulfides and bi-metal sulfides possess inferior photocatalytic H2 generation performance, since several metal sulfide materials suffer from photodegradation, which limits their long-term operational durability. In addition, to enhance the performance without structural or sulfur deficiencies, it is important to use sacrificial agents to improve performance. However, enhanced photocatalytic performance can be achieved by creating heterojunctions, doping with anions or cations or with carbon materials to expose more active sites, preventing agglomeration and amalgamation, and electronic structure modification of nanoparticles.
Remarkably, PEC water splitting of metal sulfide materials has received less attention. However, the PEC performance of these materials is in need of improvement and the use of edge sites, defect engineering, carbon heterostructures and processing temperature optimization need to fully exploited to improve final device PEC performance. Furthermore, as shown in Scheme 3, metal sulfides are prominently considered to enhance the performance of ORR/OER, photocatalytic dye degradation and photocatalytic CO2 reduction.
11.2 Fundamental scientific challenges and future perspective
The strategies to synthesize a variety of metal sulfide structures have been considered by developing an understanding the formation mechanism and production of the degree of charge carriers for effective electrocatalysis and photocatalysis application. Whilst achievements have been made over the past decades in the design and synthesis of metal sulfide related catalysts and photocatalysts for an enriched water splitting reaction, only limited amounts of H2 have been generated to date by electrocatalytic methods (i.e. few hundreds of micro-liters (μL) to few tens of milli-liters (mL) per gram of catalysts) using metal sulfide systems. There is a need to increase the H2 production rate to a few hundreds of milli-liters (mL), or greater. Similarly, for PEC water splitting a solar to hydrogen efficiency of only ∼12% has been achieved. Such production rates and efficiencies are inadequate for commercial application of metal sulfide systems. The following aspects need to be considered to develop disruptive metal sulfides for the energy sector and chemical industry (see Scheme 4).
 Scheme 4  Future perspectives on metal sulfide materials to enhance energy conversion and storage performance. 
(i) Most of the metal sulfides for electrocatalytic and photocatalytic applications are still at an early stage. The most studied metal sulfides for HER are MoS2 and WS2 with a limited number of photocatalytic applications for materials such as ZnIn2S4, and CdIn2S4. Thus, for future development, other metal sulfides including Sb, Sn, Mn, Re, V, Ni, Cu, Nb, and Ta metal based mono- and bi-metal or multi-metal sulfides with precisely defined shapes, sizes and compositions deserve future study.
(ii) There is a need to realize new metal sulfides using new strategies, such as using graphene, a carbon based matrix, selective doping, processing temperature control, nanostructural tuning, defect engineering and enhanced porosity and conductivity. Such future research paths can widen the metal sulfide family for both electrocatalytic and photocatalytic applications.
(iii) A challenge for photocatalysis is the inadequate potential energy of charges to drive the chemical reaction, which can be resolved by the creation of Z-scheme metal sulfides. The Z-scheme allows a controlled combination of electrons and holes from different materials by electron mediators such as rGO, Fe3+/Fe2+, Au, Ag, and IO3−/I−.
(iv) An alternative approach is to increase the activity of metal sulfide electrocatalysts and photocatalysts by decoration with single atom catalysis such as Pt, Au and Ru. Single atoms could be loaded on the monolayer of the metal sulfides or they can be loaded on the inner or outer areas of nanoboxes, hollow shells and nanobubbles of metal sulfides, which can offer outstanding performance such as activity, trapping of charge, surface reaction and comprehensive atomic utilization. Usually, the electro- or photo-catalyst used in single-atom catalysis is a supported metal catalyst that comprises only isolated single metal atoms as the major active centers. The single atomic decoration mechanism for the formation of metal sulfides and the time-resolved progression of charge transport during catalytic and photocatalytic reactions should be further examined with the aid of kinetics experiment and DFT.
(v) The decoration or hybridization of metal sulfides with metal organic frameworks (MOF's) is a potential strategy to increase the performance of any metal sulfide systems. This kind of structure can be made by introducing one or more different metal sulfide based co-templates into a MOF system. Such elegantly designed hybrid and/or decorated structures with novel morphologies and precise composition are fairly promising for emergent energy conversion processes.
(vi) Among the 2D and 2D related materials, the manufacture of metal sulfides of mono-layered MoS2 and WS2 have reached a commercial scale, however, large scale production of other materials such as NiS, SnS, TaS, ZnIn2S4, and CdIn2S4 remains a challenge. Up scaling is likely to further develop the industrial application of metal sulfides as catalysts or co-catalysts for renewable energy and fossil fuels.
(vii) The chemical stability of metal sulfides under a wide range of pH is a technical challenge that needs to be addressed. While many metal sulfides perform well for reasonably short periods of time, they can be vulnerable to oxidation and the performance of the devices can deteriorate over time as a result of corrosion. The decoration of Pt, Au, Pd, Ru and/or carbon matrices could be an alternative approach to improve corrosion and oxidation resistance. It has been established that metal sulfides are unstable during electrocatalytic reactions and transformed to consistent metal oxides, which were responsible for their catalytic activity. Hence, after catalytic reactions, the experimental analysis of metal sulfide catalysts to assess structural or compositional variations would be beneficial.
Based on the strategies mentioned above, it is also conceivable to develop metal sulfides for high performance overall water splitting systems (bifunctional activity i.e. HER with OER) with improved catalytic activity and long-term durability for industrial application.

Table Content:

Sundaram Chandrasekaran
	Dr Sundaram Chandrasekaran received his BSc (2004) and MSc degrees (2007) from Madurai Kamaraj University, Tamilnadu, India. He obtained his PhD degree in 2013 from Gandhigram Rural University, India. In 2013, he joined Korea University, South Korea as a Research Professor and later he worked as a Post-doctoral researcher at University of Ulsan, Ulsan, South Korea from 2014 to 2018. Since 2018 until now, he is working as a High Talent Post-Doctoral Research fellow at Shenzhen University, Shenzhen, China. His research interests focus on the synthesis and applications of nanostructured materials, graphene, and other high performance 2D materials, and on the development of new energy materials for electrocatalytic applications such as HER, ORR/OER, photocatalytic, photoelectrochemical water splitting and energy storage.

Lei Yao
	Lei Yao is an assistant professor in Shenzhen University. She obtained a PhD degree in Materials Science and Engineering from Tsinghua University in 2016. After a postdoctoral training in Prof. Peixin Zhang's group, she was appointed as an assistant professor in 2018. Her research focuses on solid electrolyte and porous materials for energy conversion and storage.

Libo Deng
	Libo Deng is an assistant professor in Shenzhen University. He obtained a Bachelor's degree from South China University of Technology, a Master's degree from Zhejiang University, and a PhD degree in Materials Science from the University of Manchester where he continued postdoctoral research in carbonaceous materials with Prof. Robert Young. He then established his own research group in Shenzhen University in 2015. His main research interests are electrochemical energy conversion and storage including photocatalysis, electrocatalysis and batteries, particularly focusing on pyrolytic carbons and metal oxides.

Chris Bowen
	Prof. Christopher Rhys Bowen has a BSc degree in Materials Science from the University of Bath (1986–1990) and a DPhil in Ceramics from the University of Oxford (1990–1993). Post-doctoral work has been undertaken at Technische Universität Harburg-Hamburg and University of Leeds (1994–1996). He was Senior Scientist at the Defence Evaluation and Research Agency from 1996–1998. He joined the University of Bath as a Lecturer in 1998 and is now Professor of Materials. Research areas include energy harvesting, piezoelectric materials, dielectrics and functional ceramics.

Yan Zhang
	Dr Yan Zhang obtained her BSc (2004–2008) and PhD (2008–2013) degrees in Materials Science from Central South University. She worked in Hunan University, China from 2014 to 2016. In 2016, she was granted the Marie-Skłodowska-Curie Fellowship with the host of Prof. Chris Bowen in the University of Bath, UK. Since 2018 until now, she is a research associate working with Prof. Chris Bowen. Her current research interests are porous materials, ferroelectrics, pyroelectrics, bio-piezoelectric composite, sensors and energy harvesting applications.

Peixin Zhang
	Peixin Zhang is a professor in Chemistry at Shenzhen University, China. His research areas focus on energy storage and conversion including batteries, electrocatalytic and photocatalytic hydrogen evolution and solar cells, through nano-structured transition metal oxides and compounds. He has published over 300 papers and owns 20 patents.

 	Fig. 23  Schematic of the construction of NiCo2S4 HPHTMSs by well-interwoven NTs. In the presence of NH4F, a slow anisotropic growth leads to compound I of NiCo2(OH)x(CO3)y(HCO3)z, while the product is already compound II of NiCo2(OH)3(CO3)1.5 after only 0.5 h and its morphology is spherical with a rough NW-covering without NH4F. The nanostructure and composition change with time during the first ion-exchange process from compound I to compound II is presented in detail. The second anion-exchange results in the formation of compound III of NiCo2S4 HPHTMSs. The insets illustrate local magnifications of several interwoven NWs and sulfides NTs. A wire-interwoven network structure in a steel mesh with strong mechanical stability is also presented. Reproduced from ref. 374 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018.
